BREAKING: Elon Musk's First Interview Since Jury Rejected Claim Against Altman's OpenAI

Forbes(2.1M) ·26:01 ·154.4K views · 2026-05-19
Elon Musk (CEO, SpaceX and Neuralink) Randall Lane (Editor, Forbes) Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
> "When technologies are hitting like Jesus-level miracles—enabling people who have never spoken to speak, or the blind to see—that’s when you know innovation is truly profound.
TL;DR: Elon Musk predicts that AI will surpass human intelligence in every metric within two years and advocates for moving massive AI data centers into space to harness solar energy, while warning that OpenAI’s transition to a for-profit model sets a "dangerous precedent" for...

TL;DR: Elon Musk predicts that AI will surpass human intelligence in every metric within two years and advocates for moving massive AI data centers into space to harness solar energy, while warning that OpenAI’s transition to a for-profit model sets a "dangerous precedent" for looting charitable organizations.

The OpenAI Lawsuit and Ethical Precedents

  • OpenAI Verdict: Musk views the recent legal developments as proof that the OpenAI board breached its fiduciary duty.
      • Charitable Looting: He argues that allowing a non-profit to convert into an $80 billion for-profit entity undermines the foundation of charitable giving in America.
      • Clause Removal: Musk highlighted the recent removal of a clause that would have reverted all stock to the charity upon the advent of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), calling the transition "stolen one piece at a time."
  • Legal Appeal: Musk confirmed he is appealing the case to prevent this from becoming a legal precedent where individuals can "essentially loot charities" after they become successful.

AI Predictions and the "Intelligence Explosion"

  • Timeline for AGI: Musk predicts that AI will be smarter than humans in "every way," including innovation, within the next one to two years.
  • Five-Year Outlook: By roughly 2031, Musk believes digital intelligence will exceed the sum total of all human intelligence.
      • Humanoid Robotics: He expects between 100 million and 1 billion humanoid robots to be in operation within five years.
      • Economic Doubling: Due to the massive increase in productivity from AI and robotics, Musk predicts the global economy could double in size within the next 5–7 years.

Space-Based Infrastructure and Energy Harnessing

  • AI Data Centers in Space: Musk argues that the future of heavy AI compute belongs in orbit rather than on Earth.
      • Energy Superiority: He notes that all human energy consumption is less than one-trillionth of the sun’s output. Moving to space allows AI to scale without Earth's energy constraints.
      • Launch Capacity: Through Starship, SpaceX aims to launch 10,000 satellites annually. Musk envisions "terawatt-level" AI satellite clusters, providing twice the electricity consumption of the entire United States.
  • The "Acid Test" for Mars: Musk's goal for SpaceX remains a self-sustaining city on Mars.
      • Logistics: Success is defined by moving approximately one million tons of cargo to Mars to create an industrial base that can survive even if resupply ships from Earth stop coming.
      • Reusability: The fundamental breakthrough required this year is the first fully reusable orbital rocket.

"Jesus-Level" Innovation and Future Disruptions

  • Neuralink’s "Miracles": Musk describes brain-machine interfaces as providing "cybernetic superpowers," including restoring sight to the blind and enabling the paralyzed to walk again.
  • Synthetic Medicine: He identifies a massive opportunity in "digital medicine" via synthetic RNA, moving away from "analog" drug discovery to programmable cures for almost any ailment.
  • 3D Transportation: Musk reiterated that the only way to solve urban traffic is to go 3D with tunnels (The Boring Company), as 2D road networks cannot support 3D high-rise buildings.
  • Historical Influences: Musk cited Henry Ford as a primary inspiration for inventing the mass manufacturing of complex objects, alongside Nikola Tesla, Thomas Edison, and Ben Franklin.

Is Defense the Next Trillion-Dollar Category? | a16z American Dynamism Summit

a16z(274.0K) ·12:02 ·1.0K views · 2026-05-19
Erin Price-Wright (Partner, a16z) Michael Duffey (Defense Industry Executive) Dino Mavrookas (Defense Industry Executive)
To counter rising geopolitical competition, the U.S.
TL;DR To counter rising geopolitical competition, the U.S. defense industrial base must shift from bespoke, low-volume production to software-driven autonomy, simplified manufacturing processes, and a "commercial-first" approach that aligns private capital with national...

TL;DR

To counter rising geopolitical competition, the U.S. defense industrial base must shift from bespoke, low-volume production to software-driven autonomy, simplified manufacturing processes, and a "commercial-first" approach that aligns private capital with national security priorities.

Bending the Cost Curve via Software and Autonomy

  • First-Principles Design: Dino Mavrookas (Co-founder & CEO, Saronic) highlights that the U.S. cannot compete with adversaries like China on raw material or per-hour labor costs. To succeed, the U.S. must fundamentally redesign ships to use less steel and fewer labor hours.
  • The Power of Software Autonomy: Designing platforms around software and autonomy allows for drastically simpler designs that are faster and cheaper to manufacture.
      • Labor Hour Comparison: Saronic's autonomous vessel, the *Marauder*, requires approximately 50,000 labor hours to construct. By comparison, a traditional Navy destroyer requires 7 to 9 million labor hours.
  • Preserving Human Life: Embracing robotics allows the military to scale up its presence and achieve necessary strategic mass while removing service members from high-risk combat environments.

Democratizing Manufacturing: The "IKEA" Approach

  • Bypassing the Labor Bottleneck: The maritime industrial base cannot wait 15 years to train elite, highly specialized welders. Instead, companies must design ships that do not require legacy shipbuilding expertise to build.
  • Simplified Work Instructions: Saronic utilizes a manufacturing philosophy described as *"less like an encyclopedia, more like IKEA."*
      • Cross-Industry Talent: Ship manufacturing processes must be simplified so that workers transitioning from automotive (Ford, GM) or aerospace (Boeing, SpaceX) can become highly productive shipbuilders almost immediately.
  • Workforce Culture: Rebuilding the domestic industrial base requires creating a modern shipyard culture that offers job security, advanced technology, and a clear mission to attract a new generation of builders.

Reforming Pentagon Acquisitions and Funding

  • Production as the Central Constraint: Honorable Michael Duffey (Pentagon/Defense Security Cooperation Agency) notes that defense production has historically been artificially limited by the size of the U.S. defense budget, causing massive bottlenecks in fulfilling international and domestic orders.
  • Co-Investing with Private Capital: The Pentagon is shifting its acquisition strategy to incentivize defense contractors to invest their own private capital to expand manufacturing capacity, moving away from traditional, slow-moving government handouts.
  • Overcoming Bureaucracy: The Department of Defense is implementing an acquisition transformation strategy aimed at removing bureaucratic hurdles, "letting a thousand flowers bloom," and establishing direct lines of communication between industry startups and Pentagon leadership.

The Strategic Power of the Commercial Market

  • Port Alpha Project: Saronic is preparing to launch Port Alpha, a generational shipyard project designed to build large-scale, autonomous commercial vessels (such as cargo ships, bulk carriers, and oil tankers) to bolster American energy and logistics dominance.
  • Wartime Capacity during Peacetime: To avoid financial fragility and complete reliance on government contract cycles, defense tech startups must find viable commercial markets. This ensures active, hot production lines during peacetime that can pivot to wartime needs if necessary.
  • Eliminating Single-Point Failures: Duffey warns that the traditional defense industrial base is highly fragile due to unprofitable, sole-source suppliers building bespoke equipment. Emphasizing commercial-first designs inherently improves supply chain resilience for the DoD.

Notable Quotes

"Warfare will require human involvement at some point, but I've seen it and you should never send a human if you have the ability to send a robot. You just shouldn't do it." — Dino Mavrookas
"I'll steal one of the lines from our head of manufacturing. He says, 'less like an encyclopedia, more like IKEA.' Everybody can build furniture. If someone can't come out of building a car or an airplane and build ships quickly, that's our fault." — Dino Mavrookas
"It's really frustrating how much fragility we encounter within the traditional defense industrial base because we have a sole supplier that's not really that profitable that was a feeder to a design that was bespoke... If we can prioritize commercial first and producibility as a primary input to the development of a design, that creates all kinds of resilience." — Michael Duffey
"There's a real generational opportunity to build what this country needs for the next hundred years, and we need more founders. We need more builders, and we need more folks in government pushing for change." — Dino Mavrookas

It's absolutely critical to have that commercial market because at the end of the day, what our goal is is to provide the Department of War wartime production capacity during peacetime. And if we can't enter the commercial market in a viable way, in an economic manner, then we're just looking to the government for the next contract vehicle and the next paycheck to support our company's longevity. And maybe it's five years from now, maybe it's ten years from now, maybe it's twenty, maybe it's thirty, but eventually we may end up right back where we are. If we can prioritize commercial first and producibility as a primary input to the development of a design, that creates all kinds of resilience and benefits for the Department of Defense as well. So that is, I think, key to our success. Yeah, on our team we often say there's no defense industrial base without an industrial base, so the two really go hand in hand. Honorable Duffey, this room is filled with PEs and PMs from across the Pentagon, as well as many founders that are building for the department. What's one key message you'd like to send this group as we look to build together and build faster in 2026? Well, let me start with an expression of gratitude for the leaders in this room who are driving innovation, who are fighting through the bureaucracy to deliver the capability that the warfighter needs on schedule. And I think the other message I would say is it's really up to everybody in this room, right? We recognize, I think, as a part of this acquisition transformation strategy, the only way for it to succeed is really to let a thousand flowers bloom and ensure that we are removing the obstacles to companies and program leadership to deliver the best capability the warfighter needs on schedule. And that's going to take time and it's going to take cooperation. You know, one of the things that we've been trying to do as we try to change culture within the Pentagon is maximum communication throughout the hierarchy of the acquisition system directly with industry, and ensuring that there's clear understanding of what's getting in the way. We want to continue to expand on that, and I invite everybody in this room to engage with my team or others and just say, "Hey, this is not working, how can we remove this barrier?" Because we're committed to making sure we can go as fast as possible. Thank you. And Dino, advice to other builders who might be a click or two... a click or two behind you? Yeah, find what you believe in and then go for it, right? We're in a... there's a real generational opportunity to build what this country needs for the next hundred years, and we need more founders. We need more builders. And we need more folks in government like the secretary pushing for change. This isn't a one-company, one-person is going to solve this problem. We need a partnership across the country, and folks that are thinking about getting into this space, building a company, I would say, you know, build the conviction in what you believe in and then go all in on it because the country needs you and the partners are here to support you. Amazing. Thank you both so much. Honorable Duffey, Dino, it's been a real pleasure.

LIVE: Vice President JD Vance Holds White House Press Briefing

Forbes Breaking News(5.6M) ·107:40 ·3.1K views · 2026-05-19
JD Vance (Vice President of the United States)
Vice President JD Vance held a White House press briefing to detail the Trump administration's "locked and loaded" yet diplomatic negotiations with Iran, defend the controversial $1.8 billion Department of Justice "lawfare" compensation fund, and rep...
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance held a White House press briefing to detail the Trump administration's "locked and loaded" yet diplomatic negotiations with Iran, defend the controversial $1.8 billion Department of Justice "lawfare" compensation fund, and report on the...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance held a White House press briefing to detail the Trump administration's "locked and loaded" yet diplomatic negotiations with Iran, defend the controversial $1.8 billion Department of Justice "lawfare" compensation fund, and report on the multi-billion-dollar savings achieved by the Anti-Fraud Task Force.

Iran Diplomacy and Defense Strategy

  • The Two Pathways: Vance outlined two paths for the administration's Iran policy: negotiating a peaceful resolution (Option A) or restarting kinetic military operations (Option B). Vance stated the U.S. remains "locked and loaded" if negotiations fail.
  • Nuclear Red Line: The administration's absolute non-negotiable term is that Iran must never obtain a nuclear weapon, warning that an Iranian nuclear capability would spark a dangerous nuclear arms race across the Gulf and the globe.
  • Islamabad Negotiations: Vance detailed his intense travel schedule to demonstrate diplomatic good faith, spending 22 hours flying to Islamabad, Pakistan, 24 hours returning, and 21 hours on the ground negotiating directly with Iranian officials.
  • The Russia Rumor: Vance dismissed speculative reports that Russia might take possession of Iran's enriched uranium, stating it is not a part of the current U.S. negotiating plan.
  • Poland Troop Rotation: Addressing the delayed deployment of 4,000 U.S. troops to Poland, Vance clarified it is a standard rotational delay rather than a permanent reduction. He emphasized that European allies must take more ownership of their continental defense.

The $1.8 Billion DOJ "Lawfare" Fund

  • Compensating Victims of Lawfare: Vance defended the $1.8 billion Department of Justice fund, framing it as a tool to compensate Americans who faced politically motivated or disproportionate prosecutions under the Biden administration.
  • Bipartisan Eligibility: Vance asserted that the fund is open to both Republicans and Democrats, noting that Hunter Biden is welcome to apply and will go through the same case-by-case vetting process.
  • January 6th Case Evaluations: Payouts will be evaluated individually. While Vance clarified that the administration does not intend to compensate individuals who attacked police officers, he defended evaluating J6 defendants who received unconstitutional pretrial detentions or disproportionate sentences.
  • Economic Justification: Responding to critics who questioned the $1.8 billion expense during a time of high inflation, Vance countered that the administration is simultaneously supporting families through the Working Families Tax Cut and a $40 billion rural healthcare fund.

Anti-Fraud and Border Security Initiatives

  • Anti-Fraud Task Force Savings: Vance announced that the task force has halted "billions upon billions of dollars" in systemic fraud across hospice care, Medicaid, Medicare, and immigration systems, alongside hundreds of millions in fraudulent Small Business Administration (SBA) loans.
  • Closing Asylum Loopholes: Vance criticized the prior administration's asylum policies, arguing they allowed economic migrants to obtain work permits and bypass legal processes by falsely claiming persecution.
  • Fentanyl Reductions: Vance credited strict southern border enforcement for progressively driving down annual fentanyl overdose deaths from 2024 through 2026. He noted that President Trump has continuously raised the issue of illicit Chinese chemical exports with President Xi Jinping.
  • Ilhan Omar Investigation: Vance confirmed that the DOJ is actively investigating Representative Ilhan Omar for potential immigration and marriage fraud, promising equal justice under the law without prejudging the outcome.

Political Endorsements, AI Policy, and Administrative Integrity

  • Texas Senate Endorsement: Vance addressed Trump's endorsement of Ken Paxton over incumbent Senator John Cornyn, stating Paxton stood by the president when "it really counted" and warning that representatives who prioritize corporate lobbyists over voters will find themselves out of step politically.
  • AI Policy and Papal Guidance: Vance expressed interest in Pope Leo XIV's upcoming encyclical on artificial intelligence. On U.S. policy, Vance noted the administration aims to win the global AI race while working collaboratively with tech firms to mitigate safety risks associated with new models like "Mythos."
  • Presidential Stock Trading: Vance denied that President Trump directly trades stocks from the Oval Office, clarifying that Trump's assets are managed by independent wealth advisors. Vance reaffirmed his personal support for passing a complete ban on individual stock trading for members of Congress.

Notable Quotes

"We are not going to have a deal that allows the Iranians to have a nuclear weapon, so as the President just told me, we're locked and loaded. We don't want to go down that pathway, but the President is willing and able to go down that pathway if we have to." — Vice President JD Vance
"What I would propose is something very simple: That if you are willing to turn the page on Joe Biden's lawfare, why don't we prosecute people—very simple principle—why don't we prosecute people who violate the law? Let's not prosecute people because they said the wrong thing, or because they had the wrong political candidate..." — Vice President JD Vance
"The United States cannot be the policeman of the world. We want to be good allies, we want to make sure that our troop presence promotes regional stability in Europe... but Europe has to stand on its own two feet." — Vice President JD Vance
"What we believe in this White House is what we need more and more of is high wages for American workers and investing in our own people. What you see all over the West, and it's kind of crazy, is this idea that the way to generate prosperity is to bring in millions and millions of unvetted people and drop them into your neighborhoods, and we simply reject that idea." — Vice President JD Vance

Does the government need to create a new mandatory review process for new AI models, given the concerns about Mythos? And secondly, in a few days, Pope Leo is going to release his encyclical on artificial intelligence. What influence, if any, do you see the Pope's guidance on AI having on broader society? Well, I mean, I—I think when the Pope issues an encyclical on artificial intelligence, it's going to have some influence. I, of course, don't know how much influence, and I don't know exactly what it's going to say, but I think when the—the leader of the world's largest Christian denomination speaks on an issue like that, it's certainly going to have some influence, and I'm sure it'll contain a lot of insights, some of which I'll probably agree with, some of which I—I may not. But I think that it's going to be a very, very important document. And I think that, you know, one of the things I always find fascinating about Pope Leo is that he chose the name Leo XIV, which, of course, is recollecting Leo XIII. Leo XIII was the Pope during a period of incredible industrial transformation in the entire world. Of course, that industrial transformation, according to a lot of people, led to the rise of fascism and communism in Europe. I think it was interesting that Leo XIV chose that name to maybe apply Christian social teaching in a new era with a new technological innovation, in the same way that the Industrial Revolution was the technological innovation of its time. So, I think it'll be fascinating, I'm looking forward to reading it, and my guess is it's going to have a lot of influence. On—on the question of what our policy is going to be. Look, what we're trying to do in the—in the Trump administration is very simple. The President wants us to be pro-innovation; he wants us to win the AI race against all other countries in the world. He recognizes that AI is going to be an important tool, not just for our economy, but for our military, and so he wants to ensure that we are winning that particular race. We also want to make sure that we're protecting people—we're protecting people's data, and we're protecting people's privacy. I think with this Mythos release, one of the things that we're very focused on, of course, is whether, not necessarily the developers of Mythos, but whether some other bad actor could use Mythos to target various cyber-cybersecurity vulnerabilities. So, it's something that right now we're working on in a collaborative way with the technology companies, and we're just trying to make sure that the American people are as safe as possible. I'm not going to get ahead of the executive order or any other actions that—that are going to come out, but we're trying to balance those two things. We want to be pro-innovation. We recognize—I mean, artificial intelligence could be great, it could help us find cures to diseases that currently, you know, people are dying from or suffering from. It also does have some downsides, and we're trying to balance that safety against innovation, and we think that we've got the right balance here in the Trump administration. But, it's something we're going to have to keep on working on because that's just the nature of these technologies, is—is they certainly change. Let's—let's go. Stripe Sessions 2026 | Indexing the economy

Stripe(105.0K) ·31:07 ·701 views · 2026-05-19
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI) Nat Friedman (Investor and Former CEO, GitHub) Daniel Gross (Investor and Entrepreneur)
Stripe’s macroeconomic data reveals that despite market volatility and fears of job displacement, the global economy is structurally replatforming around AI through a massive surge in hyper-lean, high-revenue solopreneurship and the rapid emergence of autonomous, machine-to-machine commerce.
TL;DR Stripe’s macroeconomic data reveals that despite market volatility and fears of job displacement, the global economy is structurally replatforming around AI through a massive surge in hyper-lean, high-revenue solopreneurship and the rapid emergence of autonomous,...

TL;DR

Stripe’s macroeconomic data reveals that despite market volatility and fears of job displacement, the global economy is structurally replatforming around AI through a massive surge in hyper-lean, high-revenue solopreneurship and the rapid emergence of autonomous, machine-to-machine commerce.

Macroeconomic Trends and Debunking Common Myths

  • SaaS Resiliency: Despite software losing $1 trillion in market value in January due to fears that AI makes software substitutable, SaaS payment volumes on Stripe remain significantly higher than before the sell-off. SaaS continues to grow steadily.
  • Return of the Profitability Premium: Outsized valuations have shifted firmly toward highly profitable companies. Unlike the dot-com bubble or recent speculative waves, public markets are currently rewarding realized profitability rather than growth at any cost.
  • The Delayed Reality of Tariffs: While trade volume remained near 2024 levels after recent tariffs, the expected inflation of durable goods is finally showing. Historically, durable goods see annual deflation, but 2025 and 2026 data show prices rising significantly as businesses pass accumulated costs onto consumers.
  • Debunking the "K-Shaped" Economy: Despite viral news about premium airline seating outperforming economy, Stripe’s aggregate data shows that the spending gap between high-income and low-income consumers has actually been shrinking, refuting the theory of an over-reliance on wealthy consumers.
  • AI and the Labor Market: While US unemployment has ticked up about 1% over three years, this is primarily driven by post-pandemic hiring unwinding, tighter immigration, and high interest rates slowing down recruitment, rather than direct AI job displacement.

High-Dynamism Startups and the Solopreneur Boom

*Emily Sands, Stripe's Head of Data and AI, joined the presentation to share these insights:*

  • The Rise of the Solopreneur: A resurgence in US business formations is being driven almost entirely by "non-employer firms" (solopreneurs). This is no longer a hobbyist segment; close to 5 million Americans now earn their living as solopreneurs generating over $100k in annual revenue.
  • Rapid Startup Scaling: Stripe Atlas recently hit 100,000 incorporations. The Atlas class of 2025 doubled the revenue of the 2024 class at the same stage, while the newly incorporated class of 2026 is tracking to generate *five times* the revenue of the 2025 class.
  • The "Born Global" Playbook: Startups are bypassing the traditional model of establishing domestic traction before expanding internationally.
      • The median of the top 100 AI startups on Stripe earns most of its revenue internationally and sells into 55 countries in its first year of existence.
      • Emergent Labs, founded in the US in 2024, already drives nearly 70% of its revenue from abroad, spanning a highly fragmented tail of global markets.
  • The Coasean Impact of AI: Drawing on Ronald Coase’s transaction cost theory, AI is drastically reducing the coordination costs of interacting with external markets. Rather than building massive internal departments, future firms will be structurally smaller, automate aggressively, and coordinate via open market mechanisms.

The Shift Toward Agentic Commerce

  • The Evolution of Autonomous Buying: Commerce is shifting through distinct developmental phases:
      • Level 1 (Form Filling): Software completing checkout details on behalf of consumers (e.g., Stripe's in-app checkout experience with Meta).
      • Level 2 (Constraint-Based Search): Shopping assistants reasoning through complex parameters instead of basic keywords (e.g., ChatGPT or Wayfair finding highly specific items).
      • Levels 3–5 (Autonomous Purchasing): AI agents independently making and executing buying decisions.
  • Developer Demand for Agentic Payments: The OpenClaw framework saw 125,000 downloads of payment-related skills on ClawHub in just 12 weeks, signaling massive developer momentum behind agentic commerce.
  • Machine-to-Machine Microtransactions: To support autonomous software-to-software purchasing, businesses require new financial infrastructure. Stablecoins are emerging as the preferred rail because they offer near-zero transaction costs, making micro-payments (e.g., fractional-cent API calls) viable.
  • Live Demonstration: John Collison demonstrated an AI agent autonomously researching commodity data, purchasing a $0.04 dataset from Alpha Vantage using a stablecoin wallet, compiling a PDF report, and launching a live monetized web page (`johnsreport.vercel.app`) with an `llms.txt` file config so other AI agents can purchase the report for $5.

Key Complements of Abundant Intelligence

When any resource becomes abundant and cheap, its complementary assets experience an explosive surge in value. As intelligence becomes cheap, several strategic complements are gaining outsized value:

  • Hardware and Physical Infrastructure: This extends beyond GPUs (such as Nvidia's compute and networking growth) to physical energy. Gas turbines (such as Siemens Energy) and nuclear power are seeing massive order surges to support datacenters.
  • Proprietary Data: Companies with unique datasets are restricting public web scrapers and directly monetizing their assets. For example, Reddit's non-advertising revenue (driven by data licensing agreements) has reached $35 million per quarter.
  • Network Effects: Strong marketplaces are realizing higher returns; the average take-rate across top marketplaces has steadily increased over the past three years due to advanced, proprietary AI matching algorithms.
  • Real-World Execution Moats: Companies that bridge digital AI with physical execution have highly defensible moats. John Deere has built an enduring moat not just from software, but by integrating GPS guidance, machine vision, and sensor arrays across millions of tractors in 130 countries.

Overcoming the AI Productivity Lag (The Solow Paradox)

  • The Historical Electricity Lag: When electricity was first introduced to Manhattan in 1882, macroeconomic productivity flatlined for nearly 30 years. Factories had been architected around steam belts and shafts; productivity did not double until the 1910s when factories were completely redesigned from scratch to suit electrical power.
  • The Solow Paradox: Named after economist Robert Solow’s 1987 observation that "computers are everywhere except in the productivity statistics." Technological revolutions require a significant lag phase while the real economy digests and reorganizes around them.
  • The AI Replatforming: The current disconnect between advanced AI capabilities and lagging national productivity stats is a modern Solow Paradox. However, because the friction of starting global, automated businesses has collapsed, the reorganization phase for AI will occur much faster than prior industrial revolutions.

Notable Quotes

"On net, with AI, we expect fewer people per firm, more output per firm, just more firms, and more coordination happening through market-like mechanisms." — John Collison, Co-founder and President, Stripe
"AI actually is showing up in the macro data; you just have to know where to look... Solopreneurship is now how close to 5 million Americans earn their living." — Emily Sands, Head of Data and AI, Stripe
"Transformative technology looks for a long time like it's not doing much. If you're looking at the economic gauges, you're kind of sitting there and tapping the gauge: 'Is this thing on?' ... Electrification took 30 years to reorganize the economy, but I suspect we won't need to wait anywhere near as long as that for AI." — John Collison, Co-founder and President, Stripe

So, you usually, in most years, get this gentle, predictable slope downward. This chart only goes up to 2024. If we add 2025, it is totally different and bucks the trend. Another phenomenon you might have heard about is this K-shaped economy: the idea that wealthy consumers are holding things up, representing an increasingly large share of the spending. If we look at the ratio of high-income to low-income consumer spending in our data, you'd expect this chart to be going up in a K-shaped economy. But it's the opposite. The line is trending gradually downwards. The gap between high-income and low-income spending has been shrinking. So, we don't actually see, when we look for it, this overreliance of the economy on high-end consumers that everyone has been talking about. The other major economic topic is the worry that AI is taking all the jobs. If you look at the numbers, it's true that the labor market is cooling slightly. US unemployment rates are ticking up about a percentage point over the past three years. But you have to ask the question: how much of that is actually AI? Part of it is the delayed pandemic hangover. If you look at the hiring rate, companies really binged on hiring in 2021 and 2022, and they're still unwinding that. Tighter immigration also played a bit of a role. And then, of course, you have interest rates. The Fed's latest forecast for the end of 2026 is half a point higher than they predicted at this time two years ago. Money is tighter, and tight money slows hiring. So, AI is in the mix, and it's presumably going to have a big future impact. But it's one force among many, and probably not the dominant one in the labor market numbers you've seen to date. Now, you're probably wondering what we're seeing at Stripe and how it corresponds to what's going on in the wider world. Stripe now processes almost 2% of global GDP, so we have a really useful window into the forefront of the internet economy. There are three areas that I want to dive into deeper with you. The first is that we're seeing a structural increase in economic dynamism. So, this is a totally new startup playbook: you launch globally on day one, you keep headcount very lean, and you automate aggressively. But not every business can be a solopreneur or a top AI startup. So, what does all of this mean for the median firm? Despite the fact that one of us has a PhD in economics and the other dropped out of college and now hosts a podcast in a pub, John is going to teach us a little bit about economic history. I think you might be surprised to learn just how many economic theories have been devised over a pint, Dr. Sands. Okay, some economic history, are we ready? In 1931, a 21-year-old British student took a road trip across Depression-era America. Back then, as now, a bunch of young people were pretty into communism. Central planning had this real intellectual prestige at the time. A lot of smart people thought that top-down coordination might be the way to do things, far better than messy, chaotic markets. And this student noticed something interesting: within a firm, resources aren't allocated by prices. There's no little market inside the company; companies themselves are centrally planned. And so he wondered: if markets are so efficient, why do we keep building little islands of central planning inside markets? His name was Ronald Coase, and his answer was that firms exist because coordinating inside a company is often actually cheaper and easier than coordinating through markets. That answer won him the Nobel Prize 60 years later. They don't rush into decisions over there in Stockholm. I know what you're thinking: John's economic story is heavy on narrative and light on economics. But here's where we're going: what's the Coasean reading of AI? In the near term, the within-firm effect is the most obvious. Companies have shared context, systems of record, and aligned incentives, and all of that allows for easier coordination with AI. You're probably feeling this in all your own companies already. But in the medium term, external markets are likely to get even more efficient. Agents are already great at discovery. They make it trivially easy to integrate a new piece of software, and they make contracting much more straightforward. As we'll discuss in a minute, they can transact much more frequently and for smaller amounts than humans can. Together, all of this should bring coordination costs between firms down by quite a bit. Now, some of these changes will take a little while to play out, but some of them are already here. On net, with AI, we expect fewer people per firm, more output per firm, simply more firms, and more coordination happening through market-like mechanisms. Coase would have liked that idea. He always thought firms were an inefficient setup. The more we can do via markets, the better. An indie hacker from another era. Yeah, thanks, Emily.
