And it raises a really interesting question, which is my third topic. In a world where intelligence can do all of that, and everyone has access to that kind of intelligence, what actually becomes more valuable? This is an old rule in economics: when something gets cheap, its complements get more valuable. So, when containerized shipping collapsed the cost of moving goods, ports that could handle the ships became much more valuable. The first radio spectrum auctions in the 1990s raised hundreds of millions of dollars. But then mobile phones got cheap, loads of people had them, and the same airwaves were suddenly worth orders of magnitude more; governments started auctioning them off for tens of billions of dollars. So, these things have joint demand curves. And so, one question we should be asking about AI is: what are AI's complements? What are the complements to intelligence? What becomes more valuable as intelligence becomes cheaper? Some of the answers are obvious. For example, you can see this effect very clearly in chips. You know, GPUs were really useful before AI, but it's clear from NVIDIA's deliveries and market cap that chips have become much more useful recently. They were previously majority gaming, and now you see the compute and networking segment take off. Same goes for energy. Power is more valuable if you have intelligence to plug it into. Nuclear power is undergoing a renaissance, largely because we just need nuclear power to power data centers. In the meantime, we're going to need a lot of gas turbines, which you can read about in the report. You can see the order volumes taking off here. This is also reflected in the market value of the handful of companies that make them—this is Siemens Energy, one of the big gas turbine makers. But a less discussed complement to AI is proprietary data, which gets much more valuable when you can let super-intelligent agents reason over it. One way you can tell data's getting more valuable is that companies that used to give it away for free have stopped doing that. This chart shows the percentage of various parts of the internet that have been shut off to AI crawling. Instead, those companies are starting to monetize it. Reddit has always had a ton of data—you know, all the comments in the subreddits were always there. But before AI, it was a dormant asset on the balance sheet, and today their non-ad revenue, which comes from data agreements, is $35 million a quarter. We see the same dynamic with our own data. Take Stripe Radar. Stripe Radar has always been able to reason across Stripe's entire corpus of data, but in recent years, as the AI models have gotten better, the underlying data is then more valuable. Network effects is another one we should talk about. Buyers and sellers still need places to meet, and if switching costs are low, then network effects are even more important. To understand this better, we took a look at the public take rate for 10 top marketplaces. What you see is take rates flat for a few years, and then you get this bump up during the pandemic and then a steady increase over the past three years as marketplaces see higher returns to better AI techniques. The last complement I want to call out is just companies that have figured out the complex interactions between software systems and real-world execution. I think we'll see that that is an enduring moat. Take John Deere. If I asked you to name an AI beneficiary, John Deere might not be the first name that you'd think of. But they've spent years integrating GPS guidance, machine vision, and sensor arrays into their equipment. And the defensible part, the moat here, it's not the AI; lots of people could build the AI. It's having the tractors in the fields across 130 countries. So, those are five things we think probably increase in value alongside AI and therefore create even more durable competitive advantages in the years ahead. And for all of you, AI should change how you think about your own competitive advantage. You might previously have built the best software in your space, but you might be finding that software is not the name of the game anymore. What you do have is powerful proprietary data, interesting network effects, real-world operations, and tools that took a decade to get right—all sorts of advantages that hold their value or even become more valuable in a world of abundant intelligence. So, as we wrap up, will you indulge me in just a little more economic history? I can't resist. In 1882, Thomas Edison lit up 82 customers in lower Manhattan using six dynamos. Finally, electricity in Manhattan! And for decades after, even as electricity adoption grew, productivity growth barely budged, or even slowed down as the railroad investment boom started to wear off. And the economists were confused initially. I mean, we had this awesome technology in electricity; why wasn't it showing up in the statistics? The problem wasn't the technology; the electricity did work. It was that the economy had to digest it. You see, factories had been built around steam—the shafts, the belts, and the floor plans—it was all wrong for electricity. And it wasn't until we redesigned factories from scratch that the productivity gains finally appeared. People sometimes forget, but this took a full 30 years from 1882 all the way to the late 1910s. And then in the 1910s, in that single decade, the growth rate of output per worker more than doubled. But there was this big lag. We saw the same phenomenon again with the birth of computing. In fact, economists have since dubbed this whole phenomenon the Solow Paradox, after Robert Solow's 1987 quip that computers were everywhere to be seen except in the productivity statistics. He was right; they weren't to be seen, and they wouldn't be until the mid-1990s. Transformative technology looks for a long time like it's not doing much. If you're looking at the economic gauges, you're kind of sitting there and you're tapping the gauge: "Is this thing on?" I think this is what we're actually watching in real time with AI. You see the seeds of this in the phenomena I already mentioned. The minimum efficient size of a serious business is collapsing. Solopreneurs are scaling to seven figures and beyond. Agents are buying from agents. Companies are launching globally from day one. But none of that fits in the old model. These changes might yield productivity dividends tomorrow because we have to digest them. But they're the early indicators of an economy that's replatforming itself. The businesses you're all building now are not a footnote to AI history; they are the AI history. They are the story of the economic and productivity gains. Electrification took 30 years to reorganize the economy, but I suspect we won't need to wait anywhere near as long as that for AI. Thank you so much for being here. I hope you are getting a ton out of Sessions. Enjoy the rest of your morning, and I will see you back here for our final fireside with Daniel Gross and Nat Friedman this afternoon.

Episode 54: Michael Shvo - Chairman and CEO of SHVO

Generating Alpha Podcast(1.9K) ·32:21 ·41 views · 2026-05-19
Michael Shvo (Chairman and CEO, SHVO)
Michael Shvo, founder and CEO of SHVO, shares how he rose from a penniless immigrant to managing an $8 billion super-prime real estate portfolio.
TL;DR Michael Shvo, founder and CEO of SHVO, shares how he rose from a penniless immigrant to managing an $8 billion super-prime real estate portfolio. His success is built on inventing the "real estate team" brokerage model, pioneering fashion-branded residences, and executing...

TL;DR

Michael Shvo, founder and CEO of SHVO, shares how he rose from a penniless immigrant to managing an $8 billion super-prime real estate portfolio. His success is built on inventing the "real estate team" brokerage model, pioneering fashion-branded residences, and executing high-conviction, contrarian acquisitions during market downturns.

From Harlem to Real Estate Pioneer

  • Early Adversity & Financial Whiplash: Grew up in Israel to academic parents. As a teenager, Shvo made millions trading Israeli oil and gas stocks, only to lose everything. He arrived in New York in 1995 with just $3,000, sleeping on a floor in Harlem.
  • The Taxi Fleet Venture: To survive, Shvo leased a single Crown Victoria yellow cab and subleased it to drivers. Within three months, he built a fleet of 10 yellow cabs and 30 drivers operating out of Midtown Manhattan.
  • Inventing the "Real Estate Team" Model: Shvo transitioned to Douglas Elliman as a rental broker, renting 360 low-end apartments in his first year by working off-hours to grab client calls. To scale past the hourly limits of a single broker, he hired a group of 27 people to show properties while he focused solely on closing deals—effectively inventing the team/group brokerage concept now standard across the residential industry.

Transforming Real Estate Through Luxury & Art

  • The Birth of Branded Residences: Frustrated by the plain, uninspired marketing of high-end real estate compared to luxury fashion houses like Chanel or Louis Vuitton, Shvo founded Shvo Marketing in 2004.
      • He hired talent directly from Bergdorf Goodman, Gucci, and Brioni to bring a high-end customer experience to real estate.
      • He partnered with Giorgio Armani to design 20 Pine, creating the world’s first fashion-branded residential building and pioneering a multi-billion-dollar global trend.
  • Transitioning to Development: Shvo shifted from marketing consulting to development to fully control execution. His first project, The Getty on 24th Street and 10th Avenue, was designed by Peter Marino and featured a highly publicized art exhibition ("The Sheep Station") that drove significant commercial leasing interest.
  • Integrating Public Art: Shvo leverages his super-prime real estate sites (such as the Transamerica Pyramid and The Raleigh) to exhibit museum-quality public art, aiming to remove the intimidation barrier of traditional galleries while driving substantial asset foot traffic.

The "Super-Prime" Strategy & Contrarian Pandemic Bets

  • Aggressive Pandemic Buying: While the market stalled during COVID-19, SHVO completed massive acquisitions, including the Transamerica Pyramid in San Francisco, "Big Red" in Chicago, and 711 Fifth Avenue in New York.
      • Financing Advantage: By buying during the market lull, SHVO secured 10-year fixed-rate acquisition loans between 2.5% and 3%—rates that would be 6% to 7% today.
  • Conviction-Driven Investment: Shvo rejected the permanent "work-from-home" narrative, utilizing biblical philosophies to assert that the pandemic was not the end of the world and that humans are inherently social creatures who require physical collaboration.
  • Tripling Rents at the Transamerica Pyramid: Despite widespread skepticism about San Francisco’s office market, SHVO's repositioning of the pyramid—incorporating a Norman Foster design, public art, and luxury restaurants—successfully drove building rents from $60 to $300 per square foot.
  • The Resilience of Super-Prime: Shvo emphasizes that B- and C-grade properties fail during crises, whereas hyper-luxury, super-prime locations sustain value because they are irreplaceable 20-year plays.

Personal Discipline & Advice for the Future

  • A Spiritual and Physical Routine: Shvo wakes up at 4:00 or 4:30 AM daily. He spends his first five hours alternating between three hours of Torah study and two hours of gym training to prepare his soul and body before starting his business day at 10:00 AM.
  • The Importance of Specialization: Shvo advises young professionals and entrepreneurs to be specialists rather than generalists. In an era increasingly automated by AI, individuals must carve out a highly differentiated, unique selling proposition.

Notable Quotes

"Luxury is basically the idea that you convince people to go ahead and pay for something that's above and beyond the material value of what it is." — Michael Shvo
"Survival is a great tool for success, because when you have no choice, you put it all in." — Michael Shvo
"Even through the pandemic, what we've seen is that super-prime locations and super-prime buildings sustain... [There was] blood on the streets, but not on the streets that we want to buy real estate." — Michael Shvo
"Don’t try to be everything for everybody. Be the right thing for the right person at the right place at the right time." — Michael Shvo
This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Michael Shvo, founder and CEO of Shvo and one of the most visionary builders in luxury real estate today. At 23 years old, Michael landed in New York from Israel with $3,000 to his name. He started by managing a fleet of taxis and within a decade, he'd become the top broker at Douglas Elliman, doing over $300 million in sales across more than 400 deals. He left to found Shvo in 2004 and within four years, had done billions in transactions worldwide. He pioneered the marriage of fashion and real estate, bringing Armani to residential development before anyone else and built a firm that now oversees more than $8 billion in assets across New York, Miami, San Francisco, Chicago, and Los Angeles. His portfolio includes some of the most iconic addresses in the country: 711 Fifth Avenue, the Crown Building, "Big Red" in Chicago, and the Mandarin Oriental Residences—properties defined not just by scale, but by the art, architecture, and culture woven into every one of them. In our conversation, we talked about arriving in America with nothing, building one of the most recognized names in luxury real estate, what he actually means when he talks about luxury, and much more. If you enjoy this episode, please follow the podcast and rate it five stars on Spotify, subscribe on YouTube, and share it with anyone who you think might find it valuable. I really enjoyed having this conversation with Michael, and I hope you guys enjoy listening. Thanks. Thanks, Michael, for coming on, really appreciate it. Thank you. Thank you for having me. Well, I want to start where I always do: the beginning. Tell me about your childhood. If I'm correct, you grew up in Arsuf, Israel, the son of two chemistry professors. What was that environment like? What kind of kid were you? Tell me all about it. It's a good question. You know, my memory doesn't go that far, but my parents were academics. They were both organic chemists, teaching at Tel Aviv University, and then they spent a year at Stanford and a year at Yale. That's when I first came to the States, in 1978 and 1979, which really was the premise of what brought me back here—kind of the American Dream as a kid. As a six-year-old kid in 1978, when Israel was a very undeveloped country, coming to America and coming to New York, seeing the buildings, seeing everything, and seeing the technology at the time, it was magical. So that was where my love affair with the United States started, and a little bit with real estate. But as a kid, I was a problematic kid. I wasn't really good with authority then. We grew up in a place that was far away from school, so it's not like I could walk away and see friends and things of that nature, so obviously that shaped me in a specific way. Then, after my childhood, you go to the army in Israel. I served in the army for three years and I studied a bit. I got kicked out of high school; as I said, I was not the best with authority as a kid. I ended up in the United States after I made a lot of money as a very young kid. I started trading stocks with nothing, with a few thousand dollars, and made millions of dollars as a kid. Millions. Wow. I was trading on gas and oil stocks in Israel, which just didn't exist back then. Now they have discovered gas in Israel, but back then the fluctuation with the stocks was really high. I figured out the trade there, and then one day I lost everything, as expected, which was the greatest blessing in that sense. I got on a plane the next day with $3,000 of cash in my pocket and ended up in the United States. And from there, if I'm correct, you started managing a fleet of taxis. Why not go back into trading? Why was that the first step? I had no money. I was sleeping on the floor in Harlem. I was literally sleeping in a guy's house on the floor in Harlem. When I got out of the airport, I took a cab to this guy's house and I asked, "How does this taxi thing work?" He explained to me that there's a medallion and there's a car. I ended up buying one Crown Victoria—you're too young to know what a Crown Victoria is, but it's an old yellow cab that you see in the movies, in the very old movies. I leased a medallion, and I subleased the car and the medallion to two drivers by shifts, making a bit of money. Three months later, I ended up having 10 yellow cabs and 30 drivers that I was operating out of a street corner here in Midtown. That is kind of how I got my start in New York City. Different things kind of happened and I ended up, in essence, by sheer coincidence—although everything is from God, so there is no sheer coincidence—being a real estate broker. I knew nobody. I was at a point where, after I did the yellow cabs for a couple of years, I had to do something else, and somebody told me, "You should be a real estate broker." I said, "I know nothing about real estate," but he introduced me to a lovely man who ended up hiring me at a company called Sofa Real Estate, which ended up being purchased by Douglas Elliman. And I became a real estate broker. I started as a rental broker, only renting apartments less than $2,000 a month. Every single customer that nobody in the office wanted—and this goes back to the mid-90s—they used to send to me. This was not the days of the internet and all this stuff; there was no StreetEasy. These were the days when we were wearing beepers and pagers. I used to come to work very early to the office and go home very late in order to pick up all the calls that used to come into the office during off-hours when people were not there. My first year, I rented 360 apartments. I think the most expensive one was $2,400 a month. Eventually, a few years later, I became the top residential broker in the United States and then left Douglas Elliman to start a real estate marketing firm, which partnered with developers to come up with concept marketing and sales for new developments. And why make the change from Elliman to doing the marketing? What was the catalyst there? It's interesting. So at the time, the Time Warner building, which was the number one building in Columbus Circle, was the new development—kind of the number one development in the country. I sold a bunch of apartments there to clients of mine. I thought it was odd that this building had, at the time, a billion dollars of inventory, which was a lot 20 years ago, and yet they marketed the building through some conference room on an upper floor with some plastic drawings and a plastic model. The whole thing didn't add up because down the street you had Louis Vuitton, Chanel, Dior, and all these luxury brands investing tens of millions of dollars in their stores to create a customer experience. Meanwhile, here, where you had an inventory that was probably 100 times what they had in any of those stores, the experience was very plain. So I figured there was a better way to do it, and I started Shvo Marketing. We hired a bunch of people from the luxury world who worked for Bergdorf, Gucci, and Brioni, people who understood luxury, and we brought luxury marketing into real estate. Two things that I've done in my career really changed the industry, I think. When I was a broker, going back a few years, I used to show 40 apartments a day. I maxed out the hours. I figured there was no more growth because you're limited by the hours of the day, even if you show more expensive apartments. So I ended up bringing a guy to work for me. I said, "Here's a client. Take him, go show him these apartments, don't say a word, bring him back to the office, and I'll close the deal." Then I took another guy and another guy, and I ended up with 27 people working for me. That was the creation of what today is known as "the team" or "the group" concept in residential real estate. When I started in real estate, there were only individuals. I created a team, the Shvo Group, and that became, in essence, the first group in real estate. Today, as you know, everybody has a group. That is the way the business operates, and that was my first contribution to the business. The other thing on the development side is that I'm known for inventing the marriage of fashion and real estate. I brought Giorgio Armani to design the first residential building in the world to be designed by a fashion designer. That changed the world. I brought Versace, Cavalli, and a bunch of other brands into real estate. The whole idea of branded residences, this mix of luxury and residential, is something that I started exactly 20 years ago with 20 Pine, which was designed by Armani. Today, if a new development is not branded, something feels off. I left Douglas Elliman because I thought there was a huge need in the market to fill this gap, taking real estate from a plain four walls, a kitchen, and a bathroom, to a true experience. Later, when we transitioned into office and retail development, it was all with that same concept: real estate is about an experience, not just about the walls, the kitchen, and the bathroom. Tell me about that transition into development. What were the first few formative deals, and what do you think having that background in luxury marketing made you do differently in the beginning? The issue there was that in my previous business as a marketer, you are essentially a consultant. They pay you, you give advice, and they can choose to do what you say or not. At some point, I got tired of trying to sit there and convince other people why they should do certain things to make money for them. It didn't make that much more money for me because I was on a percentage, but for the developer, it made a huge difference if they did the right thing. Some developers listened, and some didn't listen so much. So I said, instead of just advising others, I'll just do it myself. My first project at the time was The Getty on 24th and 10th Avenue, which I brought Peter Marino to design. It was a beautiful building, and it became quite well-known because we put an art exhibition there, "The Sheep Station." We put Lalanne sheep sculptures inside a former gas station, which was a huge success at the time. I was always thinking about unique opportunities to draw attention to our projects. We planted grass inside the gas station and put up a picket fence. It became the most-viewed art show in Chelsea at the time. It ended up driving two real estate deals because I leased the gallery and the space above the gallery to an art foundation because they saw the foot traffic we generated with a great show. All these art marketing ideas are not just because I'm in the mood to do something cool; there is always a financial drive behind them to bring money to the bottom line. And if I'm correct, you actually spent a few years in the art world, I think around the 2008 period. Tell me about that period. What did you learn? I guess not many real estate developers spend time in the art world, so what did you take out of that experience? In December of 2007, I gave a keynote speech to the board of Hilton where I said that the real estate market was coming to an end and was going to collapse. I decided to retire at that point and gave myself 12 months to do so. Sure enough, nine months later, Lehman Brothers collapsed. I was already very involved in the art world prior to that, buying and selling. The art world is an interesting industry because values are driven heavily by perception—which is exactly what real estate is if you take away the four walls, kitchen, and bathroom. If you're down to only the bricks and the mortar, it's different. But with any luxury brand—whether it's a watch, a bag, or a car—you're not paying because the material is that much more expensive. You're paying because someone convinced you that an Hermès bag or a Chanel bag has more value than a Coach bag. It might not cost much more to make, maybe a peanut more, but it is sold for a massive premium. The same thing applies to watches. You can sell a watch for half a million dollars, and maybe it cost two or three thousand dollars to make. That's the beauty of luxury. Luxury is basically the idea that you convince people to go ahead and pay for something that is above and beyond the material value of what it actually is. And what do you classify as the Shvo perception? What do you want Shvo buildings to be perceived as? For us, the important thing is that it is very driven by service. The quality of the finishes is something that a lot of people can do, and that's ultimately a taste issue. You might like one designer, and I might like another. But one thing we are very focused on, both in residential and specifically in commercial buildings, is service. Commercial buildings are historically secondary to residential as far as quality is concerned, mostly because nobody has been paying attention. Service is something that simply doesn't exist in the commercial world. It exists in hospitality and residential, but not in commercial. During that time when you were looking at art, other than perception marketing, was there anything you took away that influences how you build buildings today? We work with some of the top architects in the world. Today, half of the buildings we design are by Norman Foster or Peter Marino. Peter Marino is very involved in the art world and often commissions specific artists, as does Norman Foster, to do site-specific installations. We've done that throughout our buildings. The other thing I do is use our sites, because they are prime real estate, to display art to the public. I believe art is something that a lot of people miss out on; they feel like they don't understand it and are intimidated. So we install public art at our super-prime sites, like the Transamerica Pyramid, The Getty, and The Raleigh. It brings the public in to view the art, which is a way we can give back to the community while bringing high-quality art to high-traffic locations. When talking about those architects, how do those relationships work, and what freedoms do you give your architects that maybe other developers don't? How is your relationship unique with them? Working with architects like Peter Marino or Norman Foster is a true collaboration. I am very close with both of them on a personal level; they are good friends of mine, and that relationship makes the buildings much better. Where other developers make mistakes is they want to hire the big-name architect, but then they are not willing to pay for the execution of the design. Their fees are high, but what is truly expensive is executing their design properly. Many developers want the brand names like Peter Marino, Norman Foster, Renzo Piano, or Herzog & de Meuron, but they try to cut corners afterward. Peter Marino, for example, rarely works with developers because he knows they won't spend the money to build a proper Peter Marino building. He has done four buildings for me. My commitment to quality and execution is very high to ensure we align with the designer's original vision and execute it at the correct level. Touching a bit on the developments you've done over time, if you had to pick one during your entire career that you see as the most monumental or meaningful to you, what is the story behind that development? Each one of them has its own story. The Transamerica Pyramid, which we recently repositioned, really changed San Francisco. When I bought the building, people thought I was crazy. They told me the area was dead, it was on the wrong side of town, and it was a dilapidated building. It became the number one building in San Francisco. We took rents from $60 to $300, tripling the rents in the building. We repositioned the entire building through Norman Foster's design, the art, and the restaurants, which had an effect not only on the building itself but on the entire neighborhood. Similarly, we rezoned Miami Beach and got a 20-story building approved. These are projects that have an effect far beyond the boundaries of the building itself, allowing us to positively affect the entire community. You've obviously had a lot of success in real estate, both as a broker and then as a developer. I liked the story where you built the first team and hired people to show apartments while you closed them. You rose up the ranks of Douglas Elliman very quickly and built Shvo in a shorter period than most. Do you see your talent in developing as innate, or is it learned? Why is Michael Shvo great at what he does? My experience as a broker is what gave me the ability to do what I do as a developer. The most successful developers in the country often come from backgrounds in construction, law, or architecture. I came from the consumer side. I walked people by the hand into apartments, so I know exactly what windows they like, what bedrooms they want, and where they want the bathroom. I have first-hand experience of what the buyer wants, and we pride ourselves on spending a tremendous amount of time understanding the buyer before we start building. I don't believe in the strategy of "build it and they will come." If the market is on fire, anyone will buy anything. But if you want to get a super-premium for your building, you have to build something that is highly targeted for the right person at the right place at the right time. I want to take a moment to touch on a specific moment in your career. A common thread in the careers of ultra-successful people is that there are generally a handful of times in which they go against the grain, go very contrarian, and bet big. During the pandemic, you went on one of the most aggressive acquisition runs of your career—buying the Transamerica Pyramid, "Big Red" in Chicago, and 711 Fifth Avenue, spending billions while everyone else was staying on the sidelines. What was the logic behind that, and how did you build that conviction? I remember being interviewed just when COVID started on a podcast with the publisher of The Real Deal. He asked me what was going to happen with this COVID thing. I was sitting in my house, spraying Amazon boxes and leaving them out for two days, and everybody thought the end of the world was coming. I told him, "Listen, I'm a religious Jew. The only answer I have is from the Bible." I pulled up the Bible and referenced Noah. When God flooded the world, Noah survived in the ark, and afterward, God made a covenant never again to destroy mankind. I quoted that and said, "Look, that tells you this is not the end of the world." If we know the world is not coming to an end, then this is the best time to buy real estate. Then the question was, "Well, people are saying everyone is going to work from home going forward." I pulled out the Bible again. When man was created, God said, "Lo tov heyot ha'adam levado"—it is not good that man should be alone. I said, "Do you think the whole world is going to sit in their pajamas in their kitchens on Zoom for the rest of their lives?" Being alone is not how humans are wired. I went on CNBC and Bloomberg and said this whole notion of work-from-home is nonsense and is going to go away. That conviction is what got me to buy those buildings. I had conviction not because I'm a genius, but because it says so in a book. It's not the end of the world, and humans are not meant to be alone. While real estate prices were dropping and interest rates were near zero, we locked in fixed loans between 2.5% and 3% for 10 years. Today, those same loans would be 6% to 7%. I got the answer from God. At this point, Shvo has over $8 billion in assets across a handful of cities. How would you describe the larger vision, and what do you want Shvo to become? What's next? I'm partnered with the German state pension fund, and we had some opportunities to make certain exits that made sense at the time. But I'm a one-trick pony; I know how to do one thing and one thing only, and that is super-prime real estate. When the pandemic hit, everyone thought they were going to buy assets for pennies on the dollar. We didn't buy assets for pennies on the dollar, but the debt was very cheap. People would ask me if there was blood on the streets, and I said, "Yes, there is blood on the streets, but not on the streets where we want to buy real estate." Through the pandemic, we saw that super-prime locations and super-prime buildings sustain themselves. B and C buildings in the wrong locations suffered, but our strategy of investing in super-prime real estate works as long as you have a long-term vision. This is a 20-year play because these are irreplaceable assets. I want to go all the way back and touch on that moment you landed in New York in 1995 with $3,000 in your pocket. What was the dream for you at that moment, knowing what you have accomplished now? It's interesting you ask. I was not in a position to dream; I was in survival mode. People think, "Wow, you came to New York with a dream," but the dream was just to have food to eat that evening. Today it's a great topic of conversation, but when you are 23 or 24 years old, it is highly stressful. I went days without having money to buy food, and I slept on someone's floor in Harlem. I was too embarrassed to ask my parents for help. My only concern was survival. By the way, survival is a great tool for success because when you have no choice, you put everything you have into it. That was the initial phase. What does a typical day in the life of Michael Shvo look like at this point, running an $8 billion development firm? I wake up very early in the morning and spend the majority of my morning doing two things: studying Torah and going to the gym. I study first, then go to the gym for an hour, study Torah again, go to the gym for another hour, and study again. My first five hours of the day are basically three hours of Torah study and two hours at the gym. Once my soul and body are centered, I start dealing with work. I find it is great because I never used to have time to study, and now I can invest time into it. The more I study, the more I realize how much I don't know. My library is full of books, and God willing, I will read all of them one day. After my morning routine, I get to work and take it from there. My final question, which I ask every guest: I'm 16 right now. If you had to give one piece of advice to a 16-year-old today, what would it be? Be a specialist. In my opinion, the only way to succeed is to be a specialist and not a generalist. When you ask some developers what they build, they might say they have buildings on Long Island and on Fifth Avenue. But what is your unique selling proposition? When I teach brokers, I ask them, "There are tens of thousands of brokers. Why should I use you? What do you bring to the table that the ten other people sitting next to you don't?" If you don't differentiate yourself today, especially with the world turning flat because of AI, you won't stand out. You have to be an entrepreneur or work with your hands. The jobs of the future require you to know how to think and bring a unique perspective to something. Don't try to be everything for everybody. Be the right thing for the right person at the right place at the right time. Well, Michael, it's been an absolute pleasure. Thanks for taking the time, and I'm glad we could make this happen. Thank you. Thanks, Amir.

Sam Altman in conversation with Patrick Collison

Stripe(105.0K) ·57:31 ·5.7K views · 2026-05-19
Sam Altman (Co-founder and CEO, OpenAI) Patrick Collison (CEO, Stripe)
OpenAI Cofounder and CEO Sam Altman and Stripe CEO Patrick Collison discuss the parabolic rise of AI agents, OpenAI's transition into a high-volume global utility provider, and how the democratization of frontier models is fundamentally shifting the ...
TL;DR OpenAI Cofounder and CEO Sam Altman and Stripe CEO Patrick Collison discuss the parabolic rise of AI agents, OpenAI's transition into a high-volume global utility provider, and how the democratization of frontier models is fundamentally shifting the landscape for...

TL;DR

OpenAI Cofounder and CEO Sam Altman and Stripe CEO Patrick Collison discuss the parabolic rise of AI agents, OpenAI's transition into a high-volume global utility provider, and how the democratization of frontier models is fundamentally shifting the landscape for startups, enterprise operations, and scientific discovery.

The Parabolic Rise of AI Agents and Interface Evolution

  • Threshold of Capability: Late last year and early this year, AI model intelligence—specifically for coding—crossed a critical reasoning threshold, causing user metrics to go parabolic.
      • Primary Interfaces: Models like Codex are transitioning from simple programming assistants into the primary interface for computer interaction.
      • Automating Computer "Drudgery": The next major unlock is not a single domain, but the automated coordination of mundane computer tasks (e.g., cross-app copy-pasting, messaging, and basic administrative flows), allowing users to sit back and watch the AI execute workflows.
  • The Magic of Agentic Systems: Altman highlights "Open Claw" (Claude) as a breakthrough AGI moment where a stateful chat session paired with tool usage crossed into feeling "magical."
      • Emergent Behaviors: Altman noted a test where GPT-5.5 planned its own launch party, providing highly detailed feedback on event flow, dates (suggesting May 5th), and an interactive suggestion loop for GPT-5.6.
      • Tool-Enabled Executions: Collison shared how Stripe's Tempo blockchain team built a Slack-integrated agent harness that successfully drafts, deploys, and verifies code pull requests autonomously.

Scaling OpenAI: From Research Lab to Global Token Utility

  • The Three Phases of OpenAI: Altman outlines the structural evolution of the 10-year-old company:
      • Phase 1 (The Research Lab): Finding breakthroughs to build AGI when the concept was widely dismissed.
      • Phase 2 (The Product Company): Learning how to ship products (e.g., ChatGPT) and scale customer-facing infrastructure.
      • Phase 3 (The Token Utility): Becoming a "mega-scale token factory" and infrastructure utility provider, requiring deep full-stack integration and massive hardware build-outs.
  • CapEx and the Compute Build-Out: Building out AI data center infrastructure is on track to be the most expensive infrastructure project in human history.
      • Uncapped Demand: Despite fears of a CapEx bubble, Altman argues that demand for cheap, rapid machine intelligence is practically uncapped, meaning the immense capital expenditures are justified by actual revenue and scaling utility.
  • Culture and Elite Talent Management: Altman manages highly iconoclastic, elite performers by focusing on deep, shared organizational convictions rather than equal distribution of resources.
      • High-Conviction Betting: During the training of GPT-3, OpenAI bet the vast majority of its computing resources on a single research program, ignoring criticisms from peer labs that decentralized research structures were safer.
  • The Secret Era of GPT-4: Following the completion of GPT-4's training, OpenAI spent eight months testing and refining the model internally before release, creating a bizarre period where only company insiders knew of the imminent paradigm shift.

The New Startup and Enterprise Playbook

  • Revenge of the "Idea Guys": The startup landscape is undergoing a massive shift where deep technical capability is no longer the sole primary gatekeeper for starting a tech company.
      • Focus on User Needs: Founders who deeply understand user pain points but cannot write code are now highly fundable, as AI can handle the end-to-end software development.
  • Harnesses over Wrappers: Instead of building simple "wrappers" that patch minor model deficiencies, startups should build robust "harnesses." Sustainable AI companies are those that actively benefit when underlying frontier models get smarter.
  • Uncomfortably Permissive Data Access: The most successful small startups are maximizing AI efficiency by giving models comprehensive, unchecked access to their internal codebases, Slack messages, emails, and recorded meetings.
  • Internal Automation Mandates: CEOs like Shopify’s Tobi Lütke are pioneering successful enterprise AI adoption by personally automating their workflows and mandating that all business segments adopt AI automation.

Accelerating Deep Tech, Science, and Global Philanthropy

  • The OpenAI Foundation: OpenAI is building what Altman expects to become the largest philanthropic foundation in the world, focused heavily on "AI resilience" and accelerating science.
  • Accelerating Scientific Discovery: The compounding effect of using models (like GPT-5.5) to speed up material science, biotechnology, and drug discovery could compress a decade of traditional research into a single year.
  • Curing Complex Diseases: Stripe and OpenAI are key partners of the Arc Institute, which aims to find the first cure for a complex genetic/environmental disease (beginning with Alzheimer's).
      • Evo2 Model: During a sabbatical at Arc, OpenAI Cofounder Greg Brockman helped train Evo2, the largest biology foundation model ever created.
  • Underrated Sectors & Predictions:
      • Material Science: Altman identifies material science as the most underrated AI-accelerated field, with massive implications for energy catalysts and advanced physical hardware.
      • Nuclear Fusion: Altman boldly predicts the world's first profitable nuclear fusion reactor will arrive within the next five years, driven partly by the massive electricity demands of data centers.

Notable Quotes

"We had to figure out how to build this like mega-mega scale token factory for the world. I think of what we're doing as building sort of a new utility... We need to make that as smart, as cheap, as abundant, as easy to use as possible." — *Sam Altman*
"The degree to which most people will realize they can like sit back and watch an AI do most of their like drudgery... is gonna surprise people. In my own experience trying to work that way, it actually gives me much more enjoyment of work." — *Sam Altman*
"I believe in democratization and personal agency and access and that everybody deserves a really great life... I think my single biggest contribution has been, and will be, pushing for this to be a democratized technology that people get to use and build on." — *Sam Altman*
I think we've some Codex fans in the audience? Love to hear that. How's the week going? So fun. It's a busy week. But I'm happy to be here; this is an unexpected surprise. Well, thank you for joining us, we appreciate it. So we opened this morning by saying that we've kind of arbitrarily decided that the singularity started on January 1st, and thus today is day 119. What do you think of that? It does feel like we are somehow in the takeoff. Day 119 feels like a reasonable enough guess. Yeah, I won't fight it. Did you, did you feel—so, we started to see a bunch of our metrics inflect as of late last year, beginning of this year. I mean, things were kind of doing well but somehow they really, just the shapes of the curves changed. They really went parabolic. Is that matched in what you guys see? Was there some trajectory change? Right, like, why are we seeing this? I do think the models got really good, especially for coding, but really good in general starting late last year, very early this year. And at least in my own experience of using the technology and seeing what other people are doing with it, there is also this sense that every week is now a little bit different than the week before. Just like a lot happens very fast. It seemed to all correlate with the models hitting some threshold. And why did they, like why did coding models suddenly start to click over the last, you know, couple months? Like was there a research trick? Was it just that we got enough code data in the pre-train? Like what, why did it suddenly start to work? Yeah, it's a great question. We've wondered a lot about like why kind of several people crossed that threshold at the same time. I'm sure it's a number of factors, but model intelligence, just the raw kind of reasoning horsepower, enough of a feedback loop with people using it for code to figure out where it was good and where you needed to improve it, enough data—I think it was like all of these things. And then also there was like a, like many other endeavors, once you know something's possible, it's much easier to go do it with vigor. And so it seems like Codex is kind of having a moment right now. Yeah, it really crossed some subjective threshold for me with the latest app updates and 5.5. And this is also like it's quite hard to say why right now and not a little bit sooner or why not the next model. But one of the things I have learned about the history of all of the things we've put out is it is very hard to say why this particular thing was the thing that worked. And this goes back to ChatGPT—like why was GPT-3.5 the thing that got over the threshold where most people went from saying 'not that impressive' to 'it's gonna change the world'? And why not one model earlier or later? I really can't explain it. You just kind of feel it. And the—I've had two inflection points with Codex. One was kind of with GPT-5.2 and then a really big one last few weeks where it's like, okay, this is going to be the primary interface to a computer for me. And is everyone using it for coding or are you starting to see usage diffuse into other domains? I think the most adamant users are still using it for coding, but there's been just this like tidal wave of people coming into Codex recently. And one of—and I'm like really trying to understand what has happened that is causing this, and the depth of what people are using it for, or starting to use it for, has surprised me. So certainly our ambition is for it not just to be about coding but to be about all the work you do in front of a computer. And you know, I would say we're maybe like 10% of the way there for the non-coding stuff, but now that we see what's happening, now we have a real user base sort of using it in these other ways, I think we'll get good at it very fast. What do you think will be the next domain that subjectively feels like it has this big unlock after coding? Like is it going to be spreadsheets? Will it be you know, performance reviews? What, what, what's it gonna be? So I think there will be a lot of—first of all, I do think coding's a little bit special and these models are a great fit for coding, as the world needs so much more code than currently gets written. There may be no other domain that is quite like coding, but there will be a lot of other ones that I think are close. But the next kind of coding-like thing that I think will happen is not any specific domain but the realization of how much time people waste trying to use a computer. And the idea that you can do a huge percentage of your day in a very different way. And you know, maybe—maybe you don't realize like how much time you spend clicking between messaging apps and copying and pasting stuff and like, you know, responding to very boring things that you could clearly automate once. But the degree to which most people will realize they can like sit back and watch an AI do most of their like drudgery is gonna surprise people. And in my own experience trying to work that way, it actually gives me much more like enjoyment of work. I didn't realize how much like the little stuff drags me down, gets me like out of a sort of happy flow state or whatever. So the subjective like quality of life improvement is huge. Are you an Open Claw user? I am. I—any Open Claw users here? We have some good news for you all coming. You can just tell them. Open Claw has been one of my biggest like 'this is magic AGI' moments ever in the field. I—I remember the first time someone told me about it, they were trying to explain it and I was like, 'Okay, that all sounds cool but you know, I can like make a lot of that work.' And then it was a real reminder how, when the models cross some threshold and also the product designer gets a handful of critical ideas really right, it's like a much more magical experience than it sounds like. I find I—I've been sort of an attempting Open Claw evangelist, trying to describe that experience that you just recounted to others. And I find it a difficult experience to communicate. I mean, it sounds kind of prosaic, right? It's a stateful ChatGPT session that can also make some use of tools and so forth. Like, what do you use your Open Claw for? If we scrolled through your message thread, what would we see? So, this is like a very embarrassing thing to admit. The thing I always try first with a new kind of AI system of any sort is—I'm like a home automation nerd, and so I try to build a better home automation interface system because it never works, like it's never good. And Open Claw was the first time that I was able to get like a setup that I was happy with. I also built a messaging app that I had always wanted to work. I've since switched it to something I built with Codex, but Open Claw was the first time I was able to—I'm sure like you, just feel like drowning in messages, and it's like this very unpleasant task to wake up in the morning and have to go through all this stuff. So I was like, 'Alright, I'm probably gonna be able to automate this.' And that was like—again, it should have been doable with previous systems. It's hard to explain like what it's like when it all actually just works and you trust that it's going to work. Yeah, there's all of this—there's all this stuff that feels, no matter how convinced you are intellectually that this is not a real thing wanting a real gift for itself, and no matter how much you're convinced like, 'Okay, this is like a weird emergent behavior and I'm not supposed to read into this,' there are these things that feel a little strange. Yeah, yeah, okay. 10 years. A long 10 years. I can't really remember pre-OpenAI life that well at this point. It feels like it's been so long, but yes. You've a singularity looking forward but also a singularity looking backward. So what's the craziest OpenAI story that's never been told? We kind of knew that it was dramatically better and different and gonna unlock a bunch of things in the world, and no one outside the company, or almost no one outside the company, knew about it. What's the—what's the Sam Altman management style? If I'm working for you directly or maybe indirectly, leading some product or something, what does that look like? I'm definitely not a hands-on manager. I'm very much of the style that you get great people, kind of give them a very high-level thing to point at, and try to let stuff just happen. I think there have been kind of two main phases of OpenAI and we're heading into a third. The first one was when we were only a research company, trying to figure out how we were going to build AGI at a time when it sounded completely, completely crazy and we really had no idea what to do. And then there was a second phase where, in addition to continuing to do that, we had to figure out how to build a product company. Now we have to, in addition to both of those two things, figure out how to build this like mega-mega scale token factory for the world. I think of what we're doing as building sort of a new utility, and people are going to want to use a lot of tokens, a lot of intelligence, in all sorts of ways. We need to make that as smart, as cheap, as abundant, as easy to use as possible. And that will require, I think, pretty deep full-stack integration in a massive, massive infrastructure build-out. The thing that I didn't really appreciate between the phase one, phase two shift was how much my management style had to change. So I either have to find someone or a few great people to hire, or I have to figure out how to do things in a different way. Or I have to build an AI that can manage this new thing. When I interviewed Jensen here two years ago, he told me and his several thousand closest friends about his 60 direct reports. I don't know exactly how to do this yet, but I would like to get to a model for OpenAI that is similar. Like, I would like us to be an infrastructure provider. I'd be happy for us to be a forever low-margin business as long as we can be huge and growing fast, and I would like us to supply kind of an intelligence meter. I don't know what quite to call it that companies can buy that they can use to automate things, accelerate things inside their company, or use to build products. People can buy it, people can take it with them, and we find ways to really align ourselves with the success of the entire, gigantic, distributed economic engine of the world. I believe that will work. I believe that switching costs of AI are going to make it hard to have huge margins in AI anyway. We've seen recently how easy it is—many people have seen—to switch from our competitor's coding product to ours. This is actually a consequence of AI getting smarter. It gets easier to do things like this; it gets easier to just say, 'Hey agent, go do this thing for me.' But if we can provide a utility, and people build on top of that utility, and we think of ourselves as that kind of a company, I think that can be quite powerful and very aligned. Well, we're happy to share lots of tips and tricks on running a low-margin business. That would be great. But so, many people have been either implicitly critical or, in some cases, explicitly critical of OpenAI for procuring so much compute. And I think not the Codex users. Right, exactly. So you, I think, were quite noteworthy for—as early as, I don't know exactly, but in the order of two or three years ago—stipulating what at the time sounded like preposterous figures with respect to the magnitude of the build-out that would be required. And obviously, the preposterousness of those figures now looks less and less tenuous by the day. Thoughts on compute, CapEx, the build-out? Yeah, it's going to take a lot of money. I think this will clearly, at this point, be the most expensive infrastructure project that the world has ever undertaken. The revenue is ramping to meet it, so people feel better about that. Also, the efficiency gains that we've all been finding are incredible. So we're going to get way more out of each GPU than I thought we were going to. But as has often been remarked, the demand goes up more than linearly as you drop the price of each kind of unit of intelligence, particularly if you can drop the price and the sort of speed with which you get it back. So this question now of, 'What is enough?'—I don't have a good answer to. In some sense, I think demand for intelligence at a low enough price is effectively uncapped. If we were in a CapEx bubble in the future, how would we tell? People love to proclaim bubbles. And I can't articulate why, but intellectually I kind of get it. And clearly, sometimes it's right. Like, there clearly are bubbles. But how you can discern between the amount of times someone calls a bubble and the times you're actually in a bubble, I have never figured out how to do. In my previous career I was an investor, so I was quite interested in trying to see if I could come up with some sort of framework for this and figure out when you're supposed to deploy capital or not, and I never was able to figure it out. Like, I went back and I read what smart people had said at different points in history, and I was like, 'Oh, they called it exactly right.' But then I read a little more and they had said it like ten more times in the ten previous years. I don't know; I don't have an answer. Economists are the people who have called eight of the last three recessions. But they're so happy when they're right. And then these super talented or effective people are not in every case super easy to work with. No. No. And look, some of them are wonderful people, and some of them are the most fantastic collaborators. I think we had very—even when people didn't like each other, and even when people thought they were much smarter than other people or had a better approach than other people, we had a few deeply shared convictions. We did, kind of collectively, believe in scale and concentrating resources, that we were going to do this one thing, and that we thought getting this right was important enough that people were going to put aside various personal conflicts. One of the most unusual things about OpenAI was that, at the time we trained GPT-3, the vast majority of our compute across the whole organization was going into this one single research program. We love music. Anyway, they would say—like, we're talking about managing unusual personalities. Yeah. They would say, 'You have to divide your compute equally; otherwise, you'll have this very toxic and competitive culture,' and this thing and that thing. And we would just take the approach that we're going to bet with conviction on this. It's not going to feel totally equal, but this is the right thing. We do think we know the direction we really want to go in. And they would say, 'Well, you know, you might be wrong; we have to do these other things, you have to have this research program.' Having a culture where we said we're going to have conviction, do this, and ignore the distractions was great. We hope this is a memorable Sessions for all of you. So John and I have been doing this thing at Stripe for, you know, quite a while now. We started out in 2010. You and Greg started out in 2015, and to your point, you've ventured through many trials and tribulations and stratospheric successes and all the rest. That was a nice little gloss over, but go ahead. Thoughts on—I mean, it's not easy to work successfully with a co-founder for now more than a decade, and for things, even after a decade, to seemingly work as well as they did from the beginning. Just thoughts on that partnership: why has it worked, and how have you guys made it such a success? Obviously, you and John knew each other for longer than Greg and I did, but Greg and I did know each other for a long time before OpenAI. And I think having the shared history really helps. One of the things that I had observed at Y Combinator was that one of the biggest predictors of success was whether the co-founders had known each other for a long time—at least relative to their lives, for a long time. And the teams that came together, like, seven days before applying to YC on a co-founder matching site or whatever—that didn't work too often. It wasn't impossible; I think there are one or two cases where it did work, but it was rare. So we had known each other for a while, and we had a sense of shared values, history, ecosystem, and we were clear on what we wanted to do. I think we had this deep mutual respect and complementary skill set that has just worked really well. I'm extremely grateful. I think having to go through any startup experience, but particularly an intense one, without a co-founder that you have a deep connection and trust with is really hard. I've watched people do it, but it's very hard. So I am, you know, extremely grateful that we've gotten to do this together. On an adjacent topic, you know we've been talking OpenAI, but then there's this entire ecosystem of companies and startups and enterprises that are building on the platform. It's obviously an interesting moment in startups given, on the one hand, the ability to build products and generate revenue at seemingly unprecedented rates. And certainly we see this in our Stripe data—the number of businesses reaching meaningful thresholds is far faster than it ever has been before. You are one of the most prolific and successful startup investors ever. You, of course, ran Y Combinator. Have the traits that make founders successful changed in this era, or is it sort of the same thing it's always been? There was a time when we used to make fun of the 'idea guy.' There are these people that wanted to start a company, and they'd say, 'I have the best idea—I'm not going to tell you what it is, but I have the best idea—I just need a coder to build it for me, and then I'm going to be in great shape.' We would make fun of these people; they weren't that successful. It was always personally annoying to me because it would be like saying, 'I have a great idea for a song, and I just need that guy with the guitar to make it for me.' And so it kind of didn't work, you know. YC had a version of this, which was that teams without technical founders were difficult to make work. All of a sudden, it's like the revenge of the idea guys, which is actually awesome for the world. I'm happy I'm here for it, for sure. But for a long time, I think the most important ingredient that I looked for—that YC looked for, that this part of our industry looked for on a founding team—was technical talent. And that's still very important, but now, people who just really deeply understand their users and can't code at all—I want to fund those people. And that's a big turnaround. How does one think about startup investing these days? Because on the one hand, you have a couple of years, potentially, to AGI or ASI or the singularity—who knows what—and then you have investing time horizons or funds with ten-year time horizons. How does that all fit together? Does it? I think to do anything at this point on a ten-year time horizon requires a real suspension of disbelief. And yet, that's probably the right way to live your life. I don't think it works to say, 'There's this singularity in three years or five years or whatever, we can't see past it, and so we're going to do nothing, or we're just going to give up, or we're going to go crazy.' You have to live as if stuff is just going to keep going in an understandable way for a long time. How far ahead does OpenAI plan? I mean, we sign like 20-year power and land agreements. And for the product? I think we have a clear vision of what things can look like in two years, and then it gets much hazier after that. So, there was in the relatively recent past a narrative that GPT wrappers and companies of that ilk were—as the pejorative suggests—undifferentiated and flimsy, and would be swept away by a rising tide of model improvements. Whereas now, it feels like that narrative in some sense is flipping somewhat, where instead of talking about wrappers, we talk about harnesses, and harnesses are seen as having this significant heft and importance. I guess I'm curious for your view on this and how you view businesses for which AI is a critical enabling component and their prospective durability. I have kind of had the same view all the way through, which is that you as a business want to be on the side of hoping that AI gets smarter. In the early model days, if you were a GPT wrapper and you were patching some kind of weakness in the current model that was clearly going to get better with the next model, when the next model was much better, you were kind of sad. If you were doing something that got better—like making any of the wonderful services that people were building with the models that benefited from intelligence—you would be happier. I think the same thing applies to the world of harnesses. I kind of think the right way to think about this is like: data center, model, harness—that whole thing is just this one cluster out of which comes this very usable intelligence. There are so many things to go build where you're just happier for that whole cluster to get better and better and better. If you're secretly hoping it doesn't because you're patching some weakness in it, probably the next model crank turn somewhere in that stack is just going to solve it. When you look at the organizations that are making the most effective use of AI today—I mean, I presume you meet OpenAI customers constantly, large and small. If you think about the top three that have impressed you the most, or the one that's impressed you the most, what specifically are they doing that's different? Everyone here knows, yes, AI is a big deal and we should make enthusiastic use of it, but what specifically differentiates those which in your opinion are employing it most effectively? A few different directions there. A friend of both of ours, Tobi Lütke of Shopify, was the first CEO I knew who just said, 'We are going to be all in on AI in the way we run our company.' He got his hands dirty just building AI automation of everything and made his team do it. And he said, 'We're just going to figure out how we take all of these things that are bad and make them good with AI.' It was not like a token leaderboard, it was not some other kind of gamified, hackable thing; it was just the CEO of the company saying, 'We are now going to put AI into everything we do, and I'm not going to be happy with you, or we're not going to allow it, if you're not doing that.' So that energy has now been replicated by other people, but when the CEO of a company says, 'We're going to automate ourselves, accelerate ourselves,' however they phrase it internally, and then really holds people to it and ideally does it themselves, that works very well. I think we're going to try this new experiment where we start sending an FTE to work hands-on with whatever the CEO of a company needs, working with them to automate as much of their job as they can. And that, I think, will have a nice fractal effect throughout the company if you just do it for the leader of a company. So that works, and we'll try to help companies do that. A second thing is being uncomfortably permissive with data access. There are huge reasons not to do this, and I'm stopping short of a recommendation; you just asked what I've seen from the most effective companies. This is easier for small startups than companies that have a lot of sensitive data and a lot of process and compliance in place. But saying, 'You know what? We are going to record our meetings, we are going to let this AI have access to our codebase, and we are going to let it have access to every Slack message, every email, every everything, and every employee at the company is going to get to use it that way.' It is amazing watching these two or three-person startups work with AI doing everything. I don't know how the world is going to decide the trade-offs on data privacy versus AI efficiency. I think some regulation is going to have to change for that, but it's so powerful. Tempo is a new blockchain that Stripe incubated with Paradigm, which obviously we're partners with you guys on. The mainnet just launched, but the project launched back last summer. So, it's a relatively new and small team—a couple of dozen people. The Tempo team set up a harness, a tool in their Slack installation, for orchestrating pretty much everything at the company. Everything. You can just ask it any task: 'Go and read these Google Docs, turn those into a bunch of Linear tasks, then go write a pull request to implement them, then go deploy them and use our log analysis tool to test that the deployment actually worked.' And the agent will happily employ tool usage across all of this. It is extremely trippy watching a whole organization—I mean, a small organization, but an organization of people—do everything in a single Slack channel. I don't think that would scale to Stripe, but it was the first time I had the experience you're describing, which is clearly incredible for them. I don't quite see how to transpose it for us, but this is really something. It's really something to watch. And I find that a lot of people just can't—this is where there's a big overhang—they have not yet been able to wrap their heads around the fact that you can just ask it anything, and it'll probably happen. I myself still find myself not trusting quite enough that it's going to be possible. I don't know exactly how it's going to transpose to bigger companies. It does feel like we're missing one more abstraction there, like how humans and AIs are going to interface at massive scale. The advantage that these smaller companies have is that it's just the AIs; they don't have to figure out the interface with all the people. But we'll figure it out. Open-source AI—where is it going? Does it have a future? For sure. Right now, people clearly want smarter, faster, cheaper frontier intelligence, and most of the demand is there, but there is also a lot of demand for open-source, and I expect that to increase relatively over time. So, I want to talk a little bit about science, because I know it's something you're very excited about. But it's also relevant to something I spend some of my time on, which is the Arc Institute. OpenAI is, in fact, a foundation, a non-profit, and recently made a grant to the Arc Institute. Maybe we'll get to the details of that in a second, but first, do you just want to speak a little bit to AI as applied to science, what you're seeing, and just how you think about grant-making generally in the context of the OpenAI foundation? So, generally on the AI and science question, I hope that this will be the most important contribution of AI, of this technology, to human quality of life over time. We hope that we can start to discover new science at a much faster rate, which can lead to new materials, cures to diseases, or any number of other things. I believe that to a first-order approximation, life gets better because we understand science better, and then we figure out how to build stuff with it and distribute it to people. Starting with the models of a few months ago, but really now with 5.5, the models have gotten smart enough that excellent scientists are saying, 'I am able to figure out better ideas.' The models are able to make some small but important discoveries, and the pace of science is going to increase. Eventually we'll have, you know, automated labs and robots and, you know, building who knows what, and we'll be able to do science much faster. Но if we can start doing a decade of science of what it would have taken us in the old world in a year, the compounding effect there and what we'll be able to do and discover will just be extremely great. So, I think this is going to be incredible, and this will be one of the big areas of focus of the OpenAI foundation—basically providing money, expertise, and technology to accelerate science, and trusting that that will flow to the world in wonderful ways. And this is going to be a big foundation. Yeah, this will—I think it is one of the biggest, maybe it's the biggest, I think it will be the biggest foundation in the world. And yeah, so we're really focused on science and then AI resilience, like helping the world through this transition with this new technology in it. But you know, we were thrilled to get to support Arc. I think it's clearly the best sort of AI and bio effort, and if we can make even a small contribution with this technology and with the capital in the foundation to helping make people healthier, treating diseases, and this whole cluster of what we can do as we get better at understanding biology, we will be very thrilled. I thought that was going to take longer, and looking at the incredible work the Arc foundation is doing, I now think it maybe won't be that far off. So, you know, in a podcast, midway through, you might hear a little interstitial ad. This is your interstitial ad for the Arc Institute. There is an Arc Institute booth downstairs, and you might wonder what it's doing at the Internet Economy conference. The context here is that the Arc Institute is an organization we started four years ago, and its goal is to produce, hopefully, the first cure for a complex disease in humans. So a complex disease is one that involves some genetic factors and some environmental factors. You can think of most cancers, most autoimmune diseases, and most neurodegenerative diseases, for example, as being complex diseases in this specific sense. И humanity has never cured a complex disease. Not one. We've cured lots of infectious diseases, and we know how to screen for monogenic diseases—for that one genetic mutation that undergirds it—but we've never cured a complex condition. So, we started the Arc Institute with this as the goal. Alzheimer's is the first complex disease that we're targeting, and our hope is that with both new genome engineering technologies like CRISPR and then the amazing advances in AI, we'll be able to make some hopefully meaningful progress. It's only four years old, but the early results are very encouraging. The Arc Institute is currently looking for a CTO. We had one CTO do a sabbatical at Arc last year—his, last year or the year before, it was last year—and his name was Greg Brockman. He did some great stuff. He helped us train Evo2, which is the largest biology foundation model ever trained. I think it's so much better that you labeled that as the interstitial ad rather than just doing it. That was good. Okay, well, we haven't talked about Stripe. Honestly, because—he didn't team me up for this—you and John were two of the most... I had seen a lot of founders, and you and John were two of the most impressive founders of any age, but certainly of pimply-faced teenager age, that I had ever met. I clearly was right about that. Like, I kind of really believed that commerce was going to move online in a huge way and there were going to be lots of startups, and both of those suggested that this could be really big. But I don't know—I was a believer, and still am, that if you can find really smart founders and a market that's going to be big, you should just invest, and that's kind of it. Before that, just to check my memory, the thing you were building—the reason you realized you needed payments—was you had built this iPhone app to download all of Wikipedia because you were going somewhere crazy offline, and it was hard for you to take payments for it. And that was like—yeah, good, good memory, yeah, okay, yeah. I hadn't thought about that in a long time. I think my advice is more toward Stripe as a company itself going forward. Well, it's more like I want to—I now have, now that I'm thinking that we should be thinking more like a Stripe-style model, I have a bunch of questions.
