When everybody gets big, they're going to just build their own thing.' And it turns out that, yes, there are multiple payments providers, and yet in practice, if you make a great product, if you make a great thing, people are still going to need to take money in the future, and they're probably just going to stick with you if you're a good, reasonable ecosystem partner and don't do crazy things. I think that's going to keep working. I actually think every company clearly does need to get more efficient with AI, and they will do that. But this whole mindset—that every company is going to go away and everything is going to be completely different, and like maybe it'll be agents that need to handle payments between each other instead of consumers and merchants—but clearly, money's going to have to move somehow. My advice would be: adopt AI, especially internally, and use AI to build better products, but don't assume that the entire socioeconomic system completely reconfigures. I think the world has gotten a little bit delusional about this. You don't have to answer this, but if we index OpenAI headcount to 100 today, what do you think OpenAI headcount is in five years? I would love it to be 200. I think that—like, we can clearly get phenomenally more efficient than we are now with these tools. Probably we can't keep it at 2x, just because we need to—if we're going to have to go learn how to build data centers and robots and all these other kinds of things, it's just a lot of stuff to do. But we're maybe—I think we're a lot more efficient than the large tech companies per person, and I think we can turn that up even more over time. Apart from AI, as we look out over the next decade, what are the technologies and areas that excite you? Apart from AI at the model and product layer, I'm obsessed now with data center infrastructure. I think there's so much cool stuff to do there. I think there will be amazing new technologies at the physical layer. Energy and robots—that stack is probably what I think about the most. It does seem like the world is finally making a little progress on brain-machine interfaces. I'm excited about that. I am excited about—well, I'm both afraid of and excited about progress in biotech. But I'm certainly hopeful that that can get much better quickly, and I think defensive biotech is about to become very important, unfortunately. I'm excited about new kinds of computer interfaces. I think we are in an insane era right now where we're stuck with these old devices and old operating systems, and we have this magic new enabling technology. It feels like Codex is amazing at what it can do, but it feels very broken to be telling this thing to use my computer, and then it's clicking around. There's all this stuff that was made for a person, but doesn't really make sense for an AI to use. We can do so much better there. I think there's a whole new internet protocol to make too, so those things. When do you think we'll have the world's first profitable nuclear fusion reactor? Depends on how far electricity prices get pushed by data center demand. Maybe sooner than we thought. I'll guess in the next five years. That's a bold prediction. I hope so. Just going to hope we're on a roll here. Hypersonic commercial air travel? I don't follow it as closely. Hypersonic meaning like Mach 4? Sure. A good chunk of time—I don't know, more than ten years. Maybe a little less, but something like that. Are there any domains of science and technology that are not in the discourse in a significant way that you think will be accelerated a lot by AI and will have broad social consequence and impact? Yeah, the one that I think just does not get enough attention is materials science. It's not a cool thing, and I think people underestimate how much of the world is materials, how much of what we depend on, and how much progress AI can make. It's such a beautifully AI-shaped problem. Just getting new catalysts? Totally. I expect very rapid progress there, and that it will impact all of our lives in a very positive way. And it gets very little attention. Last question. It feels that in some sense, at least some version of AI is kind of inevitable. There's lots of people rushing towards it, building it out, and creating the data centers and all the rest. So there's this kind of sense of determinism. How do you hope that your specific involvement changes the trajectory for the world relative to some other counterfactual? I believe in democratization, personal agency, and access, and that everybody deserves a really great life. The most controversial decision we made in the history of OpenAI was what we now call iterative deployment. A lot of the thinking at the time that we released ChatGPT was that this was insanely dangerous to do—that you can't do this, and only this small set of people who have been thinking about AI safety can know what's coming. They thought it was an info hazard to tell the world, and that it's all too dangerous to ever release. They thought we had to keep this locked up, and that in our ivory tower, we would discover these wonderful things and share the fruits with the world, but we would have the AI and we would control it. And that sat very poorly with me. I thought then, and I believe now, that it is extremely important that we avoid that kind of power concentration, and that we build this for the world. The world gets to use it in lots of ways—some of which will be good, not all of which will be good. But by enabling people to explore this very wide opportunity space in front of us—messy at times though it will be, and obviously we'll put guardrails on it for reasonable safety—we will give the world a gift, but the world will build a much bigger gift on top of it for all of us. And if you don't enable people with this technology, and if you try to keep it locked up—which, again, now may sound obvious, but this was the sort of rough consensus plan of people working on it before we came along—I think that would have been really bad. I am a believer in entrepreneurship and innovation, and that people are mostly good and mostly do amazing things with tools. So, I think my single biggest contribution has been, and will be, pushing for this to be a democratized technology that people get to use and build on. Sam Altman, thank you very much. Thank you very much.

🎤 KEYNOTE: Welcome & Azure Infrastructure Vision

ITOpsTalk(11.3K) ·55:45 ·941 views · 2026-05-19
Rick Claus (Host, ITOpsTalk) Mark Russinovich (CTO, Deputy CISO, and Technical Fellow, Microsoft Azure)
At Microsoft’s inaugural Azure Infrastructure Summit, CTO and Deputy CISO Mark Russinovich outlined a paradigm shift in cloud operations, urging enterprises to adopt "durable" security controls, migrate toward serverless container architectures like ...
TL;DR At Microsoft’s inaugural Azure Infrastructure Summit, CTO and Deputy CISO Mark Russinovich outlined a paradigm shift in cloud operations, urging enterprises to adopt "durable" security controls, migrate toward serverless container architectures like Project Radius, and...

TL;DR

At Microsoft’s inaugural Azure Infrastructure Summit, CTO and Deputy CISO Mark Russinovich outlined a paradigm shift in cloud operations, urging enterprises to adopt "durable" security controls, migrate toward serverless container architectures like Project Radius, and prepare for AI-accelerated threats by eliminating static keys and hardening automated patching.

Security Governance & "Durable" Controls (SFI)

  • The Secure Future Initiative (SFI): Launched by Microsoft in response to highly targeted nation-state cyberattacks, SFI centralizes risk prioritization across all Microsoft products. It relies on a governance group of 18 Deputy CISOs to drive security improvements globally.
  • Opinionated Standardization: To reduce attack surfaces, Microsoft is enforcing company-wide standardization (e.g., migrating all services to the MSAL token-processing library) instead of allowing individual engineering teams to build bespoke systems.
  • Durable Security Mandate: Microsoft requires all security controls to be "durable," meaning they must programmatically fulfill four lifecycle stages:
    • Start Green: New workloads must be secure by default upon deployment.
    • Stay Green: Continuous policy enforcement prevents drift back to insecure states.
    • Get Green: Automated pathways must exist to quickly bring legacy ("brownfield") workloads into compliance.
    • Validate Green: Continuous, automated scanning measures and reports compliance without manual toil.
  • Eliminating Unbound Secrets: Because credential theft remains a top vector for threat actors, Microsoft is systematically transitioning from static passwords, SAS tokens, and API keys to hardware-bound Managed Identities (MSIs) that cannot be stolen and used on external devices.

Capacity Constraints & Multi-Region Resiliency

  • AI Demand Driving Physical Constraints: The exponential growth of generative AI workloads is testing the limits of physical land, utility power, and supply chain timelines, forcing Microsoft to pre-secure and build data center infrastructure years in advance.
  • The Physics of "Latency Envelopes": Because synchronous data replication (across 3 physical availability zones) is bound by the speed of light, an Azure region operates within a strict physical boundary.
  • The Multi-Region Imperative: Enterprises must design multi-region architectures for two reasons:
    • Capacity Allocation: Highly sought-after regions (e.g., Singapore) are physically capped, meaning expansion must occur across regional borders.
    • Disaster Recovery: High-impact weather or geopolitical events require workloads to be flexible enough to operate across multiple regions.
  • Sovereignty & Confidential Computing: For highly regulated workloads that cannot leave a sovereign region, Russinovich recommends segregating data and leveraging Confidential Computing (encryption at rest, in transit, and actively in use) to mitigate risk.

Cloud Modernization & Serverless (Project Radius)

  • The Shift from VMs to Serverless Containers: Microsoft is moving its internal services away from VM scale sets and complex self-managed Kubernetes clusters toward serverless options like Azure Container Instances (ACI) and Azure Container Apps.
  • Project Radius: An open-source, Cloud Native Compute Foundation (CNCF) incubation project developed by Microsoft to solve complex platform engineering challenges.
    • Decoupled Architecture: Separates application code definitions from underlying infrastructure.
    • Platform Recipes: Platform engineers write reusable "recipes" containing the company's compliance, VNet, security, and cost-control templates.
    • No-Contact Deployment: Application developers can deploy stateful workloads natively into environments using these pre-configured recipes without needing to coordinate directly with the infrastructure team.

AI-Driven Operational Intelligence (Brain & Project Triangle)

  • Graph-Driven Management: Microsoft maps its entire physical and logical cloud topology (from chillers and rack switches to VMs, containers, and tenants) into the Azure Resource Graph (ARG) to eliminate operational blind spots.
  • The "Brain" Engine: Azure’s automated diagnostic system monitors the ARG topology. In the event of an outage, Brain correlates failures across the graph to pinpoint the root cause, automatically notifying impacted customers in under 15 minutes.
  • Project Triangle: An AI-driven triage system designed to eliminate finger-pointing between on-call engineers (DRIs). Triangle deploys specialized AI agents to represent each service on an incident bridge. These agents analyze service telemetry in real time to quickly isolate the root cause of an outage and route it to the correct team.
  • Dynamic SLO Auto-Tuning: Rather than human engineers setting manual telemetry thresholds, Microsoft trains traditional ML models on open-telemetry schemas to automatically adjust and define normal service-level objectives (SLOs) dynamically by region and hardware type.

Key Action Items for Platform Engineers

  • Eradicate Unbound Keys: Actively phase out all standard passwords, connection strings, and unbound secrets in favor of system-assigned Managed Identities.
  • Adopt Defensive AI Testing: Deploy AI-driven pen-testing harnesses to aggressively probe your own environments for security gaps before malicious actors use similar AI models offensively.
  • Automate and Accelerate Patching: Optimize server and software patching workflows immediately. The rate of vulnerability discovery is expected to scale 10x to 100x over the next two years due to AI-automated threat generation.

Notable Quotes

"We entered a mode that I think it's non-optional to be opinionated about what teams do... every single bespoke system is another opportunity for the threat actors to get in." — Mark Russinovich
"Unless you have policies that enforce certain controls, you are going to have this constant catch-up... We established something called durability." — Mark Russinovich
"The cloud is fundamentally a graph-driven environment. If you don't understand those interconnections, you're blind." — Mark Russinovich

And so security, I know, is something that I remember from literally decades ago of stuff that you published before joining Microsoft that was very, very interesting. Yeah, well, so the Deputy CISO role was introduced about two and a half, three, maybe three years ago now. We started something called the Secure Future Initiative. This was in response to a couple of nation-states that breached Microsoft production systems and compromised customer data. And so internally, in my internal head, that's SFI. That's what... That's right. That's SFI. And so we decided that we needed support for risk prioritization to drive security improvements across the whole company in a concerted, unified way. And so Igor Tsyganski, the CISO that we'd just hired, literally when this stuff was going down, created this governance group of Deputy CISOs where we've got, I think it's 18 of them now. It keeps on growing as we get more specialized with the Deputy CISO roles. Now, the way that we've organized this particular structure of a conference, we've got build, operate, and optimize. I should get that right. And so, do any of those kind of trigger some ideas of some things that you've gone through and had to prioritize as being higher risk that we've gone ahead and resolved? Or do you have any recommendations for folks that can think about how they should be doing better deployments, how they should be doing better operations inside their space, based on what you've learned from the SFI initiative? Absolutely. So when it comes to security, you need to consider it at the time you're building and obviously at the time you're deploying it and operating. I mean, you have to think about it all through three phases. It's a full cycle. It's a full cycle. One of the things that we've done in each wave is we defined KPIs, which are our controls, basically, that are measured for improvements in security. One of the KPIs is that we don't use passwords, that we're using managed identities as opposed to keys and secrets. Right. And that is a control that we drive through our standardization across Microsoft. Obviously, a lot of this runs on Azure, almost all of it runs on Azure, and so most of the controls are provided by Azure that teams have to comply with. And we define these in waves. One of the, I think, interesting things that's important for anybody that is going through this journey of improving security in a very structured, organized way, as opposed to ad-hoc—which I think a lot of us have been in ad-hoc mode, which is "let's make best efforts," right—is what we've tried to do is really institutionalize this and make it part of just the foundation of the way that Microsoft operates across the whole company. And that requires a few things. One is having this unified KPI structure across the company. So, like, what are the things that everybody needs to do? And by the way, we don't want to do it 10 ways. Because 10 ways means 10 ways to have security problems. It also means lots of duplicative engineering work. So we really entered a mode where I think it's non-optional to be opinionated about what teams do. And I know there's a lot of enterprises out there that are like, "individual teams, you pick the technologies you want to use, you design your own standards, and we'll try to map our controls onto you." That is, I think, problematic when you get to scale because, like I said, it just becomes so costly, and every single bespoke system is another opportunity for the threat actors to get in. So we really focused on unifying all of Microsoft. So for example, just to give you—I mean, there's many, many examples—but for one example is the way that we handle tokens, both on the client side and the server side of a security negotiation, authentication negotiation. We found lots of vulnerabilities because we had so many bespoke token processing libraries that had proliferated around the company. One of the first things we did with SFI is said there's a company standard. It's called MS and MSAL, which are the public libraries that customers have access to as well for token processing from Entra. Everybody needs to adopt that. And so that is a KPI that we drove across the whole company, and it's part of SFI waves. So get rid of your custom stuff, replace it with these libraries. And when you replace them with these libraries, that means fewer chances for screw-ups that are unique to a particular service. We can make sure that everybody's operating in a secure way. And we've proceeded through waves of this kind of standardization across these core KPIs, starting with the KPIs that are the highest risk. So like I mentioned, my role as Deputy CISO is identifying the top risks for Azure engineering systems and the other areas that I oversee. And that means that we look at top risks and then we look at the controls that can mitigate those top risks, and then we define standards for implementations of those controls that we are very prescriptive and opinionated about so that we can do it once and get everybody to adopt it. MS and MSAL is just a great canonical example of us driving a standardization across the company. So I would say that is the key to being secure, especially now when we're going to be confronted with security threats at a scale and rate we've never seen before because of AI-driven cyber attacks that are getting more and more powerful every month. You don't want to get overloaded with going and trying to secure a very diverse platform or layers of defense that are very diverse, because that is just going to kill you. You need to be able to concentrate your resources and make sure that you're focusing on getting those systems that everybody depends on to be as secure as possible. One of the things, and this is I think a key takeaway that we had very early, is as we started to roll out these KPIs to get the whole company, every service in the company up to certain standards like MS and MSAL, we were throwing waves of KPIs. These are SFI waves, and we've talked about this publicly—you can go read reports about our progress on them. But what we found is that the teams started to get just completely overloaded with SFI work. Not just new work, but redoing the work they'd already done. So for example, let's say that you need to put all your resources behind a front door, or you need to make sure all your resources are using MSI as opposed to passwords and secrets and keys. If you go and secure your existing resources but then you're creating new ones that are in an insecure state, then you're going to have to go and re-secure those. They show up in the scans and they're like, "yeah, now you got a bunch of new work items to do." I have been subject to some of these designs that we've done as well. Yes. Having to switch over to managed identity and then having, oh, there's something for some reason still using a token, so you have to go back and put managed identity. Yeah, I've cleared them all up. But there are many cases—and this I think is the thing that at this kind of scale we didn't recognize right up front—unless you have policies that enforce certain controls, you are going to have this constant catch-up where a team can never get their head above water just going and doing the things that we wanted them to do just as the baselines. So they'll never have bandwidth to go and work on the next level of risks. We'll always be just dealing with the top risks. So we established something called durability. And durability consists of four ways to look at a control: starting green with the control, staying green with the control once you're green, getting green with the control if it's a brownfield service, and measuring green or validating green so that you can ensure that you're always compliant with the control. And what we've said for new KPIs that come in—we started this a few waves ago—is we just took a hard line and said, "KPI owners, if you're going to come in and propose a KPI, it needs to be durable." It needs to be automated across all four of those. Because otherwise, a team is going to end up having to do the work over and over again, or they're doing manual toil on measuring, manual toil on a new resource, like, "let me go secure that again." So every week they're getting red again. If we have durability, they're starting green, the control's in place, and they never have to worry about it again. If it's brownfield and we have an automated get-green, then we apply the get-green and they never have to worry about it again. So that is the philosophy behind durability that we found we just cannot make progress without. So we got very, very strict a couple waves ago of saying new KPIs must be durable, with the ability for the Deputy CISO to provide a time-bound exception which would only be applied if there's enough risk here that we're going to pay the toil price before this control is durable, and eventually it'll be durable and then we will not have to go repeat work again, but we want to make progress now. So I think that is one of the key learnings, and I did a blog post on durability. But for anybody that is looking at provisioning resources on-prem or in the cloud, you should think about durable security controls from the start. I like how those four criteria that you came up with apply, as you mentioned, to new workloads that are in the process, as well as existing ones that have been discovered and then have to be fixed. That applies obviously to virtually almost all the workloads that happen to be and, as you call out, even hybrid in nature, not just exclusively inside the Azure space too, for how people should be looking at that. Just because I haven't looked into it, I've been on the receiving side of those types of emails internally for our internal processes. Are you aware of different ways that we can actually suggest or recommend that customers can set up notifications or scans of their own systems or other things that can identify these different issues? For instance, if they decided, "okay, SAS tokens are no longer what we're going to be using, we've got to use something else like managed identity," is there something that can scan, look for that, and have a policy in place to tell them that they're in violation of the recommendation? Yeah, I mean, the Microsoft Sentinel services, you know, will tell you. We also have baseline security controls, so you can go look and see when you're not compliant with a baseline security control. One of the things that we have focused on is just a principle in just about everything we do in Azure: if it applies to us, we want to make it available to our customers. So when we see a gap in enforcing, you know, doing the "stay green" on something, it's like, well, there's an Azure policy that we need to have for that. In some cases we had Azure policies that would only measure or only validate green, but not enforce it. So we've gone through and cleaned up those cases across what we call "baseline secure mode," which is the set of controls that everybody should just be applying. Those are all durable with Azure policy and other mechanisms. Ideally it's Azure policy, because Azure policy is so powerful—powerful in the way that you can target, and powerful in the way that you can provide exceptions. And we're making it even more powerful internally and going to make those Azure policy capabilities available to our customers as well. Very neat. The policy piece makes total sense for being able to go off and have that applied and be informed what happens, what needs to be fixed on that particular space. Do you have any examples of stuff that we've identified with S360 that we've gone in and potentially changed the default behavior of the services—not necessarily the design of someone going off and deploying it, but if I'm going to go off and deploy a storage backend, or if I'm going to go off and use Azure Front Door, or if I'm going to set up a network with virtual subnets to be able to connect up my different systems, have we gone in, learned, and then applied new defaults to these different services to make sure we're giving our customers a leg up on doing this? Sure. First, it's hard to change a default because you can end up breaking automation. Hundreds of thousands of customers all of a sudden will come at you. "What's going on, Mr. Russinovich?" That's right. And we get yelled at any time we break a default or break an API. I think that you've seen over the last few years us break defaults a few times when it's putting customers at risk to not have them be the default. But we try to avoid it. And even in the case of, "hey, this will make you more secure, like using managed identity rather than a key," that is an opt-in. But one of the things that we've done is made it so that we've gone through the core set of services and made sure that they all support MSI. And there were some services that didn't have MSI capability for authentication that used keys. So those now all support MSI. And the thing we've also done is made it so you can set policy to make it so that the resource can't be deployed without MSI enforced. So you cannot create a key for it. So net-new ones using those services, all of a sudden that doesn't work as far as a key is concerned, and you would know that early in the stage to be able to resolve it. And that's an example, by the way, of the Deputy CISO process because when looking at the Red Team operations, threat intelligence, and our own scanning of our services for vulnerabilities, credential theft is one of my top risks for Azure. And the only way you can steal a credential is if it's an unbound secret—an unbound secret like a password, or an unbound secret like a key. And so we've gone through the platform and are eliminating any need to have one of those. The way that you get rid of an unbound secret is to use managed identity because then it's managed on your behalf. We're even going to bound managed identities too, where you can never leak the thing, in the sense that you can't grab a token, take it somewhere else, and then use it. Because it's bound to the device or this virtual machine that it's on. So that is kind of the maturation of preventing credential theft and mitigating that top risk that we see in Azure where a threat actor comes in and they're like, "oh, on this box, look, here's a key, let me go take it and use it somewhere else." The way to prevent that is to bind it. But that's an example of where we've gone through our services, made sure that they support these best practices, and then have policies to enforce it. Then, as part of SFI waves, we've gone and brought any of the service resources that we had that weren't compliant up to compliance level. I'm going to shift a little bit away from the security piece, but maybe it potentially still is related. We had a question that came up because I asked on socials about having the ability to ask Mark questions during this, and one of them was talking about something that comes up almost all the time when I'm having conversations with people, which is capacity. And what's new on that front as far as capacity is concerned? Are you going to turn off your gazillion cores that you're using for your services, or... No. No. Those are mine. I love them. So what are we doing on the Azure platform side of things? Any new stuff inside the capacity discussion that we can have that you can share? Well, I can share a little bit about the strategy and how we're trying to address this. I mean, the reason that we've got a capacity shortage, there's a few factors, but a big one has been just AI demand—just the explosion of AI demand. Which has meant that we're building out data centers as fast as we can. In some cases, we're running out of space in a region, and so we're building new data centers, standing up new regions to support capacity expansion. We've been optimizing every part of our supply chain, from how we pre-provision or pre-secure capacity upfront—so we're buying land for data centers and provisioning utility power—and we're securing our supply chain for server parts and everything years in advance so that we're not going to hit an issue where we've got a hiccup. But the other thing that we're doing is trying to help our customers with being mindful about the physics of a region. I mean, an Azure region is defined as a latency envelope, and it is defined as a latency envelope so you can create an application that works reliably no matter what Azure region it's in. I remember way back—Azure's been around 16 years now, 15 years now, I think it's 2008. Since 2008, yeah. 2008. And I remember watching as a person outside Microsoft, and then inside Microsoft, just the number of regions growing and growing and growing. It's kind of been stalled at the 60 mark for forever for what we were talking about. I know that we're up above 60 now. I think we're now publicly talking about being at 80 at this point in time, which is just crazy, with more coming online. I love that you're talking about a latency perspective, and so where does that come into play? Because I think of fault domains internal to the region and internal to the data center specifically, but then I also think of zones as well. But now you're talking about, from a region perspective, latency having everything inside that one particular region for fast access. So the regions and the zones are tied together as far as the latency envelope goes because if you want data to be durable, highly reliable, and highly resilient, data needs to be replicated three times. So this is where you see cloud providers like us and others say when we go in a region, there are three zones, and that is so you can get stateful storage. So if one zone goes down, you still can write data with two durable copies. Your chance of losing data is pretty low at that point. Now, the only way to synchronously replicate data—which is what you want in many cases, as you don't want to have to deal with asynchronous replication where if something fails, now you don't know what state your system is in—is to have it within a certain latency envelope. And that is what defines the regional latency boundary, which translates into a physical boundary. And so if we're creating a region and there's a limited amount of land and power in that region, we're constrained just through the physics of that region and can't grow beyond a certain size. Now, we're talking about monster region sizes at this point. Nevertheless, there is a boundary to them, and there's some places in the world where the regions are even more constrained—like take Singapore, for example. There's very limited space there for a hyperscale cloud region, and so you've got to think about this from two perspectives. There are already regions that have been running hot and where capacity is very constrained just because they've grown and they're filling. Now, wherever we can, we're building new data centers, and this is part of our plan to accelerate growth, especially in the light of AI driving a lot of extra usage. But everybody needs to be thinking about a multi-region strategy. And a multi-region strategy is for two reasons. One is capacity, because at some point, just physics will say a region is going to run out of capacity and I can't deploy more in that region. There's only so many gigawatts you can fit inside the space, only so much physical space you can actually have the containers of the different compute power inside of those different regions as well. It's just insane. And the second reason, of course, is resiliency. You don't want to... if there's a problem with the region—and a region has at least three data centers for three zones, though many of the regions have many, many more, of course. But if something happened to that region—say a massive weather event, a massive geo event like an earthquake—we try to place the zones far enough apart to have a low risk of that, but it's just sometimes impossible in particular parts of the world, in particular geographies. You've got to consider that something might happen to the whole region. If not running out of capacity where you can't deploy more in that region, something might happen from an availability perspective. So it's just at the point where people need to be really thinking about cloud architectures that are multi-region, and having at least two regions of operation and being flexible enough to go to three or more in the future. So if they need the resilience, they shouldn't just be thinking of resilience inside the single region inside of zonal stuff, but then actually looking at it from literally virtual differences in countries, potentially. Because, as you mentioned, some countries only have one region and you've got to go outside. And, you know, as much as we wished we could create the trillion-gigawatt region where you never have to worry and that's all within that latency envelope... but we can't. Whatever happened to those undersea data centers that we did some research with on Nautilus and stuff like that? You know, maybe bring those back online again, who knows. Or data centers in space, which is the hot... So how do you address the customer's question or the architect's question when you're sitting, I don't know, having a coffee with another person like that, how do you address the question about, "okay, well, what about data sovereignty?" Like, "I've got stuff that needs to stay inside the country, and you're telling me that for resiliency I need to potentially look, for this particular workload, because I've deemed it needs to have a higher level of resiliency, to go outside of the country into a different region." Without getting into too many details, what would be the design process that someone would do for being able to secure that? So there's a few things. I mean, obviously, regulation is just a hard constraint. You have to adhere to the regulations your industry is telling you you have to adhere to. And in some cases, you have no choice but to stay within a sovereign boundary, which might only have one region. Ideally, it's not capped. I mean, if it is capped at that point and you need to go out of the country, of course that's a discussion with your regulators on how to best secure it. Now, we've got a few things like confidential computing, which you and I have talked a lot about. I think it will be turned to more and more as the reality of constrained regions—hyperscale cloud regions—starts to hit. Not just that, but if you take a look at the geopolitical events in the world, it's actually beneficial from a risk perspective to be able to move around regions. But what I would say is segregate your applications and your data to, "this is the regulated stuff that needs to be in-region," and "here's the stuff that can be out and move around." And then, if you need to, if you haven't already, brush up on confidential computing to be able to have encryption at rest, encryption in motion, and also encryption while it's actually running. Confidential computing is your friend; you've got to dig up and understand how that works for your particular workloads. Definitely cool. Inside the CTO office? On the CTO side? So, actually, I've got a few teams in the office of the CTO—four teams in the office of the CTO, in fact. I won't go through them all here, except for the one that I think is most relevant to cloud-native computing. Because I think, and let's just start with that, a lot of customers... there are customers that are still in lift-and-shift, just "get my VMs in the cloud," you know, traditional IT stuff. But, you know, you and I have been talking for over a decade about modernization, right? Cloud modernization. Cloud modernization 10 years ago was synonymous with containerization, synonymous with Kubernetes. And different levels of skills of people having to learn how to manage that from their backgrounds of, "you will take my VM out of my cold, dead hands" kind of thing, right? So that is where people should be, or even thinking about beyond that. Beyond it is serverless, which, you know, started with serverless short-running workloads. But now we consider serverless to mean anything where there's a container but you're not worried about the infrastructure—we're worried about it. And so we, through Azure Container Instances and Azure Container Apps, support containerized workloads in a serverless way where you're not worried about the Kubernetes cluster, you're not worried about the underlying infrastructure; you're worried just about the workload. And this is the future. Like, if you think about a lot of platform engineering teams today, they're very focused on, "let's get the Kubernetes cluster up," and "we're the ones doing the templates for the YAML files that describe the canonical application patterns that we want our development teams to fit into." They are looking after utilization, spinning out more compute nodes if they need to, bringing them back in again, and all that sort of stuff. Yeah, but what we want to do is take those burdens away, just like the vision of serverless has always been: let us worry about it. Like, you can just focus on the app, and we'll make it resilient and secure and scalable, and you focus on the other aspects of just meeting your requirements for your particular workload. Now, to that end, there's been a few things that we've been doing in the Azure incubation team to support that vision, and within Azure Core. Of course, I mentioned ACI. ACI (Azure Container Instances) is the primitive. You can think of them as the equivalent of a virtual machine and a virtual machine scale set because in ACI, we've now got container groups, which you can think of as VM scale sets. We're literally going back to the time when I was a PM in the Azure engineering team. I sat next to Sean, I believe, who was doing ACI stuff at the very beginning back in the day. Yeah, ACI started as something that we did Azure Cloud Shell on. But so Azure Container Instances literally is becoming the foundation for Azure. Internally, our services are all moving to it. This is the direction that we're moving everything: get serverless, get onto Azure Container Instances. There's so many benefits, from not having to manage the infrastructure so you can focus on the application, to being able to get very secure with confidential computing—another topic we've discussed, but it's best for a separate deep dive. But the future for Azure is Azure Container Instances and serverless—like I said, web apps serverless, Azure Container Apps serverless, Azure Container Instances serverless. So everybody should be thinking about, "how do I go serverless?" And by the way, a way to preserve... if you've got a Kubernetes deployment and you're managing your apps through Kubernetes using AKS, you can use ACI with virtual nodes, too. Virtual nodes let you basically have serverless pods on top of ACI. So your cluster becomes just a flexible pool rather than these specific servers. And so that's an easy way to kind of integrate it into an existing Kubernetes-based system and not be totally serverless yet, but you don't need Kubernetes to be using an ACI app. So that's one thing about just thinking about modernizing your apps into containers and then taking advantage of serverless containers through ACI—whether through virtual nodes or just natively with Azure Container Groups—as another way to take advantage of serverless containers. But where we want to go in the incubation team, which is kind of connecting your question of what we're doing, is that we want to make it really easy for platform engineering teams. And I know there's probably a lot of platform engineering team members here listening to this because you're typically the ones asking, "what's Azure doing, because I need to worry about it so that my app developers don't have to? I can provide the platform for my app developers." "I've gotta get ahead of it." "I've gotta get ahead of it." And what we want to do is make it so that your app developers and architects have the ability to define their applications, and provide you, the platform engineer, the controls that you implement for them so they don't have to worry about it. So for example, let's say that you want everybody to use virtual networks. Now, you don't want your application engineers to worry about virtual networks; you go deploy the virtual networks, and then you have them land the application in the virtual network. So you can think of Radius as the evolution of ARM, and everybody here watching probably is familiar with ARM, ARM Bicep, and ARM JSON. In ARM, if you think about the way that you describe a cloud application or service, there's no separation between the app and the infrastructure. You're basically down to the level of, "okay, here's the virtual network, here's exactly how it is configured"—very much in the minutiae to make sure that everything is just in one blob. What Project Radius does—which is one of the incubations in the incubation team that I've got—is separate the app from the platform and from the infrastructure. What the platform engineering team does is create environments for an app. You can think of an environment as a landing zone. And the landing zone here, the environment, is defined through what we call recipes. So the application developer is describing their app. They're saying, "here's a frontend; it's a containerized, scale-out frontend. Here is the middle tier; this is a scaled-out stateful tier that has SQL databases it's attached to, for example." And they describe the app that way. The platform engineering team defines an environment for the app based on its production requirements. They can have a dev-test environment, they can have a production-standard integration environment, or they can have a production environment. In the production environment, you'd say, "this thing needs a virtual network, this thing needs encryption at rest, this thing needs all these security controls we talked about earlier." And they define them as recipes. When the frontend containers or the load balancers get deployed, here's how they're configured, and you define that in the recipe. It's the prescriptive guidance already done. It's inside the Radius. That's right, you codified it in the recipe. And these recipes let the platform engineering team be specific about the company policies that they want enforced on this thing, as well as the resiliency policies they want enforced. And the developers don't need to worry about it. In fact, one of the principles we had designing Radius was that the application team never even needs to talk to the platform engineering team. "You've been trying to get people to talk together forever, and now you're telling me you want them to be separate? Come on!" Well, it's not bad if they talk. But one of the benefits of that principle is that the platform engineering team can enforce the company's controls—whether it's cost-saving controls, security controls, or compliance controls—without having to go talk and tell the app developer to do things, or modify and change designs because of X or because of this or whatever it happens to be. It's a policy. Now it's simply, "here's the recipe." That's right. "Deploy to it." "Deploy to it, yep, in that environment." And so this is a Cloud Native Computing Foundation (CNCF) project. So it's open source and has open governance. Everybody who is interested can go deploy it and play with it themselves. We have a bunch of customers we've been working with, including Millennium BCP, which is the largest bank in Portugal, who is in production with it. Oh, okay. And they found these concepts very powerful. You can also... I could go on about Radius, but it's here now, and people are using it in production. I know it's probably one of those things that, because it's not yet at massive scale and there's no managed service for it, is only for companies that are at the forefront of looking at how to take advantage of these things. But it's where we're going. Nice. I would be remiss if I did not bring up a topic that is kind of outside but still relevant, obviously, from Azure as a platform, and that's going to be this overarching theme of AI. I had to say it. Overarching theme of AI. Where do you see this coming into play from an AI perspective? Everywhere. It's affecting the design of what we're doing for our data centers, it's affecting the design of how we're developing apps, and it's affecting the whole process of how we're developing apps. And, you know, I'm a fanboy of the podcast that you and Scott Hanselman do, where you've been sharing the different experiences that you've been having working with AI as a coding solution, as an assistant, and also as independent agents and stuff like that. How does this apply to potentially the operations side, or even just in general as a technologist working with technology and working with the Azure platform? God, it's just a huge topic, and it spans everything. What's the most top-of-mind for you? I'll narrow it down a little bit to what you think is most top-of-mind. Well, from an operations perspective, I've talked about this before in my Ignite talks: the cloud is fundamentally a graph-driven environment. I think this is a realization I had over the last few years, which is that the only way to really manage your infrastructure is to understand its graph. Everything is interconnected. And if you don't understand those interconnections, you're blind. You've got gaps, for sure. You've got gaps from an inventory perspective, you've got gaps from an operational perspective, and you've got gaps from a security perspective. The only way to close those gaps is to have your assets managed as a graph. We've been, over the last six years, moving towards having all of Azure at its core managed with a graph called Azure Resource Graph (ARG), which is a public service too. Everything in Azure is in the graph because everything in Azure is modeled through ARM, so it ends up in the graph. And then we're bringing things that are not managed through ARM in Azure into the graph so that we understand the full topology of the system. This includes things like our data center infrastructure, like what is a rack, what is a chiller, or what is a network switch. If those are in the graph, then we can understand the relationship between those: the servers, the virtual machines on those servers, the containers inside those virtual machines, the services that run on the containers in the virtual machine, and which customer is running in this particular container on this particular service. You get the idea—by traversing graphs, we can do that. So we can see, for example, using a system called Brain—which I've talked about in blog posts and probably interviews with you, because we've been working on this for a while. Brain is the operational brain of Azure. When there's an incident, when something goes red, Brain looks at it using the graph and understands, "hey, this thing is red, that thing is red, which means that this other thing is probably the cause." And it's, you know, much, much more sophisticated than what I just described, but fundamentally that's what you want. Like, when things start to fail, where is the root cause? And can we automatically fix the root cause if we can, or automatically tell people and notify the customers? So if you take a look at our onboarding of all of our system services to Brain, we've been driving down the time to notify a customer of an issue down to under 15 minutes, because it's just automated. Like, there's an issue here, it's because of this, and we can pinpoint it for our developers and for our DRIs. Where is AI involved in this? Well, one area is called Project Triangle. So if there's some ambiguity about what's responsible—say there's a bunch of resources that have gone red—traditionally, what we would do is fire an ICM (an incident management ticket). DRIs of every one of those impacted services get brought to the bridge, and then they all start looking at their own logs and trying to figure out, "oh, is it me or is it you?" Everyone starts pointing the finger at each other, and that just burns huge amounts of time. So what we've got is a system called Triangle that I talked about in my reliability blog post from just shortly ago. It's an AI-driven system where we've got AI personas representing each service that are grounded in the history of that service, the kind of failures that service has, and possess the skills to look at telemetry to understand what might be going on. When there's something that happens, Brain fires off and talks to the Triangle system, brings in the relevant agents from each service, and says, "is it yours? Is it yours?" And they look and say, "it doesn't look like it's mine." And what we're finding is that Triangle gets through a lot of the ambiguity about what's responsible and which service is causing it, and then can route it to the right team so that they can jump on it and address it, if it's not an auto-mitigated issue. So that is one example. I mean, there's traditional ML all over the place there, and then there's AI in this case that we've been experimenting with that actually has been bringing down the time to mitigate by minimizing the number of hops. Because the other thing is like, "it's your service," and then you look at it and say, "nope, it's actually not; it's theirs." Handing off the responsibility. Yeah, the handoffs—that just causes the time to mitigate to go way up. So it's drastically reducing the time to mitigate and time to respond. That's one cool example of generative AI right in the middle of Azure operations. And that part is all in the behind-the-scenes stuff, as you mentioned, because it's the Brain service and then Project Triangle. I know that that ties into a public side of things from Azure Service Health and the Azure Service Health notifications. I did a session with Tajinder, I think it was, that was talking about making sure you had that configured correctly because most of our customers don't even have proper health monitoring agents correctly identified to fire off notification emails, and they were relying on other methods. So it's very cool to see the AI step in on the backend to be able to make that more efficient for helping out with outages and incidents where they happen to be. What about from a development perspective or things that you've seen? Because that's one thing that I've noticed and I'm interested in your perspective on. From you developing stuff, your teams working on things, and having different agents going off and doing different things, the speed of how that's all happening from the AI space is incredible. Is there anything that comes up with regards to making them more potentially efficient with using Azure infrastructure as their choice for where to deploy? Or helping them not make stupid mistakes because they just go the fast route? Do you have any advice for folks that are starting to look at this from an operations side? Well, AI is being applied all over the place here. One of them is just AI helping you design your Bicep templates, for example. And when you talk about being able to operate your systems, define your systems, and understand your systems, you're going to see AI just automatically doing this stuff—like automated Red Teaming of your systems. That's something that we're doing at Microsoft. There've been some interesting announcements from various AI companies on the capability—the cyber capabilities—of their models, which means you need to be taking a look at how you can apply this defensively before the bad actors are applying it offensively against your infrastructure. And by the way, going back to ARG and the graph, guess how our own Ghost team—which is the team that hunts threat actors in our environment—tracks where they're likely going and where they have been? That is through Azure Resource Graph again. They've driven a lot of requests like, "hey, we need to see this in the graph, we need to see that in the graph," so that they can understand exactly what's going on. So, again, tying back to graph-driven infrastructure, you need to be graph-driven, especially in this world of AI. But besides the application of AI from an infrastructure and ops perspective, there is traditional ML, like I mentioned. One of the things that traditional ML does is highly relevant for a lot of customers, because Brain is using a lot of traditional ML to go identify when there are anomalies. And I think if I was to give some guidance around durable controls for your security, anyone and everyone needs to be thinking about durability if they're at any kind of scale. The other one that they need to be thinking about is, "how do I consistently measure service availability?" Because this can't be a team-by-team "stick my finger in the air, yeah, it seems healthy" approach. You need to have some rigor about it. We defined this close to seven years ago. We said the way to look at the health of a service—one of the primary ways—is: is it healthy from a customer's perspective? So everybody's heard of SLIs and SLOs. What we decided is that the way to define health is through service level objectives. What does it mean for this service to be healthy? How many requests per second should it be honoring? What is a tolerable failure threshold? And then we took the SLOs and we defined an SLI schema that we've instrumented all of our services for. So going back and talking about Brain, every service uses OpenTelemetry to emit metrics in a very consistent way that is defined by this schema that we have checked in for every service. And these are metrics like the number of read operations per second on a particular API, the number of write operations, the throughput of those, failure rates, and the list goes on. All of that goes into training ML models for what a service looks like under normal operation versus what it looks like when it's not. It can be automatically adjusted based on what region it's in, other characteristics of what hardware it's on, and so on. And then Brain automatically will fire events based on missed SLOs. For these Brain-determined SLOs, we don't want humans to have to go define this stuff; it's defined by the system itself under software, using hard numbers and performance. So it's kind of an auto-tuning system. And this is something—both Brain and the SLI/SLO system—that we plan on making available to our customers as well. Everyone should be thinking, "how do I standardize this stuff across my entire estate?" because diversity just kills you. I love that. Joe Rogan Experience #2501 - Marc Andreessen

PowerfulJRE(20.9M) ·200:21 ·109.6K views · 2026-05-19
Joe Rogan (Host, Joe Rogan Experience) Marc Andreessen (Co-founder and General Partner, Andreessen Horowitz)
Venture capitalist Marc Andreessen highlights how political hurdles, regulatory dysfunction, and rising taxes are driving talent and safety tech out of major U.S.
TL;DR Venture capitalist Marc Andreessen highlights how political hurdles, regulatory dysfunction, and rising taxes are driving talent and safety tech out of major U.S. cities, while a parallel technological boom in AI and micro-nuclear energy promises to democratize...

TL;DR

Venture capitalist Marc Andreessen highlights how political hurdles, regulatory dysfunction, and rising taxes are driving talent and safety tech out of major U.S. cities, while a parallel technological boom in AI and micro-nuclear energy promises to democratize world-class expertise at an unprecedented, rapid scale.

Urban Safety, Surveillance Backlash, and Crime Statistics

  • Flock Camera System: An AI-powered municipal camera network that tracks cars in real time using license plates and unique vehicular markings.
      • In Austin, Texas, local politicians disabled Flock due to privacy and surveillance concerns, which severely delayed the apprehension of two teenage mass shooters (aged 15 and 17) who had struck 10 different locations.
      • The suspects were only caught after driving into an adjacent municipality where the Flock system was still active.
  • ShotSpotter in Chicago: Precision rooftop microphones designed to instantly triangulate and locate gunshots.
      • Chicago voluntarily turned off ShotSpotter due to progressive activist campaigns claiming the technology disproportionately targeted disadvantaged communities and was racially biased.
      • Andreessen argues this decision leaves gunshot victims to bleed out in the street without immediate emergency response, directly harming the very demographics activists claim to protect.
  • Data Manipulation and Underreporting: Official urban crime statistics are widely misleading due to systemic data issues.
      • In cities like San Francisco and Los Angeles, citizens have stopped reporting property crimes because police response is non-existent or delayed by hours.
      • Senior officials in the Washington D.C. Police Department were recently caught and indicted for deliberately faking crime numbers to show a false downward trend.

Economic Polarization, Wealth Taxes, and the Demotivation of Labor

  • The Ken Griffin Target: New York City's mayor targeted billionaire philanthropist and major employer Ken Griffin in a public video, prompting Griffin to consider shifting a $6 billion project and his Citadel business operations to Florida.
  • California Asset Tax Proposal: A progressive-backed California ballot proposition introduces a 5% tax on unrealized assets (excluding real estate but including stocks and bonds) for high-net-worth individuals.
      • The Founder Loophole: The tax is calculated on the *greater* of a founder's economic interest or voting control interest (such as dual-class super-voting stock), meaning tech founders of companies like Google could face instant bankruptcy.
      • Political Flight: While Governor Gavin Newsom publicly opposes a state-level asset tax because wealthy residents will simply flee to Texas, Florida, or Nevada, he reportedly supports introducing it at the federal level to prevent domestic tax flight.
  • Equality of Outcome vs. Effort: Andreessen contrasts the two moral definitions of fairness.
      • He warns that socialist equality of outcome kills basic human motivation, quoting a Soviet-era factory worker: *"We pretended to work, and they pretended to pay us."*
      • In European countries like Estonia, high marginal tax rates incentivize educated workers to take lower-paying retail jobs (e.g., shoe sales) to avoid punitive tax brackets, stifling ambition.
      • Per capita income in multiple fully developed European nations now ranks lower than Mississippi, the 50th ranked U.S. state.

Energy Realities and the Nuclear Microreactor Solution

  • Historical Anti-Nuclear Fallacies: Public panic surrounding Three Mile Island and Fukushima halted nuclear development for 50 years, forcing a heavy, long-term reliance on fossil fuels.
      • Epidemiological studies show zero acute radiation deaths or proven long-term cancer increases resulted from Three Mile Island, yet regulatory systems continue to block new plants.
  • The Renewables Irony: Germany's aggressive shutdown of its nuclear plants has forced the country to rely on highly volatile wind and solar energy, requiring carbon-heavy coal plants to serve as constant backup generators.
  • Data Centers & Microreactors: The rapid expansion of AI data centers requires an immense, continuous energy supply.
      • Venture capitalists and tech builders advocate for placing private nuclear microreactors directly next to data centers to bypass grid constraints.
      • The left has increasingly aligned against AI as it merges with energy demands, while a "horseshoe" alignment on the far-right (e.g., Tucker Carlson) and far-left (e.g., Bernie Sanders) has formed a joint anti-AI/anti-tech front.

The AI Revolution: AGI, "AI Vampires," and Human Superpowers

  • Crossing AGI: Andreessen believes the industry crossed the threshold of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) roughly late 2025/early 2026 with the release of GPT-5.5, Claude 4.6, Gemini 3.0, and Grok 4.3.
      • In 99% of professional use cases, these advanced models provide analysis superior to that of leading human experts.
      • The Turing Test—long considered the ultimate benchmark for AI—was easily bypassed during the launch of ChatGPT in late 2022.
  • The "AI Vampire" Phenomenon: Rather than reducing work hours, AI tools have turned top software programmers into sleep-deprived "vampires."
      • Programmers are 20 times more productive using AI coding agents (such as Claude Code or Cursor).
      • Because the opportunity cost of sleeping is so high, developers stay up 24/7 managing up to 20 parallel bots to execute complex coding pipelines.
  • Democratized Superpowers: AI is acting as a "universal basic superpower," giving global citizens free access to world-class medical, legal, and educational coaching.
      • Consumers are building highly complex, automated health dashboards by feeding personal DNA data ($200 genome sequencing), continuous Apple Watch data, and blood panels directly into AI engines.
      • Multimodal capabilities allow users to receive real-time, personalized feedback on sports performance (like Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu sparring) captured via home webcams.
  • Voter Priority Trends: A recent poll by Democratic pollster David Shor ranks artificial intelligence 29th out of 39 key voter concerns, while cultural "woke" issues have dropped to the absolute bottom as inflation, cost of living, and the economy dominate public anxiety.

Post-Show Epilogue: Joe Rogan’s Public Apology to Theo Von

  • The Context of the Apology: Rogan addresses a prior podcast episode with musician Marcus King where he ham-handedly referenced comedian Theo Von's struggles with mental health and antidepressants.
  • Misunderstood Stage Comments: Rogan explains that a viral clip of Von saying, *"I'm just trying not to end my own life,"* was actually a comedic response to an audience member asking him to film a suicide awareness video, not an active cry for help.
  • Underlying Anxiety Over Friends: Rogan admits his clumsy on-air interventions stem from deep-seated trauma and guilt over losing close friends—such as *NewsRadio* writer Drake, chef Anthony Bourdain, and comedian Brody Stevens—to suicide without warning.

Notable Quotes

"Imagine a form of alchemy that turns sand into thought... Chips are made out of sand. They're made out of silicon... We plug the chip into a data center, into power, we light it up, and we put AI on it, and all of a sudden it's thinking." — Marc Andreessen
"If everybody is guaranteed equal outcomes, if you're getting the thing regardless, then you kill motivation. And motivation is everything for people achieving things." — Joe Rogan
"There is a new term of art used in the Valley called the 'AI vampire.' You're up all night doing AI coding because you are so productive, you're getting so much done that you can't turn off. The opportunity cost of going to sleep is too high." — Marc Andreessen
