Former Secretary Of State Antony Blinken, Andy Kim Discuss The Future Of American Foreign Policy

Forbes Breaking News(5.6M) ·29:39 ·452 views · 2026-05-19
Antony Blinken (Former Secretary of State) Andy Kim (US Senator (D-NJ)) Linda Thomas-Greenfield (Former Ambassador)
To successfully counter China's rising global influence and navigate a rapidly shifting world order, the United States must rebuild its domestic and international credibility by prioritizing diplomatic reliability and forming flexible, issue-specific...
TL;DR To successfully counter China's rising global influence and navigate a rapidly shifting world order, the United States must rebuild its domestic and international credibility by prioritizing diplomatic reliability and forming flexible, issue-specific multilateral...

TL;DR

To successfully counter China's rising global influence and navigate a rapidly shifting world order, the United States must rebuild its domestic and international credibility by prioritizing diplomatic reliability and forming flexible, issue-specific multilateral alliances rather than relying on unilateral actions.

Strategic Competition and the Global Convergence with China

  • The Challenge of One-on-One Competition: Former Secretary of State Antony Blinken warns that competing with China individually is a losing proposition for the United States.
      • China's Structural Advantages: China possesses a manufacturing capacity three times that of the US, a larger navy, greater purchasing power parity, and higher rates of patent filings.
      • The Alliance Multiplier: By aligning with key global partners—including Europe, Japan, South Korea, India, Australia, and Canada—the US and its allies represent 50% to 60% of global GDP, compared to just 25% for the US alone.
  • The Trust Deficit: Recent polling across 130 countries reveals that China is increasingly viewed as a more credible international actor than the United States.
      • In one European poll, 70% of respondents in Germany characterized the US as an adversary.
      • Former Ambassador Linda Thomas-Greenfield notes that African nations often view China as a more trustworthy and reliable partner than the US.
  • The Need for Reliability: Senator Andy Kim (D-NJ) emphasizes that foreign leaders frequently question whether America remains a reliable partner. Consistent strategic policy, particularly regarding critical supply chains like semiconductor manufacturing and AI tools, is essential to restoring foreign confidence.

Strategic Failures and Diplomatic Deficits in the Middle East

  • The Iran Conflict as a Strategic Failure: Antony Blinken argues that unilateral confrontational policies have left Iran with its regime intact, its missile capabilities preserved, and significant leverage over the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Shipping and Economic Impacts: Even minimal Iranian disruption in the Strait (e.g., through low-cost shoulder-fired missiles) threatens global shipping lanes, drives up maritime insurance costs, and forces the international community to include Iran in regional negotiations.
      • Regional Destabilization: The conflict has severely altered regional dynamics, evidenced by Iran launching more projectiles at the United Arab Emirates (UAE) than at Israel.
  • Hollowed-Out Diplomatic Infrastructure: Linda Thomas-Greenfield highlights a critical vacancy crisis in US diplomacy that prevents effective negotiations.
      • The US currently lacks Senate-confirmed ambassadors in over 100 countries, including vacancies in 37 African nations.
  • Domestic and Constitutional Costs: Sen. Andy Kim criticizes the normalization of executive-led, undeclared wars without congressional approval.
      • He notes that the conflict has directly impacted American families, costing consumers over $42 billion in inflated gas and diesel prices.
      • The Hypocrisy of Corruption: Thomas-Greenfield points out that documented financial self-dealing in past US administrations severely undermines American diplomats' ability to advocate for anti-corruption and human rights abroad.

The Evolution of Warfare and National Resilience in Ukraine

  • Ukraine's Battlefield Endurance: Despite a reduction in direct US leadership, Ukraine continues to hold its military and economic lines against Russian aggression at a immense human cost.
      • A Future Defense Hub: Due to rapid innovation in drone technology and battlefield tactics, Ukraine is poised to become a central hub for Europe's future defense industry.
  • Russia's Cannibalistic War Economy: Antony Blinken notes that while high oil prices temporarily sustained Russia, Vladimir Putin has constructed an unsustainable war economy. Resources poured into perishable military hardware are hollowing out productive infrastructure investments.
  • From WMDs to "Weapons of Mass Production": Sen. Kim highlights a major paradigm shift in modern conflict, moving away from conventional weapons of mass destruction to high-volume, low-cost "weapons of mass production" like AI-assisted drones and ballistic missiles.
  • A Foreign Policy of Resilience: Rather than simply opposing isolationist foreign policies, Kim argues the US must focus on domestic and supply-chain resilience—specifically securing critical minerals, pharmaceuticals, and energy production to safeguard national security.

"Variable Geometry" and the Future of Global Governance

  • Unprecedented Pace of Technological Change: Antony Blinken underscores the hyper-accelerated speed of modern development, pointing out the time it took various technologies to reach 50 million users:
      • Telephone: 75 years
      • Radio: 45 years
      • Television: 20 years
      • Instagram: 4 years
      • ChatGPT: 4 months
  • The Rise of "Variable Geometry": Traditional rigid alliances (such as East-West or Democracy-Autocracy divides) are being replaced by fluid, issue-specific coalitions. Future governance will rely on "variable geometry"—dynamic alignments of nations, corporations, and non-state actors uniting to address distinct challenges like climate change, synthetic opioids, or AI governance.
  • Connecting Foreign Policy to Domestic Needs: Linda Thomas-Greenfield advises future leaders to ground their platforms in the daily struggles of ordinary citizens. Rather than focusing on abstract foreign policy concepts, leaders must connect global stability directly to domestic pocketbook issues, education, and local economic opportunities.

Notable Quotes

"If we're competing with China one-on-one, that's a game that we may lose... But when we're aligned with Europe, with Japan, with Korea, with India, with Australia, with Canada, we go from about 25% of world GDP to 50 or 60%, a lot harder for China to ignore." — *Antony Blinken*
"It makes us look like hypocrites. It is a very hard conversation to have with a foreign government where you're talking to them about corruption in their country and they're going to talk back to you about what's happening in your country." — *Linda Thomas-Greenfield*
"Now, we are finding ourselves in a place where we are trying to navigate these weapons of mass production. This idea of what it is when you're able to produce such quantity, whether drones, ballistic missiles, when we have the proliferation of AI... that is the future." — *Sen. Andy Kim*
"We have a new world order that is being shaped, and grappling with that is going to be the biggest test internationally for future presidents... Increasingly we're going to see alignments of not just countries but companies and other actors... around a given issue. It's what I used to call 'variable geometry.'" — *Antony Blinken*
We looked at a lot of your speeches. You said, "If the United States is competing with China alone, we may well wind up on the losing side of the equation." Where do you think the relationship stands now? What is your assessment of that, and what is your prescription on where it really needs to go from here? Well, thanks very much, Jen. It's great to be with everyone, and to be with my friends and colleagues as well. Look, I think we have a president who divides the world up into what he sees as spheres of influence, where the big countries get to do what they want in their part of the world and the smaller countries have to suffer what they will, and that includes China. So, other than some trade issues that may affect us, his inclination is to give them free rein over their region and maybe even a little bit beyond. And I think we saw that play out. The challenge is that what happens within one sphere of influence almost never stays there, and countries are always looking for more. China is an example of that as well. This is the one country that has the capacity as well as the intent to reshape the international system in a way that advances its interests and values, and probably undermines ours. I think, as we're looking at it, we, we, we have to resist trying to put it on a bumper sticker. There's no neat way of summing up what is probably the most complex and consequential relationship. We compete, for sure. We contest where we really disagree, and we have to find ways to cooperate on issues that really are in the interest of both of our peoples. But from that perspective, you've got to approach it from a position of strength. And that means making ourselves as strong as possible at home with the right investments, but it also means making ourselves as strong as possible around the world—aligning other countries, building convergence. Because, to the point you made, if we're competing with China one-on-one, that's a game that we may lose. Their market's much bigger, their manufacturing is three times our manufacturing, purchasing power parity is greater than ours, more papers, more patents, a bigger navy—you can go down the list. But when we're aligned with Europe, with Japan, with Korea, with India, with Australia, with Canada, we go from about 25% of world GDP to 50 or 60%. That is a lot harder for China to ignore. Unfortunately, where we are now is just as the president seems to be underwater on a lot of issues within the United States, we're underwater around the world. There was a poll out a couple of weeks ago, covering 130 countries, and what it found was that China was seen as a more credible actor than the United States. In Germany, 70% of people in one poll saw the United States as an adversary. So this means that our ability to align, to build convergence in ways that can deal with the challenge that China poses, has been vastly diminished, and that's a real challenge going forward. And that's sort of a perfect setup for you, Ambassador. I mean, everybody knows who the Ambassador is, but she's spent not just time living up in the apartment in New York engaging with other UN ambassadors, but also decades serving around the world in the Foreign Service. And one of the things about these trips is these countries—to your point—countries around the world are evaluating not just the United States, but also the strength of the leader that you're meeting with. I wonder if you're thinking about how President Xi and others—other potential partners, and some current allies that Tony referenced—look at this trip. How do they look at this trip? And how do you kind of prepare these relationships and try to rebuild that type of coalition that he was referencing? Secretary Blinken—I'm sorry, I worked with him before, I'll keep calling him the Secretary. Go ahead. Am I on? I think the entire world was watching Trump's trip to China, and they were watching, holding their breath, because they knew that whatever came out of this trip would not be good for them. And so, they were waiting to see. I mean, imagine the people of Taiwan and what they are thinking. All of our friends in Russia, in, in, in Europe, what they were thinking. And I think even more than anybody, the Russians were watching this very closely because when we were in New York, Russia and China were "friends without limits." They were the "bros." They kept very close connections with each other. Now, China has played a very interesting game, and... and Trump, where now we are talking to the Chinese about things that the Russians might feel uncomfortable about. So, we're seeing the pieces of the chessboard moving around in ways that have all the pawns feeling uncomfortable. You know, what about Africa? We're telling Africa, "You need to make some choices about what you want to see in your future." And they're being forced into making choices related to China because, again, I think they find China much more trustworthy as a partner than the United States. How this will boil or what it will boil down to in the UN remains to be seen. But I think China's prowess, its power, its leadership on the multilateral stage is increasing. Senator, part of our objective today is to certainly talk about the path forward. And you know, in terms of building strength here at home and building these relationships, it requires tough choices at home at times. There has been bipartisan support, for example, for something that the Ambassador referenced, which is supporting Taiwan. We don't know where that stands exactly right now. What are the tough choices you think Congress and other leaders need to make over the next couple of years in order to position this relationship in a more constructive way? Well, one thing that I think was absolutely spot on is talking about strength—talking about credibility, that the Ambassador and the Secretary talked about. But I want to throw one more word into the mix here that I hear a lot from foreign leaders. I had this one leader of an Asian country call me and talk about just the challenges that they see when it comes to US-China, and just writ large with foreign policy. And they said to me, they just said, "I want you to know that when we talk about America, we ask this question: Is America a reliable country?" And we know the answer to what that is. And so, I want to just really throw out that sense of reliability and how important that is, and how much we are struggling with it, and why we will continue to struggle as a country even after Trump is gone. And this is something that we're going to have to rebuild. So when we're thinking through in Congress, "How are we making these trade-offs?" we also have to be thinking about the importance of credibility, of reliability, in terms of what we're actually able to put forward, even when we're talking about some of our strategic advantages. For instance, all of us agree that we need to be thinking closely about how our chip manufacturing and other types of AI tools are so critical to the economy of the future. But how are we able to tell other countries what we think they should be doing when it comes to chips or manufacturing equipment, if we don't even know what the White House is going to do when it comes to chips, to China, and elsewhere? You know, when we're talking about all these different tools at our disposal, the problem is really whether or not we will be consistent with that. Seeing over the course of my time in Congress how Republicans had one tune on China for the past few years, and then all of a sudden are absolutely silent right now—I mean, not a single one talking to me about anything that they were concerned about over the past few years—it really just shows how much Trump has captured the Republican Party on China. We're no longer able to talk about this with the strategy that is necessary for what is the most defining competition of our generation. Another area where there seems to have been some capturing, shall we say, is on the war with Iran, where there still has not been a War Powers vote that has passed. It's come close. You've peeled off some people. But let me ask you, Secretary Blinken: right now, I think everybody would like to see this war come to a close. You can certainly tell us how that's going to happen if you know. But what do you see as the impact of what we're looking at right now, both—not just with Iran but in the region—and just kind of the US's role in the world, 3 years, 5 years, 10 years from now? Well, I, I, I think, Jen, we, we, we have an example here with Iran of a war choice that never should have happened, and a war that really sends almost a split screen. You can have tactical success in individual areas, and yet have a real strategic failure. And that's where we are, because at the end of the day, when the dust settles, it's likely that Iran will retain some vestiges of its nuclear program. It will certainly retain a lot of missiles; apparently they haven't in fact been destroyed. It will retain its regime, and it will have gained this incredible leverage that it didn't have before in the Strait of Hormuz, where it will, one way or another, more or less control what happens there because it will retain the ability to mess with shipping that's going through. And that means even, even in a small way, if one guy with a shoulder-fired missile can damage a ship, all of a sudden the ships get scared, the insurers get scared, and you've got a real problem—which means they're going to be in on the deal going forward. That radically changes the region, and it's going to take time to work out from that. You're going to have to find ways over time to work around the hold that Iran will have on the strait by building more pipelines—not the best thing, but it's probably the necessary thing to do—especially by focusing on renewables and other ways so that people are not under the thumb of the Iranians, and it really doesn't take much. But it's reshaped in other ways, too. We've alienated pretty much everyone in doing this. We've alienated the Europeans because we were kicking dirt in their face for two years over NATO, over Greenland, over Denmark, you name it. We didn't tell them about this. And then, when the going got tough, we said, "Hey, we need you," and all of a sudden they weren't so eager to do it. Our partners in Asia, they're the ones who've been on the receiving end of the real brunt of this in terms of the immediate impacts of prices and availability. And in a different way, our partners in the Gulf, who, to Andy's point, have seen how unreliable we are. Unlike the Europeans, unlike our Asia-Pacific partners, some of them would like the United States to, quote-unquote, "finish the job." That's not happening, so they're frustrated. Others feel like they've now been subjected to Iran's wrath. Iran has fired more missiles and projectiles at the UAE than it's fired at Israel in this conflict. It's radically changed the equation. I think there is an off-ramp, but the president's going to have to decide whether he wants to take it. And the off-ramp involves—guess what?—diplomacy and a compromise. The only two times the Iranians have made a deal in the past were to end the war with Iraq that went on for 8 years, with 200,000 Iranian dead—the UN came in, offered them a way forward, got the Iraqi troops out of Iran. The only other time was the JCPOA, the Iran nuclear deal that President Obama negotiated. It took two and a half years to get it. They were given some things, we got a lot, and we were in a much better place as a result. I think right now, if the president's looking at this, you're going to have to have some kind of an arrangement that maybe down-blends some of their highly enriched uranium, probably has a moratorium on enrichment for some period of time—that'd be a good thing. And on the Strait of Hormuz, there'll probably be some kind of tolling system, which no one will want to call by its name, where the proceeds are used for reconstruction of countries in the area that have been damaged. That's a lot to swallow because so much damage has been done for so little gain. Is there a scenario, Ambassador, where this war ends and the Iranians don't have some control over the Strait of Hormuz, some financial control? Do you think that's possible? Honestly, I don't. It will be difficult to get to. And part of the reason it will be difficult is, again, we're not negotiating with them. Why are we not negotiating with them? We don't have people to do it. We don't have Senate-confirmed ambassadors in over 100 countries. So who is going to negotiate with the Iranians? And I've heard over and over again—Tony, you've dealt with them—that they are extraordinary negotiators. We had extraordinary negotiators; we don't anymore. In Africa, where maybe we want to get some support, 37 countries do not have ambassadors. So the administration is taking the power that it has and dumping it, and so it's in a position where it is not able to negotiate an agreement that would quickly end this war. I think the only thing that will do it is the president will declare victory, which means he has accepted defeat, and move out of the area saying we've accomplished everything we've accomplished, when we know that he has not. Senator Kim, when you just follow some of the headlines, you see kind of "War Powers vote, War Powers vote, War Powers vote" failing week after week. But there have been some individuals peeled off, I should say, who have voted with the Democrats for a War Powers Act, which hasn't happened in quite a long time. Give us a sense of where you see things right now in that regard. Are there other ways that you think there is a bipartisan opportunity to hold people in this administration to account over this war? Yeah, well, first of all, I want to say that even the War Powers approach—this is the wrong approach that we should have been going down. This is not a situation where we should normalize the idea that a president can start a war on their own, and then it's up to— Very important point. Yes. —and then it's up to Congress later to disapprove of it, right? So just from the outset, this is wrong. This is an illegal, unconstitutional war. Now, we are unfortunately in a situation where we do not have three functioning branches of government right now. We have one branch of government that is simply trying to dominate the other two branches of government. I often call Speaker Johnson "Secretary Johnson," because he acts more like a cabinet secretary than he does as a leader of a separate branch of government. So that's the world that we're in right now. So yes, we're trying to operate with the tools that we can to shine a light and draw attention to the American people, who by the way fully see this for what it is. You know, when I'm back home in Jersey, I mean across the political spectrum, nobody likes this war. And they understand just the cost that they are bearing—over $42 billion more that Americans have spent on gas and diesel since the beginning of this war. You know, that's what we are trying to draw attention to, that drumbeat. So we have to make sure that we're not getting caught up on just the process between the different branches of government. This is ultimately about the people. And this is ultimately about the impact that this is having on them, and that this is a choice that this president made—not just to start a war or not, but whether to help the American people or not. And he has clearly made a decision to enrich himself and his family and his friends at the expense of the American people, and they see that so clearly when it comes to Iran. I'm so glad you took the question in that direction. It was spicy and good. Ambassador, you have spent so much time working in so many parts of the world where there are high levels of corruption, where human rights is a key talking point when you sit down with these leaders. When future ambassadors and future secretaries are going into meetings in the future, and they are looking at a past administration that financially benefited to the tune of 1.4 billion dollars—not my numbers, numbers that have been widely reported, and more—what impact does that have on the conversations? It makes us look like hypocrites. It is a very hard conversation to have with a foreign government where, you know, you're talking to them about corruption in their country and they're going to talk back to you about what's happening in your country. I think the only thing that will get us through this when there's a change of administration is that we hold those people accountable. If we hold them accountable, then we can go to other countries and say, "Yes, we had this problem, but accountability is important and this is what we're doing to hold them accountable." Now, will that happen? I, I don't know, but I would have a hard time having that conversation now with any country, because I would feel like a hypocrite—even though I feel strongly that corruption in government is bad. But to try to tell a country that from this side of the Atlantic would be hard. I want to turn—this is going to seem like a sharp turn, but I don't feel like there's nearly enough attention always on the war in Ukraine because of the war with Iran, because of corruption, because of all the other issues. And Secretary Blinken spent so much time as Secretary of State helping build coalitions, as did the Ambassador, of course. If you were to strip away the headlines about Russia, about Ukraine, about military battles, what should people in this room or people watching really understand about the state of things, now that we're four years into this war? So, Jen, I think two things. First, it's always good to remember why we got into this in the first place, why we were so determined in our support for Ukraine. Because, of course, it was an aggression against a country—and one big country, aggressing a smaller country, no one likes to see that, and that tugs on the heartstrings. But there was something else going on. It was an aggression against the very principles at the heart of an international system, no matter how challenged, that really mattered and mattered in working to keep the peace, working to help countries advance and develop—principles like sovereignty, like independence, like territorial integrity. That was the goal of the Russian aggression as much as it was to erase Ukraine and subsume it into Russia. And that's why we were so invested in it. But right now, what we've seen is something quite remarkable, which is a country of amazing resilience—a country that now, even with the United States receding in the role that it was playing, is still holding its own. Now, Europe has stepped up in ways that are really, really important, including with funding that is so necessary. But you see the Ukrainians on the battlefield holding their own at horrific cost—horrific cost to them, to their civilians and citizens, but also at horrific costs to the people that Putin continues to throw into this meat grinder of his own making. But they are holding on, and more than holding on, they're actually taking the fight, not to Russia. So, I don't think that line is likely to move very much. They're holding their own economically, and they're building a new economy again with help from Europe and from others. And the interesting thing is, with their defense industry and what they're doing with drones, they may well wind up at the heart of Europe's future defense industry. They have technology and they have techniques that we don't have, and we see that being spread around the world. And then step-by-step, they continue to build their democracy, getting closer to Europe, strengthening the tools. You've seen many people who are in high positions now being challenged on what we were just talking about: corruption. So, I think right now, my hope is that the pressure on Putin is such, it's finally catching up with him. He had a reprieve with Iran and being able to sell some oil at the higher prices, but everything else across the board in Russia is really turning. And he's built a war economy that worked for a while, but now is feeding on itself because basically everything that economy is producing, with everything going into the defense sector, is being eaten up and it's not productive. It's one thing if you build a weapon system that gets destroyed a few weeks later. It's another thing if you're investing in roads and bridges and technology. So, I think we're in a place where, if we can help in some fashion the Ukrainians to hang on, it'll get to a place where the line is more or less frozen, and the real measure of success is: can Ukraine stand on its own feet—militarily, economically, democratically? They're demonstrating that they can do that. Let me ask you, Senator, to one of the Secretary's points he just made. I mean Ukraine, it's become sort of a laboratory for intelligence and drones and kind of a different way of warfare. Once we get to a point past the "Secretary of maximum lethality," as I'll just call him, and we're in kind of a future point, what lessons do you think can be taken away from this war and what do you hope that Congress is applying? Well, I'll take it in two directions. First and foremost, yes, absolutely. This is showing this new era of warfare that we're in right now. You know, I'm somebody that grew up in the era where we were concerned about weapons of mass destruction. Now, we are finding ourselves in a place where we are trying to navigate these weapons of mass production—you know, this idea of what it is when you're able to produce such quantity, whether drones, ballistic missiles, and when we have the proliferation of AI, what we see with Mythos and other things like that that are going to completely transform. That is the future, and we do have to be thinking about it both in terms of the warfighter on our side, but also how do you defend against this? And this is why I was also going over some of our efforts to make sure that we as a nation are ensuring that we are able to do our best to ensure that our adversaries and our competitors like China and Russia are not able to gain some of these tools, whether through AI chips or the means of production to be able to produce those chips. You know, those are the types of things that are going to really determine the stratification of warfare in that way. The last thing I'll just say on this front is, I've been very much pushing that we, we can't just be saying that we're opposed to this "might makes right" approach of foreign policy of Trump, but trying to devise what is our way forward. And I, for one, believe that that revolves around a singular word, which is resilience. I think we need to focus on an era of resilience in this age of crisis, and to be able to mobilize ourselves to shore up our own vulnerabilities—whether that's critical minerals or pharmaceuticals or energy production or any number of other issues—while we're also trying to make sure that we are performing and fighting on the battlefield of our choosing and being able to advance on our strength. So that's what I think we need to be pushing forward on when we see this incredibly rapidly changing ecosystem that we're in. You know, there's no going back; we need that forward-looking vision and to be able to give a sense of what American credibility and reliability going forward will be based off of. Okay, we only have five minutes left, but we've covered a scope of issues I hope all of you care a lot about. Let me go to you, Ambassador. One of the things I would say broadly is, if I were to give unofficial, broad advice to anybody running for office or president, I would say: "sit down with really smart people and decide what you think about the really tough issues." So, let me pose this question to you: What do you hope that people who are aspiring to be sitting in the Oval Office are sitting down and spending time mapping out plans and ideas and thoughts with very smart people with right now? I hope that they are doing that, and I actually know—I expect—that they're sitting down with smart people. But what they really need to do is sit down with the people. They need to get into communities, go into towns, go into capitals, and talk to the people. Because it's ordinary people in places like Louisiana who will be voting for them. And if those people are not hearing from them, nothing that smart people say to them will get them elected. What do you wish they would be talking about? Talking about real issues, talking about pocketbook issues, talking about the price of gasoline, talking about their futures—whether their children will have jobs, whether their children will be educated, whether their school boards will be able to pull books like *Roots* out of the library—and sticking with it. You know, they want to hear those issues that touch them every day. They don't want to hear the esoteric issues about war and peace, even though those issues have an impact on them. They expect their leaders to know what to do on those issues, but they want their leaders to know what to do to make their lives better. Secretary Blinken, let me ask you the question in a different way because you have helped past people running for president think about issues, and kind of red-teamed with them what they think, how to talk about it. What would be top of the list for you, if you were doing that with some candidates right now? You know, there's so much to think about, to focus on. I guess I'd say a few things. One is—and it echoes what Andy's saying and I think what Linda's saying—there's a lot of understandable nostalgia for the past because we had an American century. We had 80 years where, as a result of the work that previous generations did, we were at the front of the parade and shaping things. And those 80 years, with all of their imperfections, profound as they were, we passed that, and the reality is you can't put the genie back in the bottle. You've got to shape a new bottle. And that's what these conversations have to all be about. We have a pace and scope of change that is moving at speeds unlike anything I ever experienced before in my 30 years in government. Just by way of one example, to get to 50 million users of the telephone back in the day, it took about 75 years. For radio, it took about 45 years. For TV, it took about 20 years. For Instagram, 4 years; for ChatGPT, 4 months. The challenge that anyone in governance has in dealing with that is acute. We have inequalities among countries that have actually decreased, but within them, they've increased, and that has all sorts of repercussions. But finally—and this is what I want to get to—we have a new world order that is being shaped, and grappling with that is going to be the biggest test internationally for future presidents. We have all sorts of emerging actors who are super-empowered by technology and by information, and as a result, can either advance or disrupt things in ways that they weren't able to before. And that means if you don't have them on the takeoff, you're probably going to have a crash landing. So, as I'm looking forward and I'm thinking about how you advise a president on how to engage the world, of course, it starts with our democratic base and you want strong democracies. I believe that one principle is enduring, and that is enlightened self-interest. The strength and success of others actually redounds to us, and that continues to be important. American engagement and leadership continues to be important. But as we're looking at the way the world is going to be designed, I think increasingly we're going to see alignments of not just countries but companies and other actors that have a stake in a given issue coming together around that issue. It's what I used to call variable geometry—different coalitions of different shapes and sizes, who are all brought together by a shared interest in a particular issue. Maybe it's dealing with opioids, maybe it's dealing with food security, maybe it's dealing with climate, maybe it's dealing with AI governance. And it won't be a neat autocracy-democracy breakdown, or East-West, or North-South. It'll be the like-minded on a given issue, willing to play by the same set of rules. I think a president has to look at that and think about how he or she organizes the country to engage in that. But it starts with, as we've all been discussing, regaining our credibility, and that's going to be the first task. It's a perfect place to end. I'm very grateful that they are all thinking about these big, difficult issues that I know are on the minds of lots of people here and people watching. Thank you all so much for this panel. Really enjoyed it.

Introducing Gemini Omni

Google for Developers(2.6M) ·44:11 ·2.5K views · 2026-05-19
Logan Kilpatrick (Host) Nicole Brichtova (Google DeepMind) Dumitru Erhan (Google DeepMind)
Google DeepMind has introduced Gemini Omni, a native multimodal model that acts as "Nano Banana for video" by seamlessly combining text, image, audio, and video reference inputs to enable highly controllable 10-second video generation and precise editing.
TL;DR Google DeepMind has introduced Gemini Omni, a native multimodal model that acts as "Nano Banana for video" by seamlessly combining text, image, audio, and video reference inputs to enable highly controllable 10-second video generation and precise editing. Fully...

TL;DR

Google DeepMind has introduced Gemini Omni, a native multimodal model that acts as "Nano Banana for video" by seamlessly combining text, image, audio, and video reference inputs to enable highly controllable 10-second video generation and precise editing.

Architecture & Multimodal Capabilities

  • Fully Multimodal In/Out: Gemini Omni is built from the ground up to understand and synthesize multiple modalities simultaneously. It processes text instructions, reference images, audio, and video clips to generate highly consistent, synchronized video and audio output.
  • The "Nano Banana for Video" Concept: While Google's Veo model focused on professional video generation, Gemini Omni specializes in flexible, language-guided video editing and consumer-focused interactions.
  • Multi-Reference Consistency:
      • Subject Modeling: Users can input up to seven reference photos or videos of a subject. By supplying shots from multiple angles (e.g., side profiles), the model builds a robust 3D-like understanding of the subject's face and structure.
      • Voice & Speech Matching: The model retains high vocal and speech consistency during edits, such as seamlessly transferring a human speaker's voice to an animated character or animal.
  • Temporal Awareness and Pacing: Gemini Omni demonstrates superior chronological control over previous models. Users can prompt it to generate fast-paced sequential frames (e.g., animating the entire alphabet with corresponding objects in under 10 seconds) or request slow-motion outputs.

Advanced Video Editing & Semantic Reasoning

  • Complex Video Edits: The model handles challenging editing actions via natural language prompts, such as:
      • Environmental Transport: Placing a subject from one source video into an entirely different setting while maintaining action consistency.
      • Object Removal: Making a specific object (e.g., a violin being played) completely invisible while keeping the rest of the subject's posture, hands, and audio unchanged.
      • Perspective Shifts: Changing camera angles and viewpoint perspectives within an existing scene.
  • Text Rendering Upgrades: Gemini Omni features a step-change improvement in on-screen text rendering. Because it learns text and visual modalities together natively, it produces highly legible, contextually accurate text overlays.
  • Story Continuity: The model exhibits emergent storytelling capabilities, allowing users to prompt it to generate "the next scene" in a video. It can intuitively continue a visual sequence—such as panning around a corner or introducing a new character—without explicit training.
  • Style Transfer: The system generalizes visual styles organically, transforming real-world video footage into creative mediums (e.g., claymation, crochet dolls, or celestial patterns) while keeping the underlying motion and composition stable.

Ecosystem Integration & Product Availability

  • The Gemini App: Targeted at mainstream consumers for quick, creative edits and 10-second generations. It is available directly to Gemini Advanced, Ultra, Pro, and Plus users.
  • Google Workspace & Flow: Designed for professional creators, Flow leverages Gemini Omni for deep, iterative editing, enabling creators to stack scenes, construct persistent characters, and utilize agentic workflows to brainstorm video ideas.
  • YouTube Shorts: Integrated directly into YouTube Shorts and YouTube Create, allowing users to remix and edit eligible video content using Omni's generative suite.
  • API Access: Developer APIs are planned for upcoming rollouts to make these generative video tools accessible for third-party application building.

Safety, Digital Watermarking, and Likeness Control

  • The Avatar Flow: To protect digital identity and prevent unauthorized deepfakes, users must go through a secure, one-time setup to utilize their own likeness.
      • Verification Protocol: Users must record themselves speaking a designated sequence of numbers to verify active consent, alongside submitting multi-angle headshots.
      • Persistent Storage: Once generated, the authorized digital avatar is safely stored and can be easily invoked across Google products like YouTube, Flow, and the Gemini App.
  • SynthID and C2PA Integration:
      • Resilient Watermarking: All generated videos are embedded with Google's SynthID watermark, which is imperceptible but designed to persist across subsequent edits, crops, and file compressions.
      • Cross-Platform Verification: Users can upload videos to the Gemini App to check if they contain a SynthID watermark. Google also embeds open-standard C2PA metadata in all output files to ensure transparency across external platforms.

Notable Quotes

"We have a really great video generation model but it especially shines at video editing... it’s really the next step towards the journey of making Gemini fully multimodal in and multimodal out." — Nicole Brichtova, Google DeepMind
"If you provide less [information in the prompt], the model has to go and be more of a director on your behalf." — Shlomi Fruchter, Google DeepMind
"In the past, most people would have never learned the professional tools to be able to do these kind of things... now you have text rendering working, and when we add in the grounding and actual real information, it just makes the models much more useful." — Gabe Barth-Maron, Google DeepMind
There's so many different kinds of edits that you can do, and I think we weren't sure what exactly the model was going to pick up on, but it actually is very versatile. We have an amazing group of people who have been making demos for the past couple of weeks, and I've been really blown away with all the kinds of things that they have been able to do, of like turning themselves into crochet dolls and like liquefying things, you know, and those things just work and it's all through prompting, which is pretty incredible. I think there's two things that people should take away from this model. One is we're basically bringing Nano Banana to video. So we have a really great video generation model, but it especially shines at video editing. So you can take in image, video, audio input, references, and right now get video as output. It was sort of like a foundational part of the original Gemini models, and then obviously the whole suite from Nano Banana to VO to some of the music and audio models is sort of state of the art across the rest of the Gen Media portfolio. And so this is literally asking the model to generate the alphabet and then examples of an object for each letter of the alphabet. Nice. Nice. I think that's one of the things that really, really improved on is that the model has the ability to create very fast potential sequences. So unlike previous generations of our models, the control over the time and being able to tell a story is much better. This is a bit of a different, extreme kind of a situation where you want to create very fast frames that go one after the other. Each one has different content, and there is a specific order. So the model is very aware temporally where everything should go. And basically, when the user asks for a relatively kind of complex sequence, then the model is able to reason about it and create this sequence correctly. To your question about the sort of, do you have to prompt it? You can, but you don't have to. It's pretty, pretty flexible in that sense. You can just say, "Well, do it in 10 seconds," and it will do it. But you can also specify like, "Well, I want it to be slow-mo," whatever it is that you want it to be. So it's pretty controllable in that sense. Part of it comes from the conditioning, so like there's this text conditioning that goes into the model—should be fast-paced, should be really slow. But then another part of it is just from the model itself. Like, it really looks to find these correlations between what you're asking for and how the video should look. I love it. There's like an AI video speed. That first example is like cool to see that it is a lot faster pace than sort of what you would normally get out of a sort of an AI video generator, which is cool. And this one's very fast-paced, and we prompted it to be very fast-paced to like fit the alphabet in. But we have another example, which goes into the editing capabilities and also the anything-in, because we're using an image as input and a video as input. So let's play a video of this woman talking. "Well, I don't know what it is, but today is going to be a good day. I can feel it." This is Irina, one of our amazing creators from the Greenfield team. And then the prompt is basically, "Turn me into this animal." Awesome. Just to draw it back to sort of folks who have some familiarity with the VO models. This is sort of like a combination of like reference to video, audio, and the part that wasn't possible before is like the audio consistency. Because like, you could do sort of video editing like that with previous VO generations, right? With like reference to video or no? Or was that not possible? You could do reference to video where the reference was an image or an audio clip, but video editing is a net-new thing that we're bringing with Omni, and that's why we like to use the reference of it's kind of like Nano Banana for video. Because we're basically combining everything that we had in VO, hence also Omni, and we're adding video editing. And it's really nice because with Gemini in the loop again—because we're building on Gemini—you can use fairly simple language, right? Like literally, "Turn me into this animal." There's nothing that complex in the prompt, and then the model just does it and then it keeps the voice the same and it keeps the speech the same, which is really nice. Yeah, we pretty much built the model from the ground up to be able to understand different modalities, so in this case the image and the video, so it can actually know what information it has to take from the source—or in this case the reference video where a person is talking—and re-contextualize it, you know, in this case as an animal talking. But the small—I think the small movements, the expressions of the animal kind of transfer from the original person, and that's, I think, really—like those nuances make it look better. Yeah, I think that's a really important point is that while VO could certainly do reference to video, that was a capability that we kind of like almost layered into it, whereas this is—with Omni, it's really a step change. It's like, this is—we started from the foundation, we kind of had to rethink how to build this model from the ground up, and we're really happy with the results we got so far. Maybe pragmatically also like, it actually does improve. The quality improves with more references that you put in of the same subject. So you can like, you can stuff in more images of yourself and more whatever, whatever it is, and it will build a better model—world model, if you want to use the term of art. And it will just be a better outcome. We can also talk a bit more about the way to provide the model with your references. So we have the avatar workflow, which we'll be talking about more, that allows you to kind of like just one time take kind of photo of yourself and speak and get the sample, and then use it across different generations. But also you can bring three or four or even more, up to I think it's about seven references of yours and voice references. And again, the more information that the model has, the more it can kind of like recreate and re-contextualize the presence of the specific person or identity in a new scene. And I think what we've seen is that it's also not just the number of references, but also what information the references have. There's obviously a lot of new things that you can do with this model, right? Including edit videos, which includes speech, including, you know, using voice and audio references. And so we are actually going out pretty conservative when we first release this model, and you might feel like we're blocking a lot of things. I think SynthID and the ability to know if video is basically generated—I think it's really key because we see the—the capabilities of models across the board get better and better. And we think that this is basically probably one of—of the—the most important ways to know that content is generated, and you should know if you want to trust it or not based on—on that information. In—in just in that, so we're obviously very proud of SynthID, which is a tool that we've developed, but we also partner with C2PA and we embed C2PA metadata in all of our content, so then if people want to check it on other platforms, they also can do that. And SynthID, like SynthID, it persists across edits as well and if people make modifications in post as well. And I just want—for so everyone knows—the way to use is you upload a video to the Gemini app, you can ask, "Is this an AI generated video?" and it will check that for you. I love it. Personally, I'm really excited about, you know, pushing the envelope on what's possible in—across, you know, quality-wise, the duration of—of video. I'm excited about everything. Everything, but specifically—specifically, I—I really would love to see people being able to create kind of like—to tell stories. The Founders of Claude AI Tell Oprah About the Impact Artificial Intelligence Has on Your Life

Oprah(945.0K) ·66:15 ·74.7K views · 2026-05-19
Oprah Winfrey (Host) Dario Amodei (CEO and Co-founder, Anthropic) Daniela Amodei (Co-founder, Anthropic)
Anthropic co-founders Dario and Daniela Amodei emphasize that while AI represents an "epochal change" comparable to the Industrial Revolution, its development must be steered by human ethics and the "speed of trust" rather than pure commercial speed.
TL;DR Anthropic co-founders Dario and Daniela Amodei emphasize that while AI represents an "epochal change" comparable to the Industrial Revolution, its development must be steered by human ethics and the "speed of trust" rather than pure commercial speed. The company operates...

TL;DR

Anthropic co-founders Dario and Daniela Amodei emphasize that while AI represents an "epochal change" comparable to the Industrial Revolution, its development must be steered by human ethics and the "speed of trust" rather than pure commercial speed. The company operates as a Public Benefit Corporation, prioritizing safety and public good—including refusing lucrative military contracts that violate democratic values—to ensure AI empowers rather than replaces humanity.

Mission-Driven Architecture and Ethics

  • Public Benefit Corporation (PBC) Status: Anthropic is legally structured to balance commercial interests with social missions.
      • Wealth Pledge: All seven co-founders, including the Amodeis, have pledged 80% of their personal wealth from the company to philanthropic causes.
  • Incentive Alignment: The company purposefully avoids an ad-based business model to prevent "attention addiction."
      • Subscription Focus: By charging for services rather than selling data, the incentive is to make the tool useful and efficient so users can complete tasks and return to their lives, rather than staying on the platform indefinitely.
  • The Sibling Dynamic: Dario (CEO) and Daniela (President) credit their relationship with maintaining a "human anchor" and group accountability, ensuring that no ego overrides the company’s safety mission.

Safety, Red Lines, and Military Refusal

  • Refusing the Pentagon: The founders detailed a "dark-night-of-the-soul" moment where they refused to remove safety guardrails for the military.
      • Red Lines: They specifically rejected use cases involving fully autonomous weapons (drone armies) and domestic mass surveillance, viewing them as contrary to democratic values.
      • Economic Risk: They maintained this stance even when threatened with being barred from doing business with any entity that contracted with the government.
  • Age Restrictions and Protection: Claude is officially restricted to users 18 and older.
      • Detection Tools: The model uses interaction patterns to identify and suspend accounts of minors attempting to bypass age gates.
  • Mythos (The "Smarter" Model): A recent breakthrough model demonstrated advanced capabilities in both conducting and defending against cyberattacks.
      • Strategic Deployment: Anthropic released this capability to "defenders" (infrastructure companies like Microsoft and Apple) first to patch vulnerabilities before a general release.

The "Light and Shade" of AI's Impact

  • The "Light" (Medical and Empowerment):
      • Healthcare: Rapid drug discovery for cancer and Alzheimer’s through specialized biotech acquisitions.
      • Productivity: AI acting as an "angel on the shoulder" or coach to empower users (e.g., helping a lawyer transition to a writing career).
  • The "Shade" (Societal Risks):
      • Relationship Displacement: The founders warned against "falling in love" with AI, arguing it should facilitate human-to-human connection rather than replace it.
      • Economic Disruption: Acknowledgment that while entry-level jobs face disruption, the value of "human-centric" roles—such as doctors with good bedside manner—will increase as technical tasks are automated.
  • The Frictionless Life: Dario argued that while AI removes "bad friction" (like disease), it must not remove the "meaningful friction" of human struggle and growth.

Regulation and Public Participation

  • Pro-Regulation Stance: Unlike much of Silicon Valley, Anthropic supported California’s SB 1047 and federal oversight.
      • Industry Backlash: The founders received significant criticism from investors and peers for supporting government accountability and "red lines" for tech companies.
  • The "Speed of Trust": Dario noted that trust is currently in short supply and that the industry has failed to let the public feel like participants rather than passengers.
      • Knowledge as Power: Daniela urged the public to become AI-literate to maintain agency and autonomy as the technology evolves.
"There's this train. It's going very fast in some direction. You don't want it to crash. You can't stop the train. But what you can do is you can steer the train. Steer the train so that it doesn't hit the rocks." — Dario Amodei
"We can only diffuse this at the speed of trust. And trust is currently in short supply. Something is happening to humanity with this technology bigger than anything that has happened in hundreds of years." — Dario Amodei
"AI is an opportunity for us to level the playing field in some ways, but it requires people to be engaged... The more you know about it, the better informed you are going to be to use your voice effectively." — Daniela Amodei

When we started testing Mythos, when we make these models, we test them for lots of things. We say, "Oh, how good is this thing at translating, you know, French to English?" Or "How good is this thing at, you know, recommending a stock portfolio?" Or "How good is it at finding bugs in software code?" You ever heard about like ransomware attacks on hospitals or schools? So, you know, these cyberattacks happen all the time. And Mythos, we found—not because we designed it to do this or because we designed it to be dangerous—turned out to be very good at both conducting cyberattacks and defending against cyberattacks. And so we said, "Wow, this thing kind of seems like a weapon." We shouldn't—you know, we probably shouldn't give it to everyone right away. And so the thing we came up with is we said, "We're going to take some companies that are particularly core to the infrastructure of the internet," right? Some of the top banks, some of the top, you know, software providers like Apple or like Microsoft or like Google, and hand it to them and let them fix everything before it becomes widely available and things can be broken. So we would kind of give it to the defenders before the, you know, before the attackers got a hold of it. And so we had a program. We've given the model to—I think it's something like 40 companies now, and they're busily finding bugs. And you know, we just had a post a few days ago that one of the well-known companies found more bugs and fixed more bugs with Mythos in a week than they had had for the last year. And—and so we are—this is really changing things. Now our hope is the defenders can fix everything and everything gets more seamless, everything becomes harder to break into. So our hope is the era of ransomware and, you know, spies breaking into your phone and— People are worried about bioterrorism and should be. Yeah. And I mean, the answer is yes, at some point we'll screw up something. But like, are we going to get the big stuff right? Yeah. And also because you all are fundamentally principled people. So I think that actually makes a big difference when you're fundamentally principled people. Not everybody is, and there's a lot of egos in every field. But I think particularly when people are making so many billions and billions and billions of dollars and there's the race to beat China and all of that, that it's easily to lose sight of what's—what really matters and what really matters to the public. I'm wondering what you all have done to sort of keep your egos in check. You know, I think it again, to us it always comes back to the mission, right? Why we did this in the first place. Why did we leave OpenAI to start Anthropic? Because we wanted to make sure that AI goes well for everybody, right? That's why we're a public benefit corporation. That's why we took the 80% pledge. That's why we've made all of these difficult decisions. You know, I was in our internal slack messaging app today, you know, I talk to the company a lot, and one of the things I was saying is, you know, we're 3500 people now. We were 1200 people like six months ago. At one point, because I encourage this, someone posted in my personal slack channel—usually it's only me who writes things in my channel—someone wrote in my channel, "This was a pretty bad thing to say and it makes everyone feel bad and this like you shouldn't have said it this way." And of course, you know, when I read it, you know, the initial reaction is always like, "Oh man, I feel like I'm being attacked." But—but like, you take a beat and then two minutes later I'm like, "I'm so glad people are saying this." And then you know I wrote something that was like, "I'm so glad you're willing to say that—" So were you always— —and then people notice this. Like many people messaged me where it's like, "I didn't know you could do that. I didn't know you could just contradict the CEO of a 3000-person—" That is a powerful example. You got to. You got to. You know why that's a powerful example? Early, early on, like in the late 80s, I think I met Henry Kravis for the first time and he was saying to me, "Be aware that the more money you accumulate, the less chance that you're ever going to hear the truth." Because he said, "Rich men—" and I guess he also meant rich women too—but he goes, "Rich men rarely hear the truth." Yes. I'll also just add, I think what you do in your personal life matters too. There's what you do at work. But, I basically have the same friends I've had since like high school and college. And guess what? They have no trouble being honest with me. And they have no trouble telling me the truth. And they have no trouble disagreeing with public decisions that Anthropic has made, right? So I think the last five years have changed a lot, but I think for me personally, I don't really feel like that much has changed outside of work. And I think that's an important grounding characteristic. I think people who tend to be in charge of these big companies, sometimes it's like they become obsessed just with their work, they're not a well-rounded person. And I think there's something when we started Anthropic of like the first, you know, of the seven or eight folks who really started the company, only one of them had one kid and now there's 11 kids. And I think there's something about the fact that we all chose to be parents, we all want fulfilling real lives outside of work. I think that just brings us a sort of groundedness, humility, low ego, and just again, like no one is bigger than the mission. So, so what comes through really clearly is that sister and brother, you started this, it's bigger than you ever imagined, you always wanted to make an impact in doing good for the world, and you stuck to your principles even when it was really difficult. What gives you the hope that you will get this right? So, we're never quite sure, I think is the honest answer. Like we believe we're trying our best and trying very hard. it's just the decisions are complicated, right? If we go back to Mythos, it's like do we give it to more people faster or less people slower? Like who do we trust? And so it's about getting all the details right in addition to just having the right principles, which I believe we do. And so I think we worry about it every day and the fact that we worry about it every day is maybe the thing that most gives us the hope, that we're so paranoid about it. But it's—it's no guarantee and we've made mistakes, we're going to continue to make mistakes, like, you know, that's—that's a given, right? Are there other people in Silicon Valley who also have it a part of their mission to get it right? Do you feel supported in that mission by others? It's hard to point to maybe like a particular company, but I think I am very touched by sometimes, you know, whatever, you go and talk in an event or another company wants you to come speak to them. And somebody in the audience, you know, comes up to you afterwards or asks a question and they say something like, "Thank you guys for the way that you do things. Like thank you for trying to get it right." There's this kind of sense that—you know, I think—I think what's great about our relationship is Dario and I also think we hold each other accountable, right? And so when, you know, we go back and forth on, "Is this the right decision for the company? Is this in keeping with our values?" We have each other, we have the whole co-founding team is still at Anthropic. I think we've also hired an amazing set of people who are also there for the mission. There's almost a kind of group accountability. And if you pair that with, like Dario mentioned, this culture internally of anybody can disagree with us, right? And yes, that's hard to do, but there's a lot of forums in which they're encouraged to do that: their manager can do it, they can do it anonymously. I think all of those qualities make it more likely that if we start to stray from the path, we're at least going to know. Okay, and you want to leave us, the public, knowing what? What is the most important thing you want us to know in this moment in time where we are in our AI journey? You know, there's something really big happening to the world here. This isn't the—this isn't the latest technology, this isn't crypto, this isn't mobile, this isn't like the latest, you know, gadget or buzzword. Something really big is happening to humanity here. While the technology is being built by a small number of people, we—we need to make it work for everyone and we need to make sure that between all of us, we make the right decisions and we reckon with changes to our daily lives, changes to our jobs, changes to, you know, government and civil liberties. And if we do this the right way, all of those things could change in a positive way. All of those things could be better than we left them. We could have the perfect world where, you know, many diseases are cured, people's lives are more joyful and meaningful. But, you know, if we get it wrong, it's easy to imagine things not going that way at all. And, you know, it's up to all of us. Some of us are in a more central position, but that's something I think we need to change as well. And, you know, it's not easy, it's not any one thing. It's going to be a bunch of different things. I think I would probably leave people very similar to what Dario said with this concept of light and shade. That the potential benefits from artificial intelligence are vast. We've written about them, we've tried to talk about them publicly, we believe they're real. And also the risks are also real, right? We're not trying to hype up the positives or hype up the negatives. I think they're both true. It's complex, but we have to hold two things at once. And I think just echoing Dario, this is not something that technology companies can do by ourselves, nor should it be. It's something that's going to take partnership from a much bigger group of people than is currently working on artificial intelligence. And I think the first step there has to be: don't—don't be afraid of it, learn about it, lean into it, ask questions, be critical. Just like we say to our staff, right? We are only going to have a chance of getting this right if we expand the circle of people that are in the conversation. I ask this of all my guests and particularly I'm interested from the two of you and what do you think is the meaning of a well-lived life? And how AI is going to contribute to that well-lived life. But what—what will be the meaning of a well-lived life for you, your family, and your children? I think for me a well-lived life is one where you have agency and autonomy. You're in the driver's seat and you're using that in the service of being the best version of yourself. I think the best version of yourself for you, for your family, for your kids, for your broader community, and I think for the world. I think the ability to be self-reflective, to learn from mistakes, to overcome struggles, and to come out the other side thinking, "You know, I know more about who I am, I like who I am, and I want to help other people." I think that to me is the best definition of a well-lived, introspective life. And like I talked about, I think—I think there's a world where AI could damage so many of the things that I think are the hallmarks of a well-lived life, and I think there's a world where it could enable those. And it could allow a lot more people to be able to have meaning and value and to self-introspect, to be the best versions of ourselves, to show up for each other well. And I hope that's the world that we're going to build toward. I'd just say the same thing as Daniela in different words. I think it's pretty simple. You know, I think, you know, just operate always with a sense of dignity and principle. Like, you know, when you look back and you're like, "You know, don't—you shouldn't when you look back be ashamed of what you did or say, you know, I wish I'd done this other thing." Of course you'll make mistakes, but you know, you were—you were putting every iota of effort you could into trying to get it right. you know, I think we have duties both to individuals, you know, to my co-founders, to all the other people in my life, but you know also to the users of our technology, to everyone else in country and world as citizens. So I think we have duties to all those people that we have to attend to and, you know, making sure that, at the end of it it was directed at some purpose, some consistent view of what we were trying to accomplish in the world. The vision we were trying to make happen. Maybe it doesn't happen, maybe we screw it up, maybe we make terrible mistakes. But you know, just having the vision be clear and trying to pursue it with integrity. If you can do that, of course you want the outcome too, but I think if doing that is all you can control, right? And more important ultimately than the outcome. Thank you for growing us forward. You're doing exactly what you envisioned as little kids. It is very strange what we are. Yes, it is. Thank you, thank you, Dario and Daniela. You've given us a lot to think about. Go well, everybody. Go well. You can subscribe to The Oprah Podcast on YouTube and follow us on Spotify, Apple Podcasts, or wherever you listen. I'll see you next week. Thanks, everybody.

Google I/O '26 Keynote

Google(14.2M) ·111:16 ·8.4M views · 2026-05-19
Google has officially entered its "agentic Gemini era," powered by the new Gemini 3.5 Flash and Omni models, alongside massive infrastructure investments.
TL;DR Google has officially entered its "agentic Gemini era," powered by the new Gemini 3.5 Flash and Omni models, alongside massive infrastructure investments. Key breakthroughs include Gemini Spark—a 24/7 background AI assistant—and Antigravity 2.0, an agent-first developer...

TL;DR

Google has officially entered its "agentic Gemini era," powered by the new Gemini 3.5 Flash and Omni models, alongside massive infrastructure investments. Key breakthroughs include Gemini Spark—a 24/7 background AI assistant—and Antigravity 2.0, an agent-first developer platform capable of autonomously building a functioning operating system from scratch in 12 hours.

The Gemini 3.5 Model Family & Global Infrastructure

Google is taking a full-stack approach to AI innovation, scaling its custom hardware and foundation models to meet exponential user and developer demand.

  • Exponential Token Growth: Google's services now process 3.2 quadrillion tokens per month, up from 480 trillion last year. Model APIs process approximately 19 billion tokens per minute.
  • Massive Infrastructure Investments:
      • Google's annual capex is projected to reach $180 to $190 billion this year, up from $31 billion in 2022.
      • TPU 8t & 8i: The 8th generation of Tensor Processing Units features a dual-chip architecture. The 8t is optimized for large-scale pretraining across a globally distributed cluster of over 1 million TPUs. The 8i is designed for inference, generating nearly 1,500 tokens per second with 2x better energy efficiency per watt.
  • Gemini 3.5 Flash: A high-speed, cost-efficient frontier model that is 4x faster than other frontier models.
      • By shifting 80% of workloads from other frontier models to 3.5 Flash, top enterprise customers can collectively save over $1 billion annually.
  • Gemini Omni: A new multimodal world model capable of simulating physical realities (like kinetic energy and gravity).
      • It can generate high-fidelity video, images, and simulations from any input, allowing users to edit video styles and content conversationally. Gemini Omni Flash is available starting today.
  • Deepfake Detection (SynthID): SynthID has watermarked 100 billion images and videos and 60,000 years of audio. Major industry players—including OpenAI, NVIDIA, Kakao, and ElevenLabs—have adopted SynthID to establish standards for AI transparency.

Antigravity 2.0 & Autonomous Software Development

Google is transitioning from AI tools that assist writing code to autonomous developer agents capable of acting and problem-solving independently.

  • Agent-First IDE: Antigravity 2.0 is a new, standalone desktop application designed entirely for multi-agent orchestration, agent-produced artifacts, and background execution.
  • 12-Hour Operating System Challenge:
      • To test the bounds of the new Antigravity Agent Harness, Google tasked autonomous agents with building a working operating system from scratch.
      • Using Gemini 3.5 Flash, a team of 93 parallel subagents completed the task in 12 hours, making 15,000 model requests and processing 2.6 billion tokens.
      • The total cost of the project was under $1,000 in API credits. The agents successfully wrote and compiled everything from the scheduler to the file system.
  • Hyper-Speed Execution: Gemini 3.5 Flash has been co-optimized within Antigravity to operate 12x faster than standard configurations, compressing multiday engineering cycles into minutes.

Consumer Agents & Reimagined AI Search

Google is deploying agents directly into its consumer ecosystem, establishing 24/7 background agents and upgrading its foundational search box.

  • Gemini Spark: A dedicated virtual-machine-based personal AI agent that runs on Google Cloud 24/7, even when the user's laptop is closed.
      • Spark integrates with Google Workspace, Gmail, and third-party tools via the Model Context Protocol (MCP).
      • It can securely execute complex background tasks, such as tracking RSVPs from emails directly to a live Google Sheet and building promotional slide decks based on Google Drive files.
      • Pricing Update: Spark is rolling out to trusted testers immediately, with a beta launch for U.S. Google AI Ultra subscribers next week. Google is introducing a new Ultra plan for $100/month and reducing its top-tier Ultra plan from $250 to $200/month.
  • The Intelligent Search Box: The Google Search box has received its largest upgrade in 25 years. It is now a multi-modal, expandable AI canvas that supports text, images, files, and video, offering real-time, AI-formulated suggestions.
  • Search Agents & Generative UI:
      • Search Agents: Users can spin up multiple continuous background agents in Search to track real-time stocks, apartment listings, or product drops 24/7.
      • Generative UI: Leveraging Antigravity and Gemini 3.5 Flash, Google Search can now code custom interactive widgets and dynamic layouts on the fly in response to complex queries (e.g., creating a real-time, customizable binary black hole orbit simulation). Generative UI is rolling out to all users for free this summer.

Agentic Commerce & Creative Media Tools

Google is redefining online shopping and digital content creation through standardized agent protocols and intuitive canvas tools.

  • Agentic Commerce Protocols:
      • Universal Commerce Protocol (UCP): An open-source, industry-backed standard (co-developed with Amazon, Meta, Microsoft, Salesforce, and Stripe) that provides a unified language for shopping agents across product research, checkout, and shipping.
      • Agent Payments Protocol (AP2): Establishes secure, tamper-proof digital mandates and spending boundaries so agents can securely buy products under strict user control.
      • Universal Cart: A cross-merchant, cross-service cart running on Gemini that automatically tracks price drops, verifies hardware compatibility (e.g., matching computer processors and motherboards), and applies payment card perks from Google Wallet.
  • AI-Powered Creativity:
      • Google Pics: A new design tool in Google Workspace with built-in object recognition, enabling users to edit, remove, and translate elements in posters and infographics seamlessly.
      • Google Flow Updates: Integrates Gemini Omni for high-precision environment replacement and features new Flow Agents that can instantly generate 16 unique video angles from a single base image.

Extended Reality (XR) & AI for Science

Google's AI models are expanding directly into wearable consumer hardware and leading-edge scientific disciplines.

  • Android XR & Intelligent Eyewear: Developed in partnership with Samsung and Qualcomm, Android XR is powering a new wave of smart glasses:
      • Display Glasses: Provide a small in-lens visual overlay for hands-free navigations, translation, and widgets.
      • Audio Glasses: Launching this fall in partnership with Gentle Monster and Warby Parker. These stylish glasses provide private, hands-free Gemini voice interaction, enabling users to call apps (like ordering food via DoorDash) completely hands-free.
  • Accelerating Scientific Breakthroughs:
      • WeatherNext: A global forecasting AI model that accurately predicted a Category 5 hurricane hitting Jamaica three days earlier than traditional systems during the 2025 hurricane season.
      • Isomorphic Labs: Reimagining drug discovery using biological models like AlphaFold and AlphaGenome. The lab is currently in pre-clinical stages with multiple projects, including potential treatments for immune disorders and cancer.

Notable Quotes

"It’s been 10 years since we pivoted the company to be AI first... Over the past 12 months, over 375 customers each processed more than 1 trillion tokens, representing incredible demand for AI across our industry." — Sundar Pichai, CEO of Google
"Artificial general intelligence is just a few years away... When we look back at this time, I think we will realize that we were standing in the foothills of the singularity. It will be a profound moment for humanity." — Demis Hassabis, CEO of Google DeepMind
"Over 12 hours, 93 subagents working in parallel made over 15,000 model requests and processed 2.6 billion tokens to take an initially empty project to the core of a functioning operating system... Multiday engineering efforts are collapsing into hours, if not minutes." — Varun Mohan, Tech Lead for Google Antigravity
