So, it's at the end of the day a logistics company. There's a lot of hardware, robotics—actually, a decent part of our tech team now works on hardware, right, and some of the, like, the videos that we saw today. And so, there's a good amount of work that we're doing on the backend, and the thought process there is also not just for the function of—again, right, it starts from the customer. Why am I doing all of this? I'm doing all of the depth in the supply chain only because every rupee of cost you save across that supply chain by becoming more efficient is a rupee you've saved the customer, or a rupee that you can invest in better selection, better delivery times, etc. And the last thing that I'll mention also, for us, the biggest culmination of this is our fruits and vegetables supply chain. Without going into too many specifics, we run one of India's largest—or at this point, the largest—fruits and vegetables supply chain in the country. And we source, you know, millions of units per week from farmers across the country—everybody from Mahabaleshwar for strawberries, or, let's say, the outskirts of Karnataka, outskirts of Bangalore in Hoskote or beyond. Can you give people a sense of the scale that you're operating Zepto at? I mean, the numbers are just jaw-dropping. I just learned today that Aadit, at 23 years old, is the largest seller of fruits and vegetables in all of India. On the platform—on the platform, yeah. No, so, I mean, we have, like, a decent amount. Like Jared mentioned, on the Zepto platform, there are millions of units per day of fruits and vegetables that we sell, and of course, all sorts of other products. But yeah, I mean, we're at a point where today we've got a customer base, you know, in the crores, right, of monthly transacting users. There are millions of people that order every day, and we do millions of deliveries per day. And we've scaled to—I mean, you know, publicly available information, right—but we've scaled to, you know, tens of thousands of crores of top line, or billions of dollars, right? And from our lens, obviously, long way to go, candidly. I think that there is—I mean, we're in the grocery market, and there's a huge amount of scope to run this up. And there's a lot of work that we still want to do on squeezing out more and more efficiency from the supply chain, squeezing out more efficiency on, you know, the consumer part of the business, which I think we discussed today as well. But we also have a meaningful advertising business on the app itself, where the top brands in the country like Coca-Cola, or Pepsi, or Nestle, bid on search keywords. So, we're doing, you know, hundreds of millions of ARR in ad revenue today. So, there are so many big opportunities in the business. And right now, we're growing quite fast, and so we think that we still have a few more years of high growth and high amounts of operating leverage and efficiency that we can unlock, and new big things—like, for example, this ads business, you know, was very small two years ago, or inconsequential, and now it's become a very big part of our journey. So, there's a long way to go, but, you know, we're still in Day One. Can you talk about the long-term vision of Zepto? I think a lot of people maybe haven't really thought about what Zepto could become in the future, and I think it'd be really interesting to hear what you imagine it could be in 10 or 20 years. Yeah, so the way that we—the way that we look at it is, we want to build an urban grocery platform, and we want to build urban grocery infrastructure for the country. If you look at it, every, you know, major country in the world has their own form of grocery infrastructure, right? Or, you know, logistics and supply chain and retail infrastructure for day-to-day goods, right? People in the US or people in Europe are buying much more sophisticatedly, right? Their costs are much lower, the sophistication they have on quality control is better, and it's much more organized, right? We basically think of ourselves as an organizing force in the grocery supply chain in the country. Obviously, we deliver a delightful experience to customers, but the big vision is, I guess, over the next four-five years, to be a large grocer in the country and do that in the top 40-50 cities. And if we can do that, then I think we can build India's, like, homegrown version of Amazon or any other e-commerce platform, right? We want to build, you know, an e-commerce platform that's unique to India. And if you look at the key, nobody's really been able to do this hyper-local e-commerce format anywhere in the world, right? We think it works beautifully in India, we think it's the right model for India, and so if we crack it, then we can build a very large outcome and hopefully also play a part in, you know, growing other businesses along with us. I think one of the other key things that I'll mention is that, you know, Zepto and all these other quick-commerce platforms—not just Zepto—have become a platform for a lot of startups to build their own consumer brands, right? And so, you know, eventually down the road, if we can create 10 more large outcomes in day-to-day consumer brands, maybe someone here will start a consumer brand. Why not? You know, that happened in the US 40 years ago with a Walmart or a Costco and all these other players. We're still at the beginning of that journey, so we want to be part of that journey. The last thing that I'll mention, of course, is the people that we employ on the ground. I think if we keep scaling this business, we keep creating more employment on the ground, and we think it's a wonderful thing. I—I'll thank, you know, Jared here. I told Jared that, you know, everybody wants to meet Y Combinator because they think of YC as a very good, positive force. A lot of people have told me that all these investors are creating lots of jobs in the country, and we think it's true, you know. So, so yeah, we're—we're here creating employment, helping build a small part of India's infrastructure. So, that's our thought process. So next, I want to talk a bit about AI. Even though Zepto got started before the whole AI boom, you guys are now really deep into implementing AI at Zepto, and really on the cutting edge of, like, what companies here are doing. And you were telling me this morning about all the ways that you transformed your internal operations with AI. Can you talk about, like, what's what—how you guys are using AI, and how it's changed things for you? Yeah, I mean, I think so there are two parts of it, obviously: there's the customer side, and there's the supply chain side. And so from our lens, there's a lot of work that we've done on the supply chain side. For example, we—we showed Jared today one of our dark stores, and we're basically walking through how it used to take a team of people, you know, days to manually forecast the entire supply chain. But now we've built out a machine learning algorithm that runs, you know, millions of units per day, doing forecasts without any humans in the loop. And what that's made is—it's made my supply chain more agile. I'm able to dispatch faster, I'm able to do more throughput in the same place because my forecast accuracy is so high, and I'm able to forecast much faster. So, it's purely as a function of, you know, a lot of work we've done on the machine learning side. On the consumer side, one of the big benefits for us has been the ads business. We've seen a big bump in ads over the past two years because a lot of products that we're building for our brands are AI-led. So, let's say on the search platform, for example, there are lots of things where we're giving people the opportunity to generate keywords, or we give them sort of—we've built out a model that helps you accurately predict what keyword is going to give you the best return on ad spend. And so, there's a lot of work that we've done there, but we've seen huge results. Once brands see that their marketing dollars are getting put to use in the most optimal way, and they can see that objectively in their advertising efficiency spends—like, "return on ad spend," as we call it in the industry—then it's been a huge bump in advertising revenue. So, building out the search stack of advertising with GenAI tools has been massive. And the last thing that I'll tell you is, internally—internally we've been able to achieve a lot more with less headcount. Like in our corporate office—hopefully some of you, you know, have interacted with our team—but you know, in our corporate office, we've been able to do a lot more with the same headcount. You know, the engineering team that we've had... I mean, obviously everyone says this, but I see this on the ground, and it's still mind-blowing to me. The amount that we're able to build with such a small team is crazy, especially like all the SaaS bills that we used to have. I was telling Jared this—you know, I can say it publicly—we've cut almost all our software spends to zero. We've cut, you know, a lot of our managed services spends, we've been able to reduce a lot of inefficiencies and manual tasks in the system, right, that were happening with people. We've been able to automate it now. And so my overall organization, if you walk into the Zepto office, there's so many more tools that are automated, and there's no outsourced tools at all, so we're saving, like, literally hundreds—I mean, huge amounts of costs on that side, so it's great for us. What is the software team like at Zepto, and are you hiring? Yeah, we've got about 500 or so engineers and another 150 people on data science, analytics, product management, designers, etc. So, you know, we're very much hiring and, you know, actively in the market always for the best talent. So if anyone here is—is keen, let us know. You know, we're still very much at the beginning of the journey. We consider ourselves an early-stage startup, so we're not a big, old, slow company. So if you're excited to come into a startup environment, please do join. Okay, and we're running out of time, but my last question for you, Aadit, is: in order to run this company, you had to master an incredible number of skills, and you had to do it at a very young age. I mean, you started this thing at 17, and now you're running, like, a massive cold storage supply chain. You've got trucks and warehouses and physical sorting machines, and 250,000 employees you have to manage. How do you keep upgrading yourself as a person and a founder, and keep learning these skills so that you could do this incredible work at such a young age? Again, you know, very... you know, that's a very generous way of looking at it. I tell the team this all the time, that it's good that we're a Y Combinator company because I've got exposure to what's happening in Silicon Valley, and after everything we've done, we feel like peanuts compared to what people in the US have done sometimes, right? You know, like Uber or DoorDash or Airbnb. So, a long way to go still. But I think the—the big thing for us has been that, I mean, both KB and I have just been incredibly lucky with the people that we've been able to surround ourselves with since the earliest stage. And you know, without exaggeration, since the group partners that we had with you, and Tim, and Surbhi at Y Combinator. But every stage of the journey, we've just been lucky to have incredible people that have been—that have surrounded us, that we've learned from. And most of them—most of that learning—has come from our management team. So, I think what the luckiest that we've been is that we've been able to find a team—whether it's our CFO, our Chief Operating Officer you met today, our CTO, you know, our head of growth and marketing, our Chief Business Officer, our, you know, leader in the product function—all of these people who've got decades of experience but are still hungry and believed in the vision very early on, despite being much more senior and tenured than us. Having them join early in the journey and learning from them was the biggest one. So I think we woke up every morning and we said, "You know, if I'm working shoulder-to-shoulder with this exceptional leader in finance and this exceptional leader in operations, then I need to pull up my boots, right? And I have to learn from them." So, you just have to be... you just have to surround yourself with people that are smarter than you, and just keep learning from them, and keep asking. And even today, like with our finance team, for example, I ask the most basic questions: "How does accounting policy work?" Or the ops team, I ask them: "How does this hardware-software work?" So, you know, we also have looked a lot, very stupid a lot internally, but then that's been good because we shamelessly ask questions, we shamelessly learn, and then you end up aggregating information from a lot of people, and then you grow. So, surround yourself with people that are smarter than you, and learn from them shamelessly—as simple as that. Aadit, I and everyone in this room is in awe of what you've accomplished. Aadit Palicha, everyone.

Developer Keynote (Google I/O '26)

Google for Developers(2.6M) ·95:41 ·27.8K views · 2026-05-19
Jeanine Banks (VP & GM, Developer X, Google)
At Google I/O 2026, Google announced a massive shift from assistive AI to autonomous agents, driven by Google Antigravity—a unified agentic development platform designed to build, deploy, and orchestrate AI agents seamlessly across Cloud, Android, and the Web.
TL;DR At Google I/O 2026, Google announced a massive shift from assistive AI to autonomous agents, driven by Google Antigravity—a unified agentic development platform designed to build, deploy, and orchestrate AI agents seamlessly across Cloud, Android, and the Web. Managed...

TL;DR

At Google I/O 2026, Google announced a massive shift from assistive AI to autonomous agents, driven by Google Antigravity—a unified agentic development platform designed to build, deploy, and orchestrate AI agents seamlessly across Cloud, Android, and the Web.

Google Antigravity & Managed Agents

  • Managed Agents in Gemini API: Developers can build custom agents using markdown-defined instructions and tools via a single API call. Every managed agent is automatically provisioned with a secure, isolated remote Linux sandbox environment hosted by Google, removing infrastructure overhead.
  • Google Antigravity 2.0: A new, agent-first desktop application designed for orchestrating complex workflows.
      • Dynamic Subagents: Allows primary agents to autonomously spin up specialized parallel helpers (e.g., QA or Data Science subagents) to accelerate tasks.
      • Scheduled Tasks: Integrates standard cron scheduling to run agents on autopilot (e.g., generating pull request summaries every morning or monitoring cloud systems hourly).
  • Antigravity CLI & SDK: A lightweight CLI brings the agent harness directly to terminal environments with custom workflow bindings, completely replacing and upgrading the legacy Gemini CLI. A standalone SDK is also available for total deployment flexibility.
  • Gemma 4 Fine-Tuning: Gemma 4 (released under an Apache 2 license) has surpassed 100 million downloads in its first month (pushing total Gemma downloads past half a billion). Developers can utilize the Antigravity CLI to autonomously write training code, run LoRA fine-tuning on remote GPUs, and deploy highly specialized custom models.

Rapid Application Deployment in Google AI Studio

  • Deployments to Cloud Run: Developers can go from a prompt to a live web URL on Cloud Run in just a few clicks. Starting today, new users can deploy instantly with no credit card required.
  • Instant Native Android App Generation: AI Studio can now generate full-stack, Kotlin-based Android apps directly from prompt instructions. Developers can preview apps via an in-browser Android Emulator, connect their Google Play Developer account, and push builds directly to the Play Store test track.
  • Google Workspace Integration: Support has been added to seamlessly connect apps to Docs, Gmail, and Calendar to keep workflows in context.
  • AI Studio Mobile App: Google announced a brand-new mobile app bringing the rapid AI Studio prototyping experience directly to mobile devices, rolling out in the summer of 2026.

Specialized Native Android Development Workflows

  • Official Antigravity Integration: Android support is officially built into Antigravity, including an Android CLI that handles SDK downloads, project creation, and device testing autonomously.
  • Android Knowledge Base & Skills: Specialized data sources provide models with up-to-date documentation and best practices (such as XML-to-Compose migrations). Internal testing shows agents utilizing these skills use 70% fewer tokens and complete tasks 3 times faster.
  • Performance Optimization: Integrations with Android Studio enable agents to audit build configurations, suggest optimizations to R8 rules for maximum binary shrinking, and configure deep links natively using the App Links Assistant.
  • iOS-to-Android Migration Assistant: A new experimental assistant in Android Studio allows developers to upload an iOS project (such as Xcode storyboards and assets) and translate it into a high-quality native Kotlin/Jetpack Compose app. The migration is validated by letting the agent autonomously execute and test custom user journeys.

Next-Generation Web Development & APIs

  • Modern Web Guidance: A curated set of skill blueprints mapped to Baseline standards. This guidance guarantees that agents build with modern, compatible APIs, improving unguided agent coding success rates by an average of 37 percentage points.
  • WebMCP (Model Context Protocol): A proposed browser standard that allows web applications to securely expose internal capabilities and UI elements directly to browser-based agents. WebMCP enters Origin Trial starting in Chrome 149.
  • Chrome DevTools for Agents: Bridges runtime execution with the coding agent. Agents can run automated audits (including a new agentic Lighthouse category that verifies `llms.txt` maps, ARIA accessibility trees, and WebMCP schema rules) and autonomously fix runtime and accessibility issues.
  • HTML-in-Canvas API: Currently in Origin Trial, this new API breaks historical limitations by allowing real, interactive, accessible DOM elements to be rendered natively inside custom Canvas environments.

Program Announcements & Developer Offers

  • Build with Gemini XPRIZE: A global $2 million hackathon challenge tasked with finding, building, and deploying real-world solutions that can positively impact the lives of 1 billion people.
  • Google AI Ultra Pricing & Credits: A simplified $100 per month Google AI Ultra subscription plan. To assist developers during the launch, all active Ultra subscribers will receive a $100 bonus credit directly in the Antigravity app.

Notable Quotes

"Honestly, it feels like the hottest new programming language is markdown, and I'm here for it." — Logan Kilpatrick
"Starting today, you can go from an idea to an Android app directly in AI Studio. No software to install, no SDKs to manage, and no local environment needed." — Paige Bailey
"Because most agents navigate the web using the accessibility tree, every ARIA role or label that you optimize doesn't just help a human; it makes your site more actionable for an agent, too." — Una Kravets

We aren't just making it easier to build agents; we're making it easier to build with agents. As you heard earlier today, we're introducing Google Antigravity 2.0, a new desktop application that is unabashedly agent-first. You can kick off multiple agents in multiple projects simultaneously. One agent vibe-codes your marketing website; another generates brand assets; and a third plans your project architecture, operating in multiple work-trees, collaborating without collisions. For coding tasks, you can use Antigravity 2.0 alongside your IDE of choice, but we all know there is much more to do than just code. Antigravity 2.0 is your mission control for orchestrating agents for all sorts of tasks. Now, we've all seen single agents get overwhelmed by massive tasks. That's why today, we're introducing Dynamic Subagents. Now, your agent can spin up specialized helpers like a QA or Data Science subagent that work in parallel to get the job done faster and more effectively. Now, that might sound impressive, but what if you want the process to run autonomously? For example, you might have tasks that you want agents to repeat on a predefined schedule. Today, we are making that a reality with Scheduled Tasks. You can now use standard cron scheduling to put your agents on autopilot. So tell your agent to summarize pending PRs every morning or monitor your cloud health every hour. And this is just the beginning of making your agents truly proactive. To show you Antigravity 2.0 in action, let's invite Kevin on stage. >> KEVIN HOU: Thanks, Anshul. Let's give Antigravity 2.0 a challenge. As you heard from Josh at the beginning, the momentum we're seeing behind Gemma 4 is absolutely incredible. Democratizing AI means allowing anyone to take open models like Gemma 4 and fine-tune them. Historically, that meant complex pipeline wrangling by ML engineers. Not anymore. With Antigravity, anyone can do it. Let me show you. The other day, my CI pipeline broke, which gave me this idea: Why not have an LLM automatically self-heal the pipeline? I would just feed it the stack trace with the build errors and say "fix this" to generate a remediation Bash command. Anshul, can you explain while I get this set up? >> ANSHUL RAMACHANDRAN: Yes. The challenge is that LLMs have been trained to be conversational, so they bury the bash command in paragraphs of explanation. In fact, when we asked for a simple Git work tree command earlier, the response came back with two separate code blocks, a conversational introduction and a detailed breakdown explaining how to choose branch names. But all Kevin cares about is the command. So what he needs to do is fine-tune Gemma 4 not to return the fluff and just give him the command. >> KEVIN HOU: Exactly. And rather than watch me type, let's go ahead and use the mic, powered by our latest audio understanding capabilities. Okay. So I want to fine-tune Gemma 4 to directly give me a bash command response with no additional fluff so I can use the response directly in my CI pipeline. I have a data set of prompt-to-bash commands. Write the training and eval code for doing a LoRA fine-tuning over this data set, as well as the code to deploy my custom Gemma 4 model on my laptop. >> ANSHUL RAMACHANDRAN: As Kevin runs that, I want to highlight a couple of things. He was able to use just his voice, and the audio model recognized terms like "LoRA". It seems like the agent's done some research, and it's generating an entire implementation plan for the training code. >> KEVIN HOU: I approved the implementation plan, and it's writing a bit of code to make this happen. Once this is done, I'm going to take this and push it to my repo. And because we're training a model, I'll need a GPU-enabled cloud machine. >> ANSHUL RAMACHANDRAN: So while Kevin does that, you just saw Antigravity 2.0, our new stand-alone app that is your mission control for orchestrating agents. But we know that for a lot of developers, the real magic happens in the terminal. So that's why we're thrilled to announce the Antigravity CLI. It's a lightweight way to spin up the same Antigravity agents right from the terminal. It gives you the exact same harness and the same models, but with a product experience that is tailored for the command line. Christel Heydemann – CEO at Orange | Investment Conference 2026

Christel Heydemann (CEO, Orange)
Christel Heydemann is transforming Orange from a process-heavy incumbent into a high-velocity, AI-driven organization by balancing the zero-risk requirement of critical infrastructure with a "winning mindset" that embraces fast failure and cross-border scale.
TL;DR Christel Heydemann is transforming Orange from a process-heavy incumbent into a high-velocity, AI-driven organization by balancing the zero-risk requirement of critical infrastructure with a "winning mindset" that embraces fast failure and cross-border scale. Strategic...

TL;DR

Christel Heydemann is transforming Orange from a process-heavy incumbent into a high-velocity, AI-driven organization by balancing the zero-risk requirement of critical infrastructure with a "winning mindset" that embraces fast failure and cross-border scale.

AI Integration and the Need for Speed

  • Strategic Deployment: Orange has trained thousands of employees on GenAI and deployed it across three primary pillars:
      • Customer Service: Using assistants to manage journeys for over 300 million customers.
      • Network & Security: Leveraging AI to manage the complexity of data flows and cyberdefense, which Heydemann notes is "not possible" to handle manually.
      • Software Development: Utilizing AI to unlock software capabilities that were previously cost-prohibitive.
  • Decision Frameworks: To move a company of 130,000 employees at a startup pace, leadership focuses on distinguishing between "one-way" and "two-way" decisions to avoid bureaucratic gridlock.
  • Security and Ethics: Despite the push for speed, Heydemann emphasizes that AI agents will not manage core networks yet due to the critical nature of the infrastructure and broader societal security concerns.

Cultural Transformation: From "Process" to "Purpose"

  • The "Process" Trap: Heydemann identified a legacy culture where employees felt safe simply by following processes, even when those processes contradicted each other or slowed down innovation.
  • Defining Values: To unite 26 countries, she established three core values:
      • Bold: Encouraging teams to innovate and speak up about failures.
      • Responsible: Taking ownership and calling out issues when they arise.
      • Caring: Prioritizing both customers and employees.
  • Winning Mindset: While Orange teams excel at crisis management (restoring service during outages), Heydemann is pushing for a "winning" culture that focuses on proactive innovation and "failing fast" rather than just reacting to extreme events.

Operational Excellence and Customer Experience

  • The "Airbus" Comparison: Heydemann challenged a 40% failure rate in fiber connectivity installations by comparing it to the aviation industry, arguing that a high failure rate should never be accepted as a "fact of life."
  • Quality as Innovation: She redefined innovation not just as new technology, but as the optimization of the customer journey—ensuring that when a customer signs up, the service works immediately and reliably.
  • Beta-Testing vs. Scaling: The company is moving toward a software-style development model: testing solutions in beta phases before scaling them to tens of millions of customers.

European Competitiveness and Market Scale

  • The Scale Imperative: As a capital-intensive, fixed-cost business, Heydemann argues that scale is non-negotiable. Being "number one" in a market provides the necessary margins to reinvest in next-generation infrastructure.
  • Fragmentation Barriers: She critiqued the European regulatory environment, noting that it is currently impossible to "copy-paste" a successful service from one country (like Spain) to another due to fragmented regulations and local "empires" within the company.
  • Asset Sustainability: She expressed concern that European operators have been forced to sell assets to fund future investments, a cycle she believes must be broken through better regulation and continental scale.

Leadership and Governance

  • Board to CEO Transition: Having served five years on the Orange board before becoming CEO, Heydemann used her "outsider-insider" status to dismantle myths regarding shareholder constraints (specifically the French government's 23% stake).
  • Direct Communication: She maintains a transparent relationship with the board, treating them as peers and bringing them into difficult tactical discussions early.
  • Leading by Questioning: Heydemann noted that in a large organization, the *questions* a CEO asks are often more influential than the answers they provide, as they signal what the organization should value.

Our next speaker may have the answer. Christel Heydemann is the CEO of Orange Group, a world-leading telecom with 340 million customers across 26 countries. She's now leading it into a world being disrupted by AI, where the technology never stops moving and the competition never slows down. In a fast-moving industry, how do you build a culture that can keep pace? Pedro Furtado Reis, the co-chief investment officer for Active Strategies, joins her to find out. Please welcome Christel and Pedro. A big welcome to Oslo, Christel. Thank you. Great to be here. So, let's just get jumped straight into it. As we just learned from Anthropic, there's a lot of change happening in leadership and in technology at this stage. How are you leading Orange to operate at a new and much faster pace now? Well, I think a company like Orange that operates in 26 countries has a mission that's so clear. We've been passionate about technology. Of course, we started with copper networks and 2G, GSM at the time, and we had Nokia phones as we've seen earlier. The company loves technology changes, so that's the easy part. Now, the difficult part is speed. I think AI is just mind-blowing and it feels like it keeps accelerating. The speed, of course, in a company with 130,000 employees in 26 countries is not a startup. So it's all about taking decisions, understanding what are one-way decisions, and what are two-way decisions where you can go backwards. That makes a huge difference. So we are just testing, testing, testing. Of course, there's a number of things we have to address, not just Orange, but as a society. Because security—I mean, Anthropic knows with Mithos—there's a lot of debate that is happening with country leadership and security agencies about what we are going to unleash when this technology goes into all our hands, whether they are bad or good hands. Beyond the need for speed, where is AI already having the most impact for your teams or for your customers right now? So we've trained thousands of our teams and we've put tools in the hands of our teams so that they could use GenAI. Of course, if you use GenAI to go faster in your analysis, email, and work, that's the basics. That doesn't really change the company itself. We've launched a number of assistants in our customer service, of course. When we serve more than 300 million customers, that's a lot of data. In terms of how we manage the portfolio of offers we have across countries and how we simplify, we have AI tools for our sales teams. To be able to propose the best information and to know the customer journey—these are things we just cannot do without AI. We have, of course, our networks and cybersecurity. Today we have Orange Cyberdefense that's preventing and helping customers cope with cyberattacks. Our networks are so complex and the data that flows through our networks is so complex that without AI it is just not possible. So there are many, many areas where we already see the potential of AI. We're not a software development company, so when I meet with Anthropic and I'm told that I'm going to have unlimited capabilities to develop software, I like that promise. It feels like we're going to be able to do things that, frankly speaking, we just could not do. The cost of it is still something we haven't tackled. And I imagine that there is a lot of experimentation going on at the moment. How do you create that room for bold innovation and risk-taking in a company that at the end of the day is built on critical infrastructure that simply cannot fail? Well, that's a very good question and that's really the culture of a company whose purpose is very clear. No matter what happens—extreme events, cyberattacks—our networks and our connectivity services have to work. Which means that the design and the way we work is absolutely zero risk. To a point where now, of course, if we think about AI, we are not going to let AI agents manage our core networks because that's not an option. So we've made it very clear. We come, in France or in many countries actually, from being an incumbent, so we come from being an administration. The company value—and when I joined the company I was very surprised—was that people think that doing good is applying the process. If I apply the process I'm safe, I'm doing my job, and that's all good. Sometime we have to challenge—actually very often we have to challenge—because the process is not fast enough. The process was designed for a different intent and sometime we have way too many processes that end up contradicting each other. So that's all the work we're doing on changing that. But in the end, it's been said earlier: the winning mindset. I've been reflecting on what "winning" means in a company like Orange. Our teams are excellent in managing crisis. So you would think that today's world is awful; we go from one crisis to another. At Orange, our teams are excellent because during extreme events, network outages, or cyberattacks, this is when the company unites around one purpose which is very clear: restore the service, serve our customers. So that's the good thing given the current environment. Now, of course, when we think about innovation, we have to move from a culture where we were thinking that if we launch a service for millions of customers, everything has to be ready and the sales people have to be trained. That does not work if you want to incubate new solutions. And so we've been designing things where it's okay to fail. It's okay to test and not scale a solution, or to fine-tune it. One of the challenges, of course, like any big industrial company, is that you're not launching a product for tens of millions of customers unless you know it's going to work. But you can launch a solution beta test like software development and then, when it's ready, you really scale it. So we've had to change that. We've done a lot on cybersecurity, satellite connectivity complementing our mobile networks, and we talk a lot about AI solutions and AI agents for our sales team and customer service. It's back to, you know, telecom operators somehow have been good at taking technology and bringing it to market. But I don't think as an industry we are very good at coming from the customer needs, the real needs, and then pulling back what type of innovation could go with it. When I joined the company, I was amazed by the failure rate we had when people move into a store and sign up for broadband fiber. Guess what? 40 percent of the time we would send the technician and fail with the connectivity. So I was challenging the team saying, "Think about companies like Airbus. If 40 percent of the time you ship the plane it crashes, why are we not collectively in the company trying to address that?" But the mindset of the company was that it's for a fact a 40 percent failure rate, and they did not realize that maybe we could optimize that. So we've been working on that. This is the type of innovation that maybe is not visible by the engineers, but it's very visible by the shop people. They see people signing up for broadband and they get the service and then they get the bill and they pay and they get their bonus, when on the contrary we had people waiting for weeks and eventually canceling their order. That's the type of innovation which is focused on quality, customer experience, going into the details of our processes, and working with our partners on it. You've mentioned winning, and we heard some simple words from many companies today. You chose for Orange: bold, responsible and caring. So my question is, how do you move that? How do you bring that to life in the 26 countries, from France to the rest of Europe to Africa? How do you make sure that those are the values when you're not in the room? Well, first when I joined the company and I asked, "Okay, what are our values?" there wasn't one answer. So we had values in Africa, we had values in France, we had values for our B2B business. And so I told the team, "Okay, what makes us a group then? What's the many-in-one value that brings us together which is more than just a sum of divisions?" We spent a lot of time on it, and given our purpose and the service we provide, it was actually very easy to come up with what creates excitement for our teams. Then we worked on these values around caring—caring for our customers and our people of course—and being responsible. People think about responsible, but we've been very clear that responsible is also that you're responsible to call it out and to speak up when things go wrong. And then "bold" is probably the value where the company is more eager to transform because this is an expectation from our teams that we want to innovate and we want to be bold. If you think about the Orange brand when it was created more than 20 years ago, it was very bold. So there's also a link with the past and where we come from. If I take bold, for instance, first of all the values work together; it's not just one or the other. It's also about speaking up about our failures. It's okay to fail. I mean, it's not okay to lose billions, but it's okay to fail and to fail fast, and it's okay to speak about it as part of our culture. There's a lot of things we've changed, starting in the management team. Then of course we worked on how we assess leaders, who we promote, and why we promote people. That's where you connect the dots between the values—which can't be just words on the wall—and the different actions we take on a day-to-day basis. I realized being a CEO that sometime there's a lot more comments in the company about the type of questions I ask than about the answers that we made to those questions. There's a lot of reasons for that in a company of our size, of course. You spent five years on the board before becoming CEO. Let's bring it home for a second and focus on Europe. There's a lot of chatter around the competitiveness of Europe on the global stage. Is scale required for high performance? We're a fixed cost business; it's very simple to explain that if you're number one in your market, you have more margin to invest and to do things than if you're number two, three, or four. You look at the math, and that's the type of discussion where I've been very open with all the stakeholders in Europe, being country leaders and regulators. We need regulation because our business is very critical, but Europe is missing the scale of Europe. I operate in eight countries in Europe, and I can't copy-paste a service. If I have a successful service that we launch in Spain, it's impossible to copy-paste across Europe simply because the list of things we would have to unleash to be able to do that is quite long. It's easy to say "simplify," but if we go into the details, there's always a good reason why. That's not the world we live in these days, so that's also the type of thing we're driving in the company.
